Summary 0:
Cross-border distribution of collective investment undertakings: EU rules on marketing and investor protection
Cross-Border distribution of collectively invested undertakings; EU rules for marketing and investors’ rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1156 on facilitating cross-border marketing and access of alternative investment funds (AIFs) and collective investment in transferable securities (UCITS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that marketing communications addressed to investors in collective investment funds* and UCITS* are clearly identifiable as such, and describe the risks and rewards of purchasing units or shares of an AIF or UCITS in an equally prominent manner.
It sets out the conditions for the marketing of marketing communications of AIFs and UCITs.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to marketing communications:
to investors who are:
investors in a collective investment undertaking (collective investment undertaking) or a UCITS;
to the general public, i.e. investors who have an interest in the AIF, UCITS or any other type of investment undertaking; and
to any person who is a member of a management company.
EU countries must ensure that:
marketing communications must be identifiable as AIF marketing communications;
they must contain information on the risks, rewards and other aspects of the marketing process;
the AIFM, the EuVECA manager, EuSEF manager or the UCITS management company has the right to terminate the marketing arrangements made for marketing.
a marketing communication;
all marketing communications must specify where, how and in which language investors can obtain summarised information on investor rights and they must clearly state that the management company can terminate marketing arrangements.
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) must create a central database containing the national database containing information on marketing communications and the information on national laws, regulations and administrative provisions applicable to these communications.
In addition, the European Supervisory Authority must publish such texts on their websites in, as a minimum, a language customary in the sphere of international finance, including their non-official summaries which would allow investors to get a broad overview of those laws and regulations and the administrative provisions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY ENTER INTO FORCE?
    даранскака барка  бъракова гарна  
It entered into force on 1 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
Alternative investment funds — EU rules and regulations (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Alternative investment funds: funds that invest in assets outside the EU, such as European venture capital funds, European social entrepreneurship funds (EuSEFs) and European long-term investment funds.
* UCITS: undertakings that are externally or internally managed, depending on its legal form.
REFERENCES

Summary 1:
EU market surveillance and compliance of products — Regulation (EU) 2019/1020 (Market Surveillance and Compliance of Products)
European Union market surveillance — Regulation(EU) 2020/852
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU)* 2019/1027 on market surveillance of products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that products are compliant with EU harmonisation legislation and therefore fulfil requirements providing a high level of protection of public interests, such as health and safety in general, consumer protection, protection of the environment, public security and protection of any other public interest protected by that legislation.
It establishes a framework for market surveillance* of products and controls on those products entering the EU market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products that are subject to EU legislation listed in Regulation (EC) No 765/2008 and Regulations (EUDAMED) relating to:
food, human and veterinary products,
animal feed,
products of plants and animals,
medical devices (e.g. implantable medical devices) and
live animals.
EU countries must:
ensure that the products covered by the regulation are placed on the market and that they comply with EU legislation;
take the necessary measures to prevent, detect and remove non-compliant products from the market;
cooperate with each other and with the European Commission;
exchange information on non-compliance with the other than those listed in the regulation.
The European Rapid Information Exchange System (RAPEX) allows EU countries to exchange information on products placed on their markets.
Cooperation between EU countries’ market surveillance authorities and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation and for the coordination of the EU’s market surveillance system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 25 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
EU legislation covers a large share of manufactured products. It is essential to guarantee the free movement of products within the EU and to create the conditions in which fair competition in the market for goods can thrive.
For more information, see:
‘Market surveillance and market surveillance’ on the Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal

Commission Implementing Regulation (EEC) 2021/853 of 20 June 2021 laying down rules on the European database on medical devices and amending Directive 2004/42/EC, Regulations (EC), (EC).

Summary 2:
EU rules on the exercise of copyright and related rights applicable to certain online transmissions of broadcasting organisations and retransmissions of television and radio programmes (Directive (EU) 2019/789 — online retransmission and online services of broadcasting companies
EU rules governing the exercise and distribution of copyright, related rights and remuneration of online retrains of broadcasting entities
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It lays down rules for the licensing of the rights of copyright (i.e. the protection of works and other protected subject matter) in the online transmission of television or radio programmes by broadcasting organisations.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
online transmissions of broadcasts of the broadcasting organisations of television programmes originating in other EU countries;
online services ancillary to these broadcasts, such as simulcasting and catch-up services.
It does not apply to the transmission of radio and television programmes.
Rights of the broadcasters
EU countries must ensure that:
the broadcasting organisations have the required rights to works and related subject matter for all the relevant territories of the EU;
the rights of the retransmitter are clear and that the retrighters are paid for their services;
there is a clear procedure for the authorisation and payment of appropriate remunerations.
The rights of broadcasters
The broadcasting organisations must:
clear and pay the necessary authorisations and payments for their online services, including:
replays of their own broadcasts;
recasts of other broadcasting organisations’s programmes;
transmissions of other broadcasters’ programmes that originate in other than their own;
rights of authors, producers and other rightholders.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE APPLY?
From 17 May 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 17 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to ensure the proper functioning of the internal market. These include:
Directive 2013/13/EEC — rules on online broadcasting of radio, television and internet programmes, including rules on:
exercise of copyright;
remuneration;
rules on the distribution of retransmitted works;
authorisations and payment for retransmitting works.
For more information, see:
Online broadcasting (European Commission).

Summary 3:
The.eu top-level domain (TLD) — implementation and functioning of the.eu TLD and amending and repealing Regulation (EC) No 733/2002 and Regulation (EU) No 874/2004 (OJ L 91, 29.3.2019, pp. 25-28)
The.EU top- level domain (tLD) is one of the largest country code TLDs (ccTLDs) in the world and is used by the European Union institutions, agencies and bodies, including for European projects and initiatives. The existence of such a domain name is important to strengthen the Union's identity online.
ACT
Regulation (EEC) 2019/517 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 19 March 2019 on the implementation and operation of the EU’s Top-Level Domain Name (EU-TLD).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes a registry for the.EU TLD.
It allows EU citizens to register a.eu domain name, regardless of their place of residence, from 19 October 2019.
KEY POINTS
The registry:
designates a registrar to manage the registry;
is responsible for managing the registry.
The registrar must:
ensure that domain names are allocated to eligible parties, subject to availability;
provide a simple administrative procedure for small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
take all necessary measures to combat the speculative and abusive registrations of domain names, including cybersquatting;
apply the relevant national law, without prejudice to rights and obligations recognised by the EU countries or by the Union arising from international instruments.
Applicants for the registry must meet the following criteria:
be a national of an EU Member State;
be an EU citizen;
have the right to contract with the registry and the registrars.
Eligible parties must be:
registered in the Member State in which they are resident;
established in the European Economic Area (EEA) or the European Free Trade Association (EFTA);
natural persons;
operating in the territory of a Member State or the EFTA.
Registry selection procedure:
the registry designates a registry. The registry must, on the basis of an open, transparent and non-discriminatory selection procedure, designate a Registry for.eu. The Registry may, at any time, withdraw from the registry if it considers that the registry no longer meets the eligibility criteria.
In the event of a Registry withdrawal, the Commission may, on its own initiative, delegate the decision to the European Commission. The Commission may adopt delegated acts in accordance with the acts delegated by the Member States.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU-tLD was established in 2002 and has been in operation since 2009.
For more information, see:
‘Top-level Domain Name’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 4:
The Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) — EU action against climate change
The European Union (EU) Action Plan 2021-2027 — Connecting European Facility
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, 2021/1153 establishing the ‘Connecting Europe Fund’
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s ‘CEF’, which aims to accelerate investment in the field of trans-European networks and to support environmentally and socially sustainable projects, including, where appropriate, climate change mitigation and adaptation actions.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the CEF's overall financial envelope, which comprises 60 % of its overall budget, based on the following coefficients:
100 % for expenditure relating to railway infrastructure, charging infrastructure, alternative and sustainable fuels, clean urban transport, electricity transmission, electricity storage, smart grids, CO2 transport and renewable energy;
40 % for inland waterways and multimodal transport, as well as gas infrastructure, provided that it enables the use of renewable hydrogen or bio-methane to be increased.
The CEF also contributes to the achievement of an overall EU budget target of at least 30 % of EU budget expenditure supporting climate objectives.
EU countries must ensure that projects identified for CEF funding are subject to a high level of transparency and ensure public consultation. The CEF is managed by the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Economic and Social Committee (ESCESC) and the Committee of the Regions.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY APPLY?
Decision (EU), Euratom-EU) 2021/1053 of 7 July 2021 establishing the Connecting EU Facility and repealing Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1316/2013 and (EC) No 283/2014.
BACKGROUND
In order to achieve smart, sustainable and inclusive growth, to stimulate job creation and to respect long-term decarbonisation commitments, the EU needs up-to-date, multimodally, high-performance infrastructure in its transport, energy and digital sectors to help connect and integrate the EU and all its islands and regions, including its remote, outermost, peripheral, mountainous and sparsely populated ones.
For more information, see:
‘Trans-European Networks’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Trans-European Network: a network of high-quality, high performance transport and energy infrastructure that links the EU countries and its islands, regions and islands.
* Inland waterways: the waterway network that connects the EU to the rest of the EU.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Council Decision 2021/1111/EU
7 July 2021
-
OJ L 249 of 14.7.2021

Summary 5:
Dissemination of terrorist content online: liability of hosting service providers (Regulation (EU) 2021/784)
Distribution of terrorist material online
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2000/31/EC on combating terrorist content
Regulation 2021/783 on addressing terrorist content on the internet
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVES?
Directives 2000/30/EC and 2001/36/EC aim to ensure the smooth functioning of the EU’s digital single market in an open and democratic society by addressing the misuse of hosting services for terrorist purposes and contributing to public security across the EU. The regulation aims to ensure legal certainty for online service providers and users’ trust in the online environment, as well as to strengthen safeguards to the freedom of expression, including the freedom to receive and impart information and ideas, and the freedom and pluralism of the media.
Commission Recommendation 2018/334 builds on voluntary efforts, which were reinforced by Commission Recommendation 2017/334, to combat terrorist content and to facilitate and direct terrorist activity online.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that hosting services are not liable for terrorist acts committed on their behalf;
ensuring that the hosting service provider is held liable for any offence committed on its behalf by a third party.
Hosting service providers must ensure that:
their services are used only for legitimate purposes;
they are not used for the purpose of facilitating or directing terrorist activities;
the content they provide is not used to commit terrorist acts.
The regulation does not apply to:
the use of their services to commit acts of terrorism;
any other offence committed by a non-EU country against the EU;
illegal activities committed by an EU country against its territory.
In the event of a conflict of interest between the hosting provider and the EU country in which the service is provided, the service provider must be liable for the loss of revenue resulting from the failure of the service to comply with the regulation.
Definitions of liability:
liability: the liability of an online service provider for any infringement of the law of another EU country.
Exemption from liability: the exemption from liability of a service provider in relation to a criminal offence committed in the territory of the country where the service was provided.
Liability of the host service provider: the provider is liable for:
any infringement committed in its territory by a user of its services,
any offence committed against the public policy of a country where it is established that the user is a member of a terrorist organisation;
an offence committed under the jurisdiction of a court of law of a EU country, which is not a party to the criminal offence.
Guidance: the regulation lays down rules on how the regulation should be interpreted and applied.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVE AND THE REGULATION APPLY?
The directive and the regulation have applied since 1 May 2021 and had to become law in the EU countries by 1 May 2022.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to address terrorist content. These include:
measures to combat the proliferation of terrorist materials on the Internet;
measures against the spread of illegal and harmful content online;
guidance on how to identify terrorist material and how to respond to it.
For more information, see:
‘Countering terrorist material’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
    баранска на гракова блаксан гарка  
Commission Directive 2000/29/EC of 29 April 2000 on combating terrorism and on the prevention of terrorist acts online (OJ L 172, 17.5.2000, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Directive 2000 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2021 and (EU), 2021/785 have applied from 1 May 2020.

Summary 6:
Horizon Europe - the Framework Programme for Research and Innovation (HFP) 2021-2027 (OJ L 170, 12.5.2021, pp. 1-8)
HorizonEurope - the framework Programme for research and innovation (HPP) 2021 - 2027 (OPEN)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 establishing Horizon Europe – the framework programme for research & innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes Horizon Europe, the European Union’s (EU's) framework programme, which aims to support research and innovative (R&I) activities to deliver on the EU's strategic priorities and commitments, which ultimately aim to promote peace, the Union's values and the well-being of its peoples.
It lays down the rules for participation and dissemination of Horizon Europe projects.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers Horizon Europe for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. It repeals Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1290/2013.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
support the creation, better diffusion and transfer of high-quality and excellent knowledge in the EU;
increase public and private investment in R&I;
promote the European research area (ERA) in which researchers, scientific knowledge and technology circulate freely and encouraging it to become more competitive, including in its industry;
enhance the impact of R&Is in developing, supporting and implementing EU policies;
support and strengthen the uptake and deployment of innovative and sustainable solutions in society, addressing global challenges, including climate change and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs), create jobs, boost economic growth, promote industrial competitiveness and boost the attractiveness of the EU in the field of R &I.
Participation and dissemination
The programme is open to research, innovation and technology (R & T) projects from all EU countries.
The rules for participating in Horizon Europe are laid down in the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
participation in the programme is based on the principles of excellence, openness and transparency;
projects are open to the public and to the private sector;
the programme is transparent and non-discriminatory;
participants are selected on the basis of the quality of their research and technological potential.
Beneficiaries
The European Commission is responsible for selecting Horizon Europe beneficiaries. They are selected by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC).
The beneficiaries are selected according to the following criteria:
the quality of the research and technology they propose;
their impact on the European economy and society; and
the impact of their projects on EU policy objectives.
They are selected in accordance with the criteria set out in Regulation (EC) No 596/2013 (see summary).
They must be funded by the EU budget and must be eligible for EU grants.
Financing
The EU budget for Horizon Europe is €1.2 billion for the duration of the multiannual financial framework (MFF) 2021–2027. The programme is funded by EU countries’ contributions to the European Research Fund (ERF), which is the main source of funding for the programme.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
    Еганска барка  
It has applied since 1 February 2021 and had to become law in the Member States by 31 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  Банксабана 
For more information, see:
‘Open Horizon Europe’ on the Horizon Europe website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  ‘Horizon European Framework Programme’ — Title I — Rules for participation in the Horizon European Programme 2021-2020
  ARTICLE 1 — Participation and dissemination — Article 2 — Rules on transparency and openness.
Successive amendments to Article 1 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 7:
EU Space Programme and European Union Agency for the Space Programme (ESA)
EU Space Programmes and ESA
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/696 establishing the EU Space Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s space programme and ESA, the European Union agency for the space programme.
It aims to encourage scientific and technological progress in the space sector and to support the competitiveness and innovation capacity of space sector industries within the EU, in particular small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), start-ups and innovative businesses.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) Member States. It does not apply to:
the European Geostationary Navigation Overlay Service (EGNOS) and then Galileo and Copernicus;
European Space Agency (ESA);
EU countries’ access to the European launch facilities;
the EU Agency for Space Technology (ESA).
Objectives
The purpose of the regulation is to ensure the continuity of EU space initiatives and programmes and the services they provide. It also aims to promote the use and uptake of the data, information and services provided, as well as the development of downstream applications based on those data and services.
To achieve these objectives, ESA is responsible for:
ensuring that the EU benefits from an autonomous access to and efficient use of launch facilities and launch services;
providing the EU-wide public access to launch facilities, including the use of the European space infrastructure;
promoting the development and use of space data and information systems;
supporting the dissemination of information campaigns regarding the benefits of the programme;
developing and promoting space applications;
exercising synergies between the transport, space and digital sectors in order to foster the broader use of new technologies, such as e-call, digital tachograph, traffic supervision and management, autonomous driving and unmanned vehicles and drones, and to respond to the need of secure and seamless connectivity, robust positioning, intermodality and interoperability.
The ESA is governed by Article 189(2) of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) which allows the EU to be a leading international player with extensive freedom of action in the field of space.
Evaluation of ESA
ESA has been developing its own space initiatives since the end of the 1990s, namely the European geostatary navigation overlay service, then the European satellite navigation system, Galileo, and the EU space programme, which respond to both the needs of EU citizens and the requirements of public policies. It is therefore essential to ensure that ESA is able to rapidly adapt to the technological developments in the sector and that it remains competitive in the market.
EU Member States must ensure that:
ESA is autonomous in terms of access to its own launch facilities (e.g. the European Space Agency);
ESA operates in accordance with the rules laid down in Article 189 of the TEU;
ESA and the Member States have the right to request the European Commission and the entities responsible for the programme to establish a European launch facility for the purpose of providing the EU with access to space launch facilities.
In addition, ESA and ESA are responsible for providing the European public with information on the programme, including:
information on the EU programme and the ESA;
information campaigns on the benefits and future prospects of the ESA and the Union Space Programme;
data and information services provided by ESA, ESA, EU countries, the Commission and ESA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 12 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
  * KEY TERMS
* European Space Programme: a European Union initiative and programme designed to promote scientific and technical progress and to encourage innovation in the area of space technology.
* ESA: the European Agency for space technology and services (ESA), which is an autonomous body established under Article 189 (2) TFEU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ебаранска нарка   банка, годарна ганна  граксан глага канта брашка and гълакабка: the Union space programme (OJ L 170, 12.5.2021, pp. 69-78)
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
Digital Europe Programme: 2021-2027 — rules on grants, prizes, procurement, indirect management, financial instruments, budgetary guarantees, financial assistance and reimbursement of external experts.
Digital Europe programme: 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/694 establishing the Digital Europe Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the financial envelope for the digital Europe programme (the ‘Programme’) for the period 2021–2027, which is to constitute the prime reference amount, within the meaning of point 18 of the Interinstitutional Agreement of 16 December 2020 between the European Parliament, the Council of the European Union and the European Commission on budgetary discipline, on cooperation in budgetary matters and on sound financial management, as well as on new own resources.
It repeals Decision 2015/2240.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027. It covers:
grants, prizes and indirect management;
budget guarantees;
financial assistance;
reimbursals;
reciprocity.
The regulation applies to:
projects funded under the programme;
projects financed under the EU’s multiannual financial framework (MFF);
the European Parliament and the Council during the annual budgetary procedure;
programme activities and underlying costs from the beginning of the 2021 financial year to the end of the 2027 financial year.
EU countries’ obligations
The European Commission must:
ensure that the European Anti-fraud office (OLAFOAF) carries out on-the-spot checks and inspections to establish whether there has been fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the EU;
cooperate fully with OLAF to investigate and prosecute any offences affecting the EU financial interests;
identify any EU-related offences;
impose administrative penalties on any person or entity involved in any of the above-mentioned offences.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 29 April 2021.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘Digital Europe’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Ибаранска банкабрака   ханна грашка (Digital Europe Regulation)
  (OJ L 166, 11.5.2021, pp. 1–8)

Summary 9:
EU programme for the internal market, competitiveness of enterprises, the area of plants, animals, food and feed, and European statistics (Single Market Programme)
EU programme to the extent that it is compatible with the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/690 establishing a programme for EU action in the fields of competitiveness of businesses, especially SMEs, consumer protection, customers and end-users in financial services, policymaking in the financial services and in the plant, animal, food & feed area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a programme to:
improve the competitiveness of EU businesses, including small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
promote fair competition in the EU’s internal market;
facilitate the production of European statistics.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following areas:
competitiveness of businesses;
consumer protection;
market surveillance;
European statistics;
public procurement.
It does not apply to the areas covered by Regulation (EC) No 1287/2013, Regulation (EU), No 254/2014 and Regulation 652/2014.
Financing
The programme is financed by the EU budget. It is divided into 2 budget lines:
the internal market budget line, which aims to streamline and exploit synergies between various actions and activities to achieve a more efficient, transparent, sustainable and sustainable internal market.
The European statistics budget line covers the European production and dissemination of statistics. The European statistics programme is funded by the European Parliament and the Council of the EU. It covers the production, dissemination and transmission of statistics for the EU and the European Economic Area (EEA). The European statistical budget line also covers the transmission of European statistical data for the EEA.
Programme objectives
The objectives of the programme are to: ensure that EU businesses and consumers have access to high-quality products and services at lower prices;promote the free movement of goods and services within the EU;improve the quality of EU statistical data;ensure that European statistics are produced in a way that is consistent with EU law.
EU actions
The EU actions are organised in the following categories:
competitive activities: actions aiming to improve businesses’ competitiveness, standardisation, mutual recognition, conformity assessment and market surveillance. They concern, in particular, competitiveness, standards, procedures and procedures for the certification of products, services, processes and business models;
customer protection: actions aimed at protecting consumers’ rights and interests, and at ensuring that the EU rules and regulations are applied correctly.
European statistical programmes: activities aimed at improving the quality and reliability of EU statistics. These include:
promoting European statistics and European production;
improving the quality, reliability and accuracy of European production data;
ensuring that the European statistical system is up-to-date and reliable;
providing information on the European statistics system to the public and to the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 3 May 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 May 2023.
BACKGROUND
The Single Market Programme (SMP) is one of the main instruments of the Lisbon Treaty. Since its inception, it has proved a major contributor to growth, competitiveness and employment. It continues to be an engine for building a more integrated market and a stronger, more balanced and fairer economy.
For more information, see:
‘The EU programme’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егаранска гракса  глака   бакца  (OJ L 153, 3.5.2021, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) 2021 /690 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
last update 28.02.2022



Summary 10:
Control of the acquisition and possession of weapons (Directive 2021/555 on control of the movement of firearms and their essential components) (codification of Directive 91/477/EEC)
Control on the movement and acquisition of firearms (directive (EU) 2021/554)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/82/EU on the regulation of the EU’s internal market for firearms and essential components
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the traceability of firearms.
It codifies and repeals the previous Directives 91(477) and (EEC).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all firearms, essential components and ammunition that are lawfully acquired and possessed in the EU. It does not apply to the carrying of weapons, hunting or target shooting.
EU countries must ensure that EU countries keep a computerised data-filing system, either centralised or decentralised, which guarantees to authorised authorities access to the necessary information regarding each firearm and its essential components. This system must contain all the data relating to its manufacture, manufacture and manufacture year.
The data-filings systems must contain the name of the manufacturer, the manufacturer and the country of manufacture of the firearm, as well as the name and serial number of the receiver and the manufacturer of the parts of the weapon.
Manufacturers must indicate on their firearms the name, manufacturer and manufacturer and receiver of the firearms and the year of manufacture in order to make it easier to trace their manufacture.
manufacturers must also indicate on the firearm the type of the frame, the calibre and the number of calibre of the gun.
National authorities must be able to access the computerised systems in which the firearm is recorded.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 6 April 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 6 April 2023.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Traceability’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Traceability: the ability to trace a firearm, its essential component and its manufacturer or manufacturer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Игранаргансканка барка   бълака, болаки  гарна года,  харакка:

Summary 11:
European Maritime Single Window environment (EMSWe) — rules for the reporting of maritime transport information (European Union rules on the transmission of information between EU countries and the EU countries’ authorities and port service providers)
European Maritime single window environment (EMSWE) rules for reporting of marine transport information
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1239 establishing a European Maritime Single-Window environment and repealing Directive 2010/65/EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European maritime single-window (EMSWE) environment.
It aims to simplify and harmonise the reporting procedures for maritime transport operators* and to improve the functioning of the EU’s internal market and to meet the needs of citizens and businesses.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must accept the fulfilment of the reporting obligations of ships arriving in and departing from EU ports in electronic format and to ensure their transmission via a single window in order to facilitate and expedite maritime transport.
The regulation sets out rules for:
the use in every maritime National Single Window of common interface software for system-to-system exchanges of information, developed at EU level;
the harmonisation of the front-end interfaces of those national single-windows on the side of the declarants;
common interfaces of the national national single windows for data transmission between the national authorities and the providers of port services.
National authorities must:
ensure that the same data sets can be reported to each maritime national single window;
provide the same information for other transport modes;
make it available to other EU countries to the same way;
establish a common interface for the use of the maritime single window for the transmission and storage of similar information;
apply the rules laid down in the regulation to all EU countries.
In addition, EU countries must also:
make available to the European Commission and the European Maritime Information System Agency (EMSSA) the software for the electronic transmission of maritime data;
develop and maintain the interface module for each national single Window;
implement the rules for data storage and processing of information in accordance with the requirements laid down by the regulation.
EU states may not impose disproportionate administrative burdens on shipping companies, as they do not impose a disproportionate administrative burden on them.
Implementation of the regulation
The EU countries are responsible for integrating and managing the EMSWE interface module and for updating the software regularly and in a timely manner when new versions are provided by the Commission. The Commission must develop this module and provide updates when needed, since the development of digital technologies is moving fast and any technological solution could rapidly become outdated in the light of new developments.
Exemption from the obligation to develop and operate a manual interface for maritime data transmission:
national authorities and service providers are exempted from this obligation;
declarants do not have to develop, operate or operate a graphical user interface for electronic data transmission;
national service providers, such as Port Community Systems, are not required to provide the user with a graphical interface for their electronic data transmissions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 25 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Maritime transport: the transport of goods, passengers and cargo in and out of the European Union (EU).
* EU countries: EU countries that have ports in the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			 	  	
                    fixme   б  х   The regulation establishes rules for EU countries in relation to:
reporting obligations of shipping companies;
data collection and data distribution to the relevant authorities and to the service providers;
transmission of information to the competent authorities of EU countries;
exemption from certain reporting obligations.

Summary 12:
Interoperable electronic road toll systems
Interoperatable electronic tolls
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/520 on the interoperability of electronic road-toll systems and facilitating cross-border exchange of information on the failure to pay road fees in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that electronic toll systems (e.g. tolls) are interoperable and to reduce the cost of, and the burdens linked to, the payment of tolls across the EU.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2004/52/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
European electronic toll service (EETS) providers;
vehicle-based toll systems;
time-based systems; and
automatic toll recognition (ANPR) systems.
EETS providers must:
provide the European Electronic Toll Service (EETS) with the required communication technologies;
make use of the on-board equipment (OBE) and of other hardware and software systems already present in the vehicle, such as satellite navigation systems or handheld devices.
Annex I to the directive sets out the conditions for the use of satellite positioning or mobile communication technologies in EETS systems. These technologies must be compatible with each other.
EU Member States must ensure that:
the EETS providers provide the EETS service with the necessary communication technologies, including:
satellite positioning and mobile communications;
the ability to link to the OBEAs well as the ability to provide users with the information necessary for the operation of the system.
Member States must also:
ensure that EETS services are compatible with the ANPR systems and with the manual toll recognition systems used by the national authorities;
allow the EETs providers to provide the European Commission with information on: the number of vehicles covered by an OBE;
obligations to pay tolls;
information on the number and type of vehicle covered by the system;
requests for the information to be made available to the Commission;
authorisations to use the information provided by EETS providers.
Implementation of the directive
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of this directive. The report must include:
a description of the technologies and interfaces used;
a summary of the technical specifications for each of the interfaces;
an overview of the various aspects of interoperability.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It applies from 29 March 2019. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 29 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * Electronic road toll system: an electronic system for the collection of road tolls.
* Time-based system: a system where the road fee due is linked to the time spent by the vehicle on the tolled infrastructure, i.e. the distance covered by a vehicle or to the vehicle passing a specific point (for example, cordon pricing).
* Automatic toll recognition system: the technology used for the recognition of vehicle registration plates.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    б   *   The directive repeals Directives 2004/51/EC, 2004/53/EC and 2004/54/EC with effect from 29 April 2019.

Summary 13:
European network of immigration liaison officers
European network for the management of migration
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1240 on the creation of a European network of Immigration liaison officers (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Network of Immigration Liaison Officers (ENLIO) to coordinate and optimise the coordination and coordination of the EU’s external border management systems with non-EU countries’ authorities.
It aims to ensure the effective implementation of all aspects of EU policies on immigration, including departures and returns.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ENLIO consists of:
European Migration Liaison Officer (EMLO) officers deployed by the European Commission to the EU countries of origin and transit of migrants and applicants for international protection;
EU countries deploying EMLO officers to the countries of transit of asylum seekers, as well as to non-euro area countries.
The purpose of the EMLO network is to:
facilitate coordination and optimisation of EU-wide cooperation between EU countries on migration-related issues;
contribute to the establishment of a comprehensive situational picture of migration flows in the EU;
provide information on the composition of migratory flows;
prevent and counter illegal immigration, smuggling and trafficking in human beings;
support the reintegration of migrants;
cooperate with EU countries in order to respond effectively to the migration crisis;
ensure the integration of migrants into the EU society;
promote the integration and integration of refugees and asylum seekers.
Eligences
The EMLO members are appointed by the Commission and EU countries. They are appointed for a maximum of 3 years. They must be appointed by each EU country and must be based on a proposal from the Commission.
They must:
have the capacity to gather and share information on migrants’ age, gender profile, family profile and family profile;
be able to provide information on applicants’ access to international protection and, where possible and relevant, on re-integration;
participate in the coordination of EU border management measures;
act as liaison officers in the field of border management;
assist the Commission in the implementation of EU migration policies;
take part in the training of national border management authorities;
operate in cooperation with the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (BERCA) and the European Migration Agency (EMAA);
be available to the public and to the media.
Rights and obligations of EMLOs
EMLOs must respect the fundamental rights and fundamental freedoms of all migrants, regardless of their migratory status, in full compliance with international law.
In addition, they must respect fundamental rights in particular in cases involving vulnerable people, in accordance with relevant international and EU law, including Articles 2 and 6 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) and Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union.
EU Member States must take the necessary measures to prevent and counter the smuggling of migrants, trafficking in migrants and human beings and to respond to the threat of human trafficking.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 14:
Strengthening the security of identity cards and residence documents for EU citizens exercising their right of free movement (Regulation (EU) 2019/1157) (SUMMARY)
Stepping up the EU’s fight against identity card and residence document fraud (Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 and (EU), 2019/1052)
SUMMONING
Regulation(EU) 2018 /852 amends Regulation (EU). It aims to improve the security and reliability of national identity cards* and residence permits* issued to EU citizens and their family members.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to:
ensure that EU citizens’ identity cards are secure;
improve the security levels of national residence permits issued by EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to issue and renew identity cards or passports to their nationals in accordance with national laws.
They may require EU citizens to register with the relevant authorities.
EU citizens who are not nationals of an EU country have the right to stay in another EU country for up to 5 years. However, EU countries may refuse, terminate or withdraw any right conferred by that directive in the case of abuse of rights or fraud.
The regulation applies to the following:
identity cards issued for the purpose of travel and residence purposes;
passports issued for travel purposes.
Residence permits issued for permanent residence.
It does not apply to the residence of EU citizens who have left the EU and who are seeking to re-enter the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
  It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TEU) establishes an area of freedom, security and justice. Article 45 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU (the Charter) also provides for freedom of movement and residence. This regulation aims to facilitate the free movement of persons while ensuring the safety and security of the peoples of Europe.
For more information, see:
‘More secure travel and identity documents’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 15:
Interoperability of EU information systems in the field of borders and visa
Interoperality of EU data systems in this field
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 on establishing a framework for interoperability between EU Information Systems in the area of borders, visa and security
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of border controls at the external borders of the European Union (EU).
It also aims to ensure that border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities have the necessary information at their disposal.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
EU information systems for security, border and migration management, including:
Visa Information System (VISES);
Information System for Authorisation of Visas;
Entry into the EU System for Migration and Asylum;
EU Information System for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Rights.
It does not apply to the following:
Information systems for the protection of human rights and fundamental rights;
information systems for asylum, migration and protection of public policy;
data systems for protection of the security of the EU.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
VISES is interoperable;
visas are issued in a timely and secure manner;
the information system for authorisation of visas is secure;
authorisations are processed in a secure and efficient manner.
National authorities must ensure that: the national authorities have access to the information they need to carry out border controls and to the systems they use to manage migration and asylum;visas and exit visas are issued and processed in an efficient, secure and timely manner;visa applications are processed and processed within the EU system for immigration and asylum.
The national authorities must also:
notify the European Commission and the European Parliament of any changes to their national information systems;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to implement the regulation;
notifies the European Court of Auditors of any amendments to the regulation.
In addition, national authorities are required to ensure:
visa information systems are interoperable with each other and with national databases;
visa information systems and national databases are secure and up-to-date;
national authorities are informed of any significant changes in their national data systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 22 May 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 22 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Border controls (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Interoperability: the ability of a single EU information system to be used by different national authorities.
* Visa: a visa issued by a country to allow its nationals to enter the EU without being required to have a valid visa.

Summary 16:
Promoting the use of renewable energy
Promoting energy from renewable sources
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/2001 on the promotion of renewable sources (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to promote renewable energy in the European Union (EU). It aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and to comply with the EU’s commitment under the 2015 Paris Agreement on Climate Change following the 21st Conference of the Parties to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate change (the ‘Paris Agreement’), including the EU 2030 energy and climate framework, including the binding target to cut emissions by at least 40 % below 1990 levels by 2030.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the electricity sector, the heating and cooling sector and the transport sector. It does not apply to:
the transport sector;
the energy efficiency sector; and
the renewable energy sector.
It applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
EU countries may set their own, more ambitious, national targets in order to deliver their planned renewable energy contributions to the 2030 EU 2030 target and exceed them.
In addition, EU countries are encouraged to encourage the development of renewable heating, cooling and energy efficiency technologies, as well as renewable transport fuels.
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 ?
From 21 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
It repeals and replaces Directive 2009/28/EC.
For more information, see:
‘Renewable energy’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Renewable energy: any energy source that is 100 % renewable or 100% energy-efficient.

Summary 17:
European Electronic Communications Code (EEC)
European Electronic Communication Code (ECC)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1972 establishing the European Electronic Communications code
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes the EU’s electronic communications code (ECCC).
It aims to ensure freedom to provide electronic communications networks and services, subject only to the conditions laid down in this directive and to any restrictions in accordance with Article 52(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and consistent rules regarding public policy, public security and public security.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EECCC applies to:
providers of electronic communication networks and of electronic communications services;
telecommunications service providers (e.g. internet service providers, mobile operators, internet hosting providers, etc.).
It does not apply to operators of fixed-line telephone and cable television services.
EU countries must ensure that:
the EECC applies to all operators of electronic telecommunications networks and to all providers of services, irrespective of their size;
all operators of mobile phone and cable TV services are subject to the same conditions;
any operator of a mobile phone or cable television service must comply with the same rules as the operators of a fixed-phone or cable TV service;
the same rules apply to all users of the same mobile and internet television service.
National authorities must:
ensure that all operators comply with all the rules laid down by the European Commission;
inform the national authorities of all the measures taken to ensure compliance with the directive;
as soon as possible with the requirements of the legislation;
take all the necessary measures to ensure that the directive is complied with;
apply the rules in line with the objectives of the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of the rules on radio spectrum policy and management;
provide the Commission with a report on the implementation of the directive every 5 years;
make available to the public all the information it needs to make an informed decision on the application and application of the regulation;
allow the Commission to review the directive at any time.
Regulation (EC) No 1211/2009 repeals Directive 2002/19/EC, 2002/20/EC and 2002/22/EC.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive has applied since 17 December 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 17 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European electronic communication code is part of a ‘Regulatory Fitness’ (REFIT) exercise, the scope of which includes four Directives, namely 2002/18/EC (see summary), 2002/21/EC(see summary).
For more information, see:
‘European electronic communications codes’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption of new heavy-duty vehicles
Monitoring & reporting of new light duty vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/956 — monitoring and reporting on CO2 emission emissions from new heavy duty vehicles (LHDs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a regulated procedure for the measurement of greenhouse gas (CO2) emissions* from and the fuel consumption* of new LHDs in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
light duty vehicles: lorries, buses and coaches;
commercial vehicles: trucks, buses, coaches, trailers and semi-trailers;
passenger vehicles: vans, minibuses and coaches.
It applies to all new vehicles registered in the EU from 1 January 2018 to 31 December 2023.
EU Member States must ensure that:
the vehicle manufacturer, importer and importer provide the European Commission with a list of the vehicles they have registered in their territory;
the EU Member State of registration provides the Commission with the list of vehicles it has registered in its territory.
The Commission must inform the Member States and the European Parliament and the Council of the results of the inspections carried out.
Member States must also ensure that the Commission publishes a report every 2 years on the monitoring of the CO2 and fuel emissions and consumption data they have received from the manufacturer and the importer. The report must be sent to the Commission within 3 months of the end of the year in which the vehicle was registered. The Commission must send the report to the Council and the results within 3 years.
Each Member State must also send the Commission a report on the number of vehicles that have been registered in that Member State.
Report
Each year, the Commission must submit a report with the information it has received on:
CO2 emissions from each heavy duty vehicle;
fuel consumption from each vehicle.
This report must include:
a description of the vehicle’s type and type of fuel consumption;
a breakdown of the total number of heavy duty trucks and buses registered in each Member State; and
the number of trucks registered in different Member States.
In addition, the report must contain:
details of the type of vehicle registered in a Member State and its type of emissions;
information on the type, type and year of registration of each vehicle, its type and its fuel consumption, as well as information on the vehicle type and category of emissions.
Reporting
The report must provide the Commission and Member States with the following information:
total number of registered vehicles in the Member State concerned;
number of vehicles registered and registered in other Member States;
percentage of new vehicles sold in the previous year;
total CO2 emitted by each vehicle in the last 3 years;
average annual fuel consumption for the previous 3 years and the year before the most recent 3 years of registration.
If the Commission considers that the information provided by the manufacturer or importer is insufficient, it must provide additional information to the national authorities and the public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The legislation applies from 31 December 2018. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
Heavy duty vehicles currently represent around a quarter of road transport emissions in the Union and, if no additional measures are taken, are expected to increase by 10 % between 2010 and 2030 and by 17 % between 2020 and 2050.
For more information, see:
Heavy-duty vehicle emissions (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* CO2: greenhouse gas emissions from light duty transport.
* Fuel consumption: the total amount of fuel consumed by a vehicle in a 24-hour period.

Summary 19:
Safeguarding the security of gas supply in the European Union (EU) (Regulation (EC) No 713/2009) and repealing Regulation (Euratom) No 994/2010 (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) 2017/1938 — measures to safeguard gas supply
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that all the necessary measures are taken to safeguard an uninterrupted supply of gas throughout the EU, in particular to protected customers, in the event of difficult climatic conditions or disruptions of the gas supply.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ national gas supply plans and emergency plans.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
the national emergency plans and plans are drawn up and approved by the European Commission;
the emergency plans are prepared and implemented by the national authorities;
gas supply disruptions are notified to the Commission, the European Parliament and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and the Committee of the Regions.
The emergency plans must include:
a description of the risks to the EU’s security of supply;
a breakdown of the supply chain;
measures to be taken in case of a gas supply disruption;
an assessment of the impact of a supply disruption on the EU economy and the social impact of the disruption on vulnerable groups of customers.
National authorities must also draw up a list of the EU countries that are covered by the emergency plans, which must be reviewed every 5 years.
in addition, EU countries must draw up an inventory of their gas infrastructure capacity and gas supply capacity.
Each EU country must also designate a national authority responsible for the preparation and implementation of the emergency plan and emergency plan. The national authority must also submit a report to the EEC, the ESSC and the EESC on the status of the national emergency plan, the national preventive action plans and the national contingency plans, as well as a report on the state of the internal gas market.
In addition, the Commission must also:
assess the risk of a natural gas shortage;
draw up a gas market contingency plan for each EU country;
make recommendations to EU countries on how to respond to a gas shortage.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 28 October 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 28 October 2018.
BACKGROUND
Energy security constitutes one of the objectives of the Energy Union Strategy, as set out in the communication of 25 February 2015 on a Framework Strategy for a Resilient Energy Union with a Forward-Looking Climate Change Policy, which also emphasised the ‘energy efficiency first’ principle and the need to fully implement existing EU energy legal acts.
For more information, see:
‘Energy security’ on European Commission's website.

Summary 20:
Energy labelling: EU rules for energy appliances and smart energy systems
Energy Labelling: European rules for electricity and gas appliances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1369 setting a framework for energy labelling and repealing Directive 2010/30/EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU rules on the labelling of energy-related products.
It aims to improve the efficiency of these products and to encourage innovation and investment in energy efficiency.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products placed on the EU market for the first time, including second-hand imported products, including products that are made available for a second or additional time. It does not apply to energy consumption of means of transport for persons or goods that are directly and indirectly regulated by other EU law and policies.
EU Member States must ensure that:
the energy consumption information on the energy label is accurate and comparable to that on the label of a comparable energy system;
the information on energy efficiency of the energy system is comparable to the information on comparable energy systems in other Member States.
The energy label must contain the following information:
a description of the product’s energy consumption and its energy efficiency;
an energy efficiency rating;
a list of energy efficiency values;
energy efficiency values.
Member States must:
ensure that the energy consumption values are comparable to those set out in the European Energy Information System (EISA);
make the information available to the public available on the European Commission's website;
European Energy Information Agency (EIA);
provide information to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions;
make sure that the information provided by the Member States is accurate, comparable and up-to-date.
National authorities must take the necessary measures to ensure that the legislation does not lead to divergent transposition by Member States and thus ensures a higher degree of harmonisation across the EU.
Exemptions from the scope of the regulation include:
products that are being sold for a limited period of time;
products which are not being sold to the general public;
electrical equipment that is not being used for the purpose of heating, cooling or air-conditioning;
motorised equipment that does not stay in the same location during operation.
Importers must ensure the following:
that the energy efficiency ratings and energy efficiency value of the products are comparable;
that they are available to consumers and that they are not misleading;
they provide the information that consumers need to make informed choices based on the information they have received on energy consumption.
In addition, Member States are required to:
take the necessary steps to ensure the quality and consistency of the information contained in the regulation;
establish a system of national authorities responsible for monitoring the compliance with the regulation and for the enforcement of the legislation;
apply the legislation in line with the principles of EU energy law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
Directive 2010/29/EU has applied since 1 July 2017 and had to become law in Member States by 1 July 2018.
Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2018/1169 and (EU), 2018/1269 have applied since 31 December 2018. The regulation repeals Directive 2010 (EU).
BACKGROUND
The EU is committed to building an Energy Union with a forward looking climate policy. Energy efficiency is a crucial element of the EU's 2030 Climate and Energy Policy Framework and is key to moderating energy demand. This regulation improves the legislative and enforcement framework for the energy labisation of products. It also contributes to the achievement of the Energy Union's 2020 and 2030 energy-efficiency targets, as well as to the Union's goals for the environment and climate change.
For more information, see:
‘Efficient and sustainable energy’ on the EIA's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy consumption: the total energy consumed by a given energy system over a given period.

Summary 21:
Protecting personal data: EU rules on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data and the free movement of such data (Regulation (EU) 2016/679)
Protecting the personal data of individuals with respect to their personal data (General Data Protection Regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 95/46/EC — general data protection regulation (GDPR)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect the rights and fundamental freedoms of persons whose personal data are used for commercial purposes.
It sets out the rules and procedures for the processing and transfer of their data in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that:
data are processed only in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
the processing of data is carried out in a way that respects the rights of the individual concerned;
there is a clear distinction between personal data* and non-personal data*;
data protection is based on the principle of proportionality.
The regulation applies to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality or residence. It does not apply to non-EU citizens who are resident in another EU country.
National authorities are required to cooperate and exchange personal data so as to be able to perform their duties or carry out tasks on behalf of an EU country’s national authority.
Natural persons have the right to:
an effective remedy and to a fair trial;
to be informed and to have access to the information they have been given;
an opportunity to participate in the decision-making process of the national authorities;
access to the data protection authorities of the EU country in which processed their data;
a right to be informed of any changes to their data.
Where a data processing activity is not in line with national law, EU countries must ensure that the competent authorities of EU countries:
have access to all the information that they have collected and processed;
have the opportunity to review the data they have received;
are informed of all the changes that have been made in their national law;
may decide to restrict the processing or transfer of a personal data to a third party that is not a national authority;
must inform the European Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) of the reasons for any such decision.
EU citizens have a right to have their data processed in a manner that is consistent with their fundamental rights and that is proportionate to their needs.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
    банка нарка  хакана бъранна  ганшка
  Ебалкага года 
It has applied since 4 May 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 4 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
The GDPR is one of the most comprehensive data protection legislation in the world. It aims to ensure the protection and security of all personal data held in the EU.
For more information, see:
EU data protection (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: personal data that is collected, processed and transferred by a company or individual.
* Non-personal: data that does not relate to the person concerned.

the percentage of households that are not occupied by a household.

Annexes to the report must be submitted to the Commission for each 5-year period from the end of the previous year.
the Commission must prepare a report on the quality and comparability of the indices.

Housing price: the price of a house divided by the number of occupied dwellings in the country.

Summary 23:
Protection against dumped imports from non-EU countries
Protection of imports from countries not members of the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1036 on protection against dumping imports from outside the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 1225/2009, which had been substantially amended several times.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s anti-dumping rules are applied in line with Article VI of the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
all non-market-economy imports* into the EU from countries other than those covered by the 1994 Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT).
It does not apply to imports originating in the EU and originating in non-European Economic Area (EEA) countries.
Importers must notify the European Commission of any imports they consider to be dumped.
The Commission must inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the reasons for initiating and pursuing an investigation and the results of the investigation, including the establishment and treatment of the facts, the imposition of provisional measures and the imposition and collection of antidumping duties, the duration and review of the measures, and the public disclosure of information relating to the investigation.
Procedures
If an investigation is launched, the Commission must:
inform the EU countries and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) of its decision to launch an investigation;
the EU countries of any measures to be taken.
If the Commission concludes that an investigation has been launched, it must inform all EU countries, the EFTA and the other EU countries concerned.
EU countries must inform each other of the decision to initiate an investigation. The Commission must also inform the other countries of the reason for initiating the investigation and of the results thereof.
Duties
The European Commission may impose duties on imports of goods from the non-EEA if it considers that they are likely to cause material injury to the EU industry. The duties may also be imposed on imports originating from the EU or originating in an EU country.
Expiry
If a country considers that an import is likely to result in material injury or injury to its industry, it may submit a request to the Commission for an investigation to determine whether it is dumping. If the Commission considers that the import is not dumping, it can impose duties and/or a suspension of imports.
Where an investigation concludes that the imports are dumping, the EU country must impose duties or suspend imports for a period of 6 months. The period of suspension may be extended for a further 6 months if the Commission determines that the duties or suspension are not justified by the facts of the case.
In the event of a suspension or a reduction in imports, EU countries may impose additional duties or a suspension on the import of goods originating in another EU country or originating from an EFTA country. They may also impose additional tariffs or suspension measures on the imports originating or originating within the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULAR REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
    Еганска банка  храка
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1023/2009 and (EC), No 1024/2009 have applied since 1 July 2009 and have applied from 1 July 2010 respectively.
Regulated (EU, Euram) No 836 has applied since 30 June 2016 and had to become law in the Member States by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Expiry: the period during which an imported product is considered to be at a loss and may be disregarded.
* Importers: the producers of the product and the importers of the goods involved.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ж  Банхкаба  бхакка (Regulation) (OJ L 176, 30.6.2016, pp. 21-24)

Summary 24:
Protection against subsidised imports from non-EU countries (subsidies and countervailing measures)
Protection of EU consumers from imports of agricultural products from outside the EU (Subsidies & Countervailing Measures)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1037 on protection against subsidising imports from countries not members of the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 597/2009, which had been substantially amended in 2009.
It aims to ensure that EU consumers benefit from the protection provided for in the Subsidies Agreement (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
subsidised imports* of agricultural and non-food products originating in the EU from countries other than the EU;
countervailable imports* originating in EU countries.
Who may lodge a countervailable complaint against these imports?
The European Commission may:
make a complaint on behalf of producers in a region of the EU, including the extent to which it should be supported by the EU industry;
make an application to the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) for a European Anti-Fraud Office (Annex 1A to the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
The Commission may also:
impose duties on imports originating in a country not covered by the agreement;
take measures to prevent or restrict imports originating from the EU of goods originating in that country;
from a country that has not been party to the agreement.
Applicants may also lodge a complaint against imports originating within the EU and originating in an EU country.
EU countries must:
ensure that EU citizens have the right to be informed of any measures taken by the Commission to prevent, restrict or restrict the import of goods from the country concerned;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to make a decision on the application of the regulation;
notify the European Parliament and the Council of the measures they intend to take.
The EU countries must inform the Commission of all the measures that they have taken in response to a complaint, including:
the amount of the subsidy;
the nature and duration of the duties imposed on the imports;
any other information that may be necessary to determine whether the imports have caused material injury or are threatening to cause injury.
If the Commission considers that the imports are likely to cause material injury, it may impose duties on them. The Commission must inform EU countries of the reasons for this decision.
Duties may be imposed under certain conditions:
in the case of EU countries, the duties must be imposed in accordance with the general principles of the WTO Agreement on Tariffs and Trade 1994;
in cases where a market benchmark does not exist or is unreliable, the benchmark must be determined by adjusting the terms and conditions prevailing in the countries concerned on the basis of actual factors available in those countries. If this is not practicable because, inter alia, such prices or costs exist or are unreliable, then the appropriate benchmark is determined by resorting to terms or conditions in other markets.
In cases where the EU country concerned determines that a subsidy does not constitute a material injury and that a benefit is not likely to be conferred on the recipient enterprise, the Commission may impose a duty. The duties may be:
suspended for a period of 5 years;
restrictions imposed on imports of certain goods;
suspension of imports of some goods from certain EU countries;
provisional imposition of duties on certain goods imported from certain countries; and
restriction of certain imports of goods imported in certain cases.
Where a duty is imposed on an import from a country which is not a party to an agreement on subvention, it must be notified to the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 30 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Subsidy: any form of income or price support provided by a government or a public body within the territory of a country to a recipient enterprise.
* Countervailable import: any type of subsidy that is counter-vailable under the WTO agreement on trade in goods and services.

  The regulation repeals and replaces:

Summary 25:
Recovery and Resilience Facility (R&RF)
Recovery & resilience facility (R & R)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/241 establishing the recovery and resilience facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the R&R Facility, a mechanism designed to help EU countries cope with economic, social and budgetary shocks.
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to recover from the COVID-19 outbreak in early 2020.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to coordinate their economic policies with a view to contributing to the achievement of the objectives of the EU and in the context of the broad guidelines that the Council of the European Union (TFEU) formulates. The coordination of the economic policies of EU countries is therefore a matter of common concern.
The R & R Facility is designed to support the EU’s efforts to cope with the effects of COVID. It aims to:
recooperate with the European Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) to identify priority investment projects to be supported by national and/or EU funding;
contribute to the European Green Deal, the European Pillar of Social Rights and the United Nations Sustainable Development Goals;
support the implementation of the Green Deal and to achieve the European Sustainable Growth Strategy 2020;
promote the just transition plans and the Youth Guarantee implementation plans.
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to the Member States’ national structural adjustment programmes (SAPAs) or the European Structural and Investment Funds (SIFAs).
It applies to the following sectors:
the economy;
the social sector;
health and social protection;
education and training;
emergency services;
transport;
energy and climate change;
environmental protection.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The COVID outbreak in 2020 changed the economic, financial and budgetary outlook in the EU, in particular the economic and social outlook, calling for an urgent and coordinated response both at EU and national level. This regulation establishes the Recovery and Resiliency Facility.
For more information, see:
‘The Recovery & ResiliENCY Facility’ on the Commission's website.
