Summary 26:
Technical support instrument for EU countries’ economic policies
Technical support for EU governments’ economies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/240 establishing a technical support instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a technical instrument to support EU countries in their economic policies in order to achieve the objectives of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
It aims to continue and enhance technical support under the Structural Reform Support Programme for the period 2017-2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland, the United Kingdom (1) and the European Economic Area (EEA), which are excluded from the scope of the regulation.
EU countries are required to coordinate their economic policy in such a way as to attain the objectives on economic, social and territorial cohesion set out in Article 174 of the TFEU. The regulation provides technical support for the implementation of EU economic policies.
It covers a wide range of policy areas, including administrative and structural reforms, as well as the creation of high-quality jobs.
National strategies for economic reform
Each year, EU countries submit their national strategies to the European Commission, which then identifies the priorities for the following year’s European Semester for economic reforms. The Commission then presents these to the national parliaments and monitors implementation of those priorities.
The Commission then adopts the national strategies in line with the priorities. The national strategies must be coherent and coherent. They must also serve as a reference framework for the EU programme.
 which identifies the European Semesters and the specific challenges identified in the context of the programme and the priority areas to be addressed.
In the light of the COVID-19 outbreak in early 2020, new priorities have emerged, linked with the crisis, specifically focussing on recovery and resilience. These include:
growth-enhancing, sustainable, smart and socially responsible reforms and investments;
sound fiscal policies;
creation of high quality jobs to respond to the new challenges, address structural economic weaknesses and strengthen economic resilience;
recovery and resilience of health systems, essential public services and effective social protection mechanisms;
the creation of good jobs;
enhancing the capacity of EU countries to prepare and implement growth-sustaining administrative reforms, including through assistance for the efficient and effective use of the EU funds.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The Regulation is designed as a continuation of the 2017–2020 Structural reform support programme, which had a budget of EUR 142.800 million.
For more information, see:
European Structural Support Programme (European Commission).

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights and obligations: Regulation (EU) 2021/782 (recast)
Rail passengers' rights and obligation: Regulation(EU) 2020/852
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU)* 2021/783 on rail passenger rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality and effectiveness of rail passenger services in order to increase the share of rail transport in relation to other modes of transport.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007.
KEY POINTS
Railpassengers are the weaker party to the transport contract and therefore are entitled to a higher level of consumer protection in the European Union (EU).
EU countries may grant exemptions from certain rules relating to non-discriminatory conditions of transport contracts, to the right to purchase rail tickets without undue difficulty, to railway undertakings’ liability in respect of passengers and their luggage, and to the requirement that railway companies provide adequate security in railway stations and on trains.
Exemption from certain obligations as regards the provision of through-ickets and re-routing
The regulation allows EU countries to grant exemptions to certain services that facilitate the use of rail services by persons with disabilities or persons with reduced mobility, as well as to rail services offered strictly for historic or touristic use. However, EU countries must ensure that these exemptions do not apply to essential rules, in particular not to those provisions relating to:
the right of the rail passenger to purchase a rail ticket;
the obligation of the railway company to ensure that passengers are adequately insured;
their luggage is adequately insured.
exemption from the obligation to provide through-tickets and to re-route passenger journeys
exemptions from the requirement to provide a through-ticket and to ensure a re-ticket in the event of a significant change in the route of a rail service.
The European Commission has adopted a number of amendments to the regulation. These amendments aim to improve consumer protection and to encourage an increase in rail travel, whilst having due regard in particular to Articles 11, 12 and 14 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU). In view of those amendments and in the interests of clarity, the regulation has been recast.
Amendments to the Regulation
The Regulation repeals, repeals or clarifies certain provisions of the previous regulation. It also repeals:
Article 91(1) of the TFEU on the rights of rail passengers;
Article 13(2) of Article 91(3) of Regulation (European Parliament and of the Council on the carriage of goods by rail (see summary);
Article 11(4) of Articles 11(5) and 11(6) of Council Regulation on the transport of passengers (See summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 July 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2023.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Railpassenger’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance: Instrument for Pre-Accession assistance (IPA III)
Pre-AccessION assistance: Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/1529
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation establishing the instrument for pre-accessION assistance (IAPA III)*
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It establishes the instrument to support the European Union’s enlargement process (EU enlargement) by providing financial assistance to countries which have applied for membership of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The instrument covers the period 2021-2027.
The beneficiaries are:
candidate countries for EU membership;
candidates countries for the enlargement of the European Economic Area (EEA) and the candidate countries for accession to the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) and for the accession of candidate countries to the Schengen area.
Annex I lists the priority areas for preaccession aid:
political and institutional reforms;
economic development;
regional cooperation;
transformation of the administrative system;
political dialogue;
human rights and democracy;
the rule of law;
protection of minorities;
market economy;
capacity to cope with competitive pressure and market forces within the EU;
promotion of the aims of political, economic and monetary union.
Each applicant country is assessed on its own merits. The assessment of progress achieved and the identification of shortcomings aim to provide incentives and guidance to the beneficiaries listed in Annex II to pursue the necessary far-reaching reforms.
In Annex III, the beneficiaries are assessed on the basis of the following criteria:
the stability of the institutions which guarantee democracy, the rule of the law, human rights and respect for and protection of minorities, the existence of a functioning market economy as well as the ability to assume not only the rights but also the obligations under the Treaties, including the pursuit of the objectives of political and economic and financial union;
good governance;
reforms of the legal systems;
improvement of the economic system; and
the improvement of the public administration.
A European State which has applied to join the EU can become a member of the Union only where it has been confirmed that it fully meets the accessions criteria established at the Copenhagen European Council in June 1993 (the ‘Copenhagen criteria’) and provided that the Union has the capacity to integrate the new member.
To prepare the beneficiaries for future EU membership, the instrument provides financial assistance for a period of 5 years starting on 1 January 2021 and ending on 31 December 2027. The period is divided into 3 phases:
1.1.2021: the first phase: the applicant countries must:
reform their political and institutional systems and institutions;
2.2.2027: the second phase: improve their economic systems and institutional structures;
3.3.2028: the third phase: reform of the administration and public administration;
4.5.2029: the fourth stage: the final phase: reforms of the internal market;
5.6.2030: the European integration policy;
6.7.2031: the EU enlargement strategy;
7.8.2032: the external action strategy.
Amendments
The regulation has been amended several times, most recently in 2018. The most recent amendment was adopted in December 2018. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 29:
Neighbourhood, development and international cooperation instrument – Global Europe
Neighborhood, Development and International Cooperation Instrument – global Europe
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 establishing the Neighbourhood and Development and Cooperation Instrument - Global Europe (NDFE)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union’s (EU's) programme for the purpose of the multiannual financial framework, the NDFE, which aims to uphold and promote the EU's values, principles and fundamental interests worldwide in order to pursue the objectives and principles of the Union's external action, as laid down in Article 3(5) and Articles 8 and 21 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument covers the period 2021-2022.
The programme is designed to:
contribute to the sustainable economic, social and environmental development of developing countries, with the primary aim of eradicating poverty;

It also contributes to preserving peace, preventing conflicts and strengthening international security, as set out in Articles 209 and 212 of the TEU.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are:
to:
support the development cooperation policy of the EU with developing countries;
to promote the European values and principles, including human rights;
encourage cooperation in all fields of international relations;
support sustainable development at all levels nationally, within the EU, in other countries and at global level.
In line with the objectives of EU external action and its other policies, the EU is to ensure consistency between the different areas of its foreign and security policies, as well as to work for a high degree of cooperation in international relations.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

BACKGROUND
   
The European Union has a special relationship with neighbouring countries, aiming to establish an area of prosperity and good neighbourliness, based on the values of the union and characterised by close and peaceful relations based on cooperation.

Summary 30:
Protecting persons who report breaches of EU law
Protecting individuals who report violations of EU laws
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/1937 on the protection of persons who disclose breaches of Union law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to enhance the enforcement of public procurement rules and to ensure that whistleblowers are protected effectively against retaliation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to acts and policy areas where there is a need to strengthen enforcement, under-reporting by whistleblowers is a key factor affecting enforcement, and breaches of European Union law can cause serious harm to the public interest.
EU countries must introduce effective, confidential and secure reporting channels for whistleblowers. These channels should be open to all persons who work for a public or private organisation or who are in contact with such an organisation in the context of their work-related activities. They should be able to disclose breaches, and should be protected against any form of retaliation. In addition, EU countries must ensure that the confidentiality of these channels is respected and that the information they provide is kept confidential.
Whistleblowers are entitled to:
the protection of their identity and anonymity;
the right to confidentiality and confidentiality of their information;
protection against any type of retaliation;
access to the confidentiality and secrecy of the information that they provide;
exclusion from the confidentiality or confidentiality of the confidential and confidentiality channels.
Exemptions from confidentiality
The legislation applies to the following areas:
contracting authorities and contracting entities in relation to the execution of works, the supply of products or the provision of services, as well as financial institutions;
financial institutions and investment firms;
investment firms and investment banks;
other entities in the financial services sector;
public procurement.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 26 November 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
EU law on public procurement is fragmented across EU countries and uneven across policy areas. The consequences of breaches of the law with a cross-border dimension reported by whistleblowers illustrate how insufficient protection in one Member State negatively impacts the functioning of EU policies not only in that Member State, but also in other Member States and in the Union as a whole. In this context, the importance of providing balanced and effective whistleblower protection is increasingly acknowledged at both EU and international level.
For more information, see:
‘Protecting whistleblowers’ on the European Commission’s website.
REFERENCES

Summary 31:
European Border and Coast Guard (EBCG) — new rules on the European border and coast guard (EBSG)
European Border & Coast Guard(ECBG) – new rules about the EBSG
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 — on the EBCG
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on how the European Union (EU)*’s external border management agency, Frontex, is to be reformed.
It aims to improve the operational capacity of the ECBG.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
EU countries’ external borders, including the Schengen area;
third countries;
the European Union Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders (EASO);
the EBSCG.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the multiannual strategic policy cycle for European integrated border management (EIBM).
The EBCGD has a number of tasks, including:
establishing a technical and operational strategy for EIBM;
carrying out risk analysis and vulnerability assessments;
providing increased technical assistance to EU countries and third countries through joint operations and rapid border interventions;
assisting in search and rescue operations for persons in distress at sea;
organising, coordinating and conducting return operations and return interventions.
In addition, the regulation provides for:
the creation of a European standing corps (e.g. a battalion of officers) to be based in each EU country;
a review of the existing capacity of EU countries to effectively address future border crises;
an evaluation of the effectiveness of the current and future envisaged operational efforts;
exchanges of information and best practice on the operational aspects of EBCD;
cooperation with non-EU countries and the return of returnees.

It entered into force on 1 December 2019.

Summary 32:
Cybersecurity: EU Agency for Network and Information Security (ENISA) and information and communications technology cybersecurity certification (Regulation (EU) 2019/881) (See summary)
Cyberattacks are on the increase and a connected economy and society that is more vulnerable to cyber threats and attacks requires stronger defences. In order to mitigate those risks, all necessary actions need to be taken to improve cybersecurity in the EU so that network and information systems, communications networks, digital products, services and devices used by citizens, organisations and businesses – ranging from small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) to operators of critical infrastructure – are better protected from cyber threats.
ENISA: cybersecurity certification
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union Agency for Networks & Information Security, ENISA, as well as the European Information and Communications Technology (ICT) cybersecurity certification system.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 526/2013.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to ENISA’s activities, including:
the certification of cybersecurity products, ICT services and ICT processes;
the assessment of the state of cybersecurity and resilience in the Union;
monitoring of cybersecurity threats and threats;
assessment of the cybersecurity state of the EU.
EnISA is responsible for:
ensuring that cybersecurity products and services are certified;
providing the public with information on cybersecurity features of ICT products and processes; and
assessing the cybersecurity status of the ICT sector.
provides a regular assessment of cybersecurity risks and threats and assessing the need to improve the cybersecurity of the sector;
preparing for, and responding to, cyberattacks.
The EU countries’ competent authorities are responsible for cybersecurity and law enforcement.
In addition, the European Commission:
is responsible for the implementation of the regulation;
is assisted by the European Cybersecurity Coordination Centre (ECHC);
is the competent authority for cybersecurity policy and coordination.
EU countries:
provide the Commission with a report on cybersecurity and the cybersecurity sector every 2 years;
ensure that the EU is prepared to respond to cyberattacks;
coordinate the response to cybersecurity incidents;
cooperate with other EU countries and international organisations;
make available to the public the information they need to assess cybersecurity risks;
promote cooperation between national authorities and the private sector in the field of cybersecurity;
establish a network of cybersecurity experts and experts in the fields of information and communication technology (ICTs) and cybersecurity.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   கமர  
 
It has applied since 7 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Cybersecurity: cybersecurity: the security and resilience of a network or a digital product or service.
* Information and communication technologies (ICT): the technical features of a product, service or process that make it possible for it to be connected to the internet and to be used by the end user.

Summary 33:
Harmonisation of gross national income at market prices (GNI)
Harmonomics: Harmonisation of Gross National Income at Market Prices
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/516 on the harmonisation of GNI aggregates and repealing Council Directive 89/130/EEC, Euratom
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the EU’s gross national incomes*.
It repeals Council Decision 2007/436/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
gross national income* aggregates* and their components;
the European System of Accounts (ESA 2010);
EU countries’ GNI information visits to other EU countries to verify the quality of the aggregates, their components and their compliance with ESA 2010.
The European Commission (Eurostat) is responsible for carrying out GNI verification visits to EU countries. The Commission may also carry out inspections in EU countries for the purpose of verifying own resources.
National statistical authorities are invited to participate in the verification visits. The participation of representatives of national statistical authorities in these visits is essential in order to ensure the correct application of the definitions and accounting rules of ESA 2010 and to ensure that GNI data are comparable, reliable and exhaustive.
European statistical system committee (ESSC)
The ESSC is chaired by the European Commission and composed of experts from the European Statistical System Committee, the European Union Agency for Fundamental Rights and the European Economic and Social Committee. The ESSC:
is responsible for:
ensuring that the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESC) is respected;
ensures that the sources and methods used to compile GNI statistics are reliable and up-to-date;
assesses the statistical bases and assessment procedures used to produce reliable statistics and, where applicable, makes necessary adjustments, avoiding gaps and double counting;
prepares a report on the implementation of the regulation every 2 years;
provides the European Parliament and the Council with a list of experts who have been delegated to the Commission to prepare reports on the GNI regulation;
regularly consults the ESSC with regard to the preparation of the reports.
Implementation
The EU countries must:
implement the regulation in line with the rules laid down in the ESA 2010 Implementing Decision;
carry out regular GNI inspections in accordance with the procedures set out in the regulation.
Inspections
The Commission must carry out regular inspections in the EU countries, in particular:
in order to verify that the EU aggregates are comparable across EU countries and that their components comply with the relevant ESA 2010 accounting rules;
to verify that EU countries have complied with the requirements laid down by ESA 2010;
on the basis of these inspections, the Commission may make necessary adjustments to the EU aggregate to ensure its comparability and the highest possible exhaustiveness;
in the case of adjustments, it must inform the EU country concerned of the reasons for the adjustments.
Repeal
In accordance with Article 10(1) of Decision 2014/335/EU, Eurom, the regulation repeals the Council Decision 2009/131/EC and its subsequent amendments.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval legislation with regard to the withdrawal of the United Kingdom from the EU
Umbrella of the European Union (EU) — type approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units
EU type approval legislation with regards to the withdraw of the UK from the Union
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out rules to facilitate the placing on the EU market of vehicles and systems from outside the EU that have been type-approved by the UK.
It complements the EU’s existing type approval rules for vehicles and components from other EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles, systems*, components*, separate technical unit* and components* that have previously been approved in the UK;
cars and light commercial vehicles (LCVs) which have been approved by the EU type approval authority in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 168/2013 (see summary).
It does not apply to vehicles or components that have already been approved elsewhere in the EU.
Manufacturers can continue the production of vehicles* and systems* previously based on the UK type approval, and to continue to place such vehicles on the market. However, they must not place vehicles on their market if they do not comply with all the legal requirements applicable to the UK vehicle and component market.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers continue to have the greatest freedom to choose the new EU typeapproval authority from which they wish to place on their EU market vehicles. This choice must be made transparent to all stakeholders, including the UK manufacturers and the European Commission.
.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that:
the new vehicles are not subject to any legal obstacles to their placing on their respective markets;
the vehicle and components are subject to the same conditions as those applicable to existing vehicles;
new vehicles are covered by the same rules as existing vehicles.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation (EU)* 2019/26 has applied since 30 March 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
The United Kingdom notified the European Parliament and the Council on 29 March 2017 of its intention to withdraw from the union pursuant to Article 50 of the Treaty on European Union. The withdrawal agreement as agreed between the EU and the UK contains arrangements allowing for the application of EU law to and in the country beyond the date that the Treaties cease to apply.
For more information, see:
United Kingdom withdrawal — European Union type approval (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle, system or component of a vehicle or component that is intended for use in a vehicle.
* Component: any part of a system, component or component which is intended to be used in the vehicle or in a component other than the vehicle itself.

Summary 35:
European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation (Europol)
Europol: European Union agency for law enforcement cooperation (EuroPOL)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/794 on Europol
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing Europol’s activities.
It replaces and repeals Council Decisions 2009/371/JHA and 2009/934/JHHA.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes Europol as an entity of the European Union (EU), funded from the general budget of the EU, to support and strengthen action by EU countries’ law enforcement authorities and their mutual cooperation in preventing and combating organised crime, terrorism and other forms of serious crime affecting two or more EU countries.
As serious crime often occurs across EU borders, it is necessary to equip Europol to better support EU countries in EU-wide crime prevention, analyses and investigations. This regulation establishes procedures for the scrutiny of Europol's activities by the European Parliament, together with national parliaments, subject to point (c) of Article 12 of the Treaty on European Union and Protocol No 1 on the role of the National Parliaments in the EU.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules for the Europol Parliamentary Committee, which is composed of representatives of EU countries and chaired by the Commission, to examine the activities of the agency and its activities. The committee is responsible for:
examining the effectiveness and effectiveness;
assisting the European Commission in Europol in its work;
;
providing the Commission with information on the activities;
withdrawing from its budget the funds it receives from the EU general budget for Europol;
exchanging information on its activities with the national authorities;
preparing a report on its work.
The European Parliament and the Council adopt the regulation on the basis of a proposal from the Commission. The Commission adopts the regulation in accordance with the rules laid down in Article 88 of the TFEU (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
 
It has applied since 24 May 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
EuroPOL was set up in 2009. It is funded by the EU General Budget.
For more information, see:
‘An open and secure Europe serving and protecting citizens’ website.

* Europol: a European police office established under the Treaty of Lisbon.

Summary 36:
European instrument for temporary support to mitigate unemployment risks in an emergency (SURE) following the COVID-19 outbreak (until 30 March 2020)
European instrument to mitigate employment risks in a European emergency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/672 on the establishment of a European Instrument for Temporary Support to Mitigate Unemployment Risks in an Emergency (SURES)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European instrument to help EU countries cope with temporary unemployment risks following the outbreak of the severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus-2 (SARS-CoV-2) in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that are in difficulties or are seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control.
EU countries must apply to the European Commission for EU financial assistance to cover the actual and possibly also planned increases in public expenditure relating to the effects on the labour markets of the EU countries.
It applies to short-time work schemes for employees and similar measures, in particular for the self-employed, as well as expenditure on some health-related measures, where appropriate, in the workplace.
Beneficiaries of the regulation are EU countries which have implemented extraordinary measures to contain the outbreak and its impact on their labour force.
The European Commission must submit a proposal to the Council, on a proposal from the European Parliament and the Council of the measures appropriate to respond to the socio‐economic situation following COVID. The Council decides on the basis of the Commission’s proposal, on the measures to be taken by the EU country concerned.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
From 1 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
The COVID outbreak has had a massive and disruptive impact on the economic systems of EU countries and has caused societal disruptions and increased public expenditure in a growing number of them.
For more information, see:
‘SURE’ on the European Agency for Employment and Social Affairs' website.

Summary 37:
Recovery and resolution of central counterparties: EU framework for the recovery and recovery of counterparties (Regulation (EU) 2021/23) (European Commission)
Recovery, resolution and recovery procedures for central counterpartying (Regulations (EU, Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) 2020/23 on a framework for recovery and resolution (CCPs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a framework to recover and resolve the insolvency of central counterparty (CCP) counterparties* in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
CCPs authorised in the EU;
the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Central Bank (ECB);
EU countries’ competent authorities, working together within supervisory colleges which group together relevant authorities for the specific tasks allocated to them.
It applies to all CCPs authorised to clear financial and commodity derivatives, including listed and over-the-counter (OTC) financial derivatives, cash equities, bonds and other products such as repos. It does not apply to clearing of credit default swap (CDS) derivatives.
Rules for the resolution of CCPs
The resolution of a CCP’s insolvent counterparties is governed by the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 648/2012 on the prudential, organisational and conduct of the CCPs. These rules aim to ensure that when a CCP is in financial distress or is at the point of failure, it does not de-stabilise the entire EU financial market and damage growth across the wider economy. The resolution procedures are based on the following principles:
the CCP must honour its obligations created by the transactions;
it must provide adequate collateral from its members as margin and as contributions to default funds.
If a CCP fails to provide sufficient collateral, it may be subject to special proceedings for the purpose of preventing the disruption of the financial stability of the EU economy. However, the resolution procedures must not jeopardise the continuity of the economy.
The European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the resolution procedure;
ensuring that CCPs comply with the requirements of the regulation;
assisting the ECB and the national authorities in the resolution process;
providing the ECB with the information it needs to ensure the proper functioning of the European financial system;
coordinating with the other EU countries and the EBA;
advising the ECB, the European Financial Stability Facility (EFSA) and national authorities on the application of the rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2023.
BACKGROUND
EU financial markets are pivotal for the functioning of modern economies. The more integrated they are, the greater the potential for efficient allocation of economic resources will be, potentially benefitting economic performance.
For more information, see:
‘Central counterparties’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 38:
European Year of Rail (2021)
European year of Rail
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/2228 on a European Year of rail
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European year of rail to mark the European Union’s commitment to becoming a climate-neutral Union by 2050.
It aims to promote sustainable and smart mobility in order to tackle climate change.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision applies to the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023. It does not apply to:
the EU countries’ national rail administrations;
the European Union Agency for Railways and the European Network of European Railways (ENERGY);
the Member States’ authorities responsible for the implementation of the Trans-European Transport Network (TEN-T) and for the management of the rail freight corridors.
Objectives
The aim of the decision is to encourage the use of rail for the transport of people, goods and capital in the EU. It also aims to improve the efficiency of the EU rail freight network and to promote the development of sustainable intermodal transport.
The objective of the European year is to promote a European railway area, which aims to create a single European rail area. The aim of this is to ensure that the rail sector has a dominant role in the European economy and that it is not only the main means of transport, but also the main mode of transport for the EU citizens and the economy as a whole of Europe.
In order to achieve this objective, the decision calls on the Member States to take the necessary measures:
to:
ensure that rail services are more affordable, accessible, cleaner and more energy-efficient;
encourage people to use sustainable transport modes, such as walking, cycling and public transport;
improve the quality and safety of rail services;
increase the frequency of passenger and freight services, particularly in rural areas and in remote regions;
strengthen rail safety and security;
promote the integration of rail into the economic and social cohesion of the whole of the Union;
make rail services more affordable and accessible to the general public;
facilitate access to rail services for the general population and to the rural areas;
provide the public with better information on rail services, including the availability of information on timetables, timetables and timetables;
develop and improve the capacity of the national rail networks;
implement measures to ensure the safety of passengers, freight and passengers;
support the development and maintenance of the trans-European transport network, including by:
improving the quality of the infrastructure;
ensuring the safety and reliability of the railway network;
enhancing the efficiency and effectiveness of the freight and passenger services, in particular by improving the safety, security and security of the passenger services; and
promoting the development, maintenance and operation of rail infrastructure.
Implementing measures
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the decision. It must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 2 years on its implementation. The report must include a description of the measures taken to achieve the objectives set out in the communication on the European Green Deal. The Commission must also present a report on the progress made by Member States in implementing this decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The regulation applies from 21 January 2021. Member States must incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
The COVID-19 crisis has hit the transport sector exceptionally hard. Despite operational and financial constraints, the sector has maintained crucial connections both for the transportation of people and of essential goods. This has been possible mainly thanks to the employees who have continued working under difficult and uncertain conditions. The strategic role played by rail during the COVID crisis has highlighted that achieving the single railway area is necessary to transform the EU economy and to rethink policies in particular in the field of transport and mobility.
For more information, see:
European Year 2020 (European Commission).

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 39:
European Security and Defence College (ESDC) - mission, mission, objectives and tasks (OJ L 348, 20.10.2020 - not published in the Official Journal)
European Security & Defence College - mission and objectives (OSD)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/1515 establishing a European Security and Defense College
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It establishes the European Security & Defense College (ESSDC) to provide training and education in the field of the EU’s common security and defence policy (CSDP) at European level in order to develop and promote a common understanding of CFSP and CSDP among civilian and military personnel and to identify and disseminate best practices in relation to these policies.
KEY POINTS
Objectives
The ESDC has the following objectives:
to further enhance the common European security & defence culture within the EU and to promote the principles laid down in Article 21(1) of the Treaty on European Union (TEU) outside the EU;
to promote a better understanding of CSDP as an essential part of CFDP;
in order to provide EU administrations and staff with knowledgeable personnel able to work efficiently on all CSDP matters within the wider context of the European External Action Service (EEAS);
to provide EU policies, institutions and procedures with knowledge of EU policies and procedures in the CSDP.
To promote the European Initiative the ESDC promotes the exchange of young officers and PhD-level research in CSDP domains;
the promotion of research in domains, including in the fields of defence, security and cyber.
Activities
ESDC activities include:
training and education activities;
exchange of experience and best practices;
organisation of courses and seminars;
coordination and coordination of activities with other EU institutions;
promotion of training and training-related activities with EU countries’ administrations and other EU countries;
cooperation with the EEAS;
supporting civilian crisis management including in conflict prevention and establishing or preserving the conditions necessary for sustainable development.
Organisation of ESDC activities
EsDC activities are organised by the Executive Academic Board, which is composed of seconded national experts and is chaired by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy. The ESDC is funded by the General Secretariat of the Council.

Summary 40:
Minimum requirements for water reuse in the European Union (EU) (Regulation 2020/741 on minimum requirements for Water Reuse
Maximum requirements for the reuse of treated waste water
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2000/60/EC — minimum requirements on water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements to be met by EU countries to ensure that treated wastewater* is reused whenever it is appropriate and cost-efficient.
It aims to facilitate the uptake of water reuse whenever appropriate.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to wastewater treated in urban wastewater treatment plants. It does not apply to wastewater discharged into surface water bodies or groundwater bodies.
EU countries may decide to extend the scope of the regulation to groundwater bodies and to other water bodies, subject to certain conditions.
The water resources of the EU are increasingly coming under pressure, leading to water scarcity and a deterioration in water quality. In particular, climate change, unpredictable weather patterns and drought are contributing significantly to the strain on the availability of freshwater, arising from urban development and agriculture. Water reuse is a promising option for many EU countries, but many of them do not have the resources to do so. This regulation sets out the minimum requirements that EU countries must apply to water reuse.
Requirements for EU countries
The legislation applies to:
water treatment plants and treatment plants;
water bodies and groundwater bodies;
treated waste water discharged into water bodies; and
water-saving irrigation techniques.
In addition to the regulation’s annexes, EU countries may adopt supplementary measures.
 such as the use of water-efficient technologies in industry and water-saving technologies in agriculture.
the reuse of wastewater in urban water treatment plants, water-efficiency measures in agriculture and water efficiency measures in industry.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLACE INTO FORCE?
 
   
It applies from 1 July 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2023.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a series of actions to promote water reuse, including:
a communication of 2 December 2015 on ‘Closing the loop – an EU action plan for the circular economy’;
the communication of 14 November 2012 on the ‘A Blueprint to Safeguard Europe's Water Resources’.
For more information, see:
‘Water Reuse’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 41:
EU Blue Card — conditions of entry and residence of non-EU nationals for the purpose of highly qualified employment (recast of Directive 2009/50/EC)
EU blue card — conditions for entry and residency of non–EU citizens for the job market
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2021/1883 on the conditions of admission and residence for highly qualified workers from outside the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to make it easier for EU citizens to enter and stay in the EU and to work in the European Union (EU).
It repeals and repeals the previous EU Blue Card directive (Directive 2009/49/EC).
KEY POINTS
The directive applies regardless of whether the initial purpose of residence of the non-European Union citizen (non-EU citizen) is highly qualified work or is another purpose which then changes to the purpose.
EU citizens who are highly qualified and who have been granted a Blue Card must have the right to:
apply for a residence permit;
be admitted to the EU on the basis of their skills and experience;
have the same rights and benefits as other EU citizens.
Member States must ensure that applicants are not treated less favourably than other applicants for the same job. They must also ensure that EU citizens are treated equally in terms of equal treatment and that the same information is provided to employers and the same conditions are applied to applicants.
Applicants must be informed of the procedure and the rights and obligations that apply for the EU blue card, including:
the right to employment, the right of residence and the possibility to work, andthe right of mobility within the EU.
the rights and responsibilities of the holder, including the possibility of working in another EU country for a period of up to 3 years;
the possibility to be employed in a different EU country than the holder of an EU Blue card.
The card is valid for 3 years. It may be renewed for a further period of 3 years after the expiry of the 3-year period of validity of the card. Member States must inform the European Commission and the European Parliament of any changes that may be necessary to extend the duration of the blue card. The Commission must submit a report every 3 years on the implementation of the directive.
Blue card holders are entitled to the following:
a residence permit issued by the Member State in which they are resident;
a work permit issued in another Member State;
an employment permit issued to an employer in a Member State where they are employed;
access to employment and promotion opportunities in the Member States in which the holder is resident.
In addition, the card holder is entitled to a number of other rights and privileges, including access to employment opportunities in other Member States and to travel within the Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 20 October 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission communication of 3 March 2010 entitled ‘Europe 2020: A strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth’ sets the objectives of the Union becoming an economy based on knowledge and innovation, reducing the administrative burden on companies and better matching labour supply with demand. It calls for a more attractive and effective Union-wide scheme to attract and retain highly qualified foreign workers.
For more information, see:
European Blue Card (European Commission).

Summary 42:
Asylum, migration and integration fund
Asylum and migration fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1147 establishing the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s Asylum and Migration Fund.
It aims to support efforts to ensure a comprehensive approach to the management of migration that is based on mutual trust, solidarity and fair sharing of responsibility among EU countries and EU institutions, with the objective of ensuring a common sustainable EU policy on asylum and immigration.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The fund covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023.
EU countries are responsible for:
reception, asylum, integration and migration systems in the Member States;
reintegration of non-EU nationals in the European Union (EU);
sharing of responsibility between the EU countries for the return of asylum seekers and refugees;
promoting international protection measures;
sharing responsibility for the transfer of vulnerable asylum seekers between EU countries.
Funding
The Fund is funded by the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) and is managed by the Commission.
The EU countries must:
provide the Commission with the necessary financial resources for the implementation of the Fund;
ensure that the Fund is implemented in line with the rights and principles enshrined in the EU acquis and the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU (Article 67 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) and the principles of gender equality, non-discrimination and the best interests of the child in migration;
take the necessary measures to ensure that children in migration are considered in all actions or decisions concerning children, including returns, taking full account of the right to express their views.
In addition, the Commission must submit a report every 3 years on how the Fund has been implemented. The report must include:
the results achieved and investments made with the support of its predecessors: the European Refugee Fund established by Decision No 573/2007/EC;
the European Fund for the Integration of third-country nationals established by Council Decision 2007/435/EC for the period 2007-2013;
a report on the progress made by EU countries in the period 2008-2013.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 15 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Asylum, Mobility & Integration Fund (EMIF) is the EU's main migration fund. It was established to support the efforts of EU countries to improve their reception, asylum and integration systems, to prevent and adequately handle situations of pressure in solidarity, and to replace irregular and unsafe arrivals with legal and safe pathways.
For more information, see:
‘Asylum & Migration Fund’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 43:
Work-life balance for parents and carers: European Union rules
Work - life balance
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/1158 on work-life-balance for parents, carers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to promote equal opportunities for both men and women in the labour market and the equal sharing of the care responsibilities between men* and women.
It repeals Council Directive 2010/18/EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to the EU countries that have not ratified the United Nations Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities.
EU countries must ensure that:
all parents have the right to paid maternity leave and to parental leave following the birth or adoption of a child, to reconcile family and professional life;
carers are entitled to the same treatment as men in terms of working hours and paid leave;
all employers have the obligation to ensure that all employees have the same working hours;
employers are required to take into account the needs of both parents and children in the calculation of their pay and working conditions.
National authorities must take the following into account when drawing up their national policies:
the number of hours worked by women and men;
the nature of the work and family responsibilities;
children’s age and gender of the parents;
parental leave and the duration of the leave.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that all workers have equal access to paid parental leave and paid maternity and parental leave; and paid paternity leave, irrespective of the age of the child;
make sure that both parents have equal opportunities to work and to be paid for the same work;
provide for equal treatment of children with disabilities.
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   ъ ш щ  
It has applied since 1 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU is committed to gender equality in all areas, including employment, work and pay.
For more information, see:
‘Gender equality’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Paternity leave: the period of time during which a person is not working for the purpose of providing care for a child.
Parental leave: a period of unpaid leave during which both parents are not working and the child is not being cared for.

Fight against corruption to the Union’S financial interests

Summary 45:
Interchange fees for card-based payment transactions: EU rules on interchange fees (until 31 December 2020)
INTERFERENCE FEES FOR CARD-BANKING TRANSACTIONS
Interference fees for credit transfers and direct debits in euro
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/751 of the European Parliament and of the Council — interchange fees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that interchange fees* charged by credit transfer and direct debit card issuers* are the same as those charged by the card issuer for cross-border payment transactions in euro.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
interchange fees* are not excessive and that they do not exceed the cost borne by the trader for the use of a given means of payment, i.e. the cost of acquiring the card.
The regulation applies to:
card-based payments and internet and mobile payments based on cards;
payment service providers’ fees;
merchant fees.
It does not apply to the interchange fees charged by payment service providers for the payment of goods and services to merchants for the purpose of acquiring payment services.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the fees charged for the processing of credit transfers are not disproportionate to the costs associated with the payment service provided by the credit transfer or direct debit service provider;
apply the ‘honour all cards’ rule to all card-related transactions;
allow new payment services providers to participate in the same interchange fee scheme;
inform the European Central Bank of Europe (ECBANK) of any changes in the level of interchange fees applicable to the EU’s payment card network.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
  
It applies from 31 December 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Union has a single payment card market. However, integration of the market is far from complete as many payment solutions cannot develop beyond their national borders and new pan-Union players are prevented from entering the market. This regulation aims to remove obstacles to the efficient functioning of the card market, including in the area of card-payment transactions.
For more information, see:
‘Interchange Fees’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 47:
Statistics of goods transport by inland waterways (codification) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/974 on statistics of goods transported by inland waters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 1365/2006, which had been substantially amended several times.
It aims to improve the comparability of EU statistics on all modes of transport, with the aim of achieving the fullest practicable comparability between transport modes.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
transport of goods by inland waterways, including by cross-border transport services;
traffic in and out of the European Union (EU).
It does not cover:
passenger transport of goods;
cross-border passenger transport;
transit of goods between EU Member States.
EU Member States must transmit the data to the European Commission (Eurostat).
Eurostat transmits the results of the surveys to the national statistical institutes and the European Statistical System (ESSS) of the Member States, which then transmit the results to Eurostat.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU. The Commission must ensure that those delegated acts do not impose a significant additional burden on Member States or on the respondents.
Quality of the data
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the transmission of the results by the Commission, Eurostat must develop and publish methodological requirements and criteria designed to ensure the quality of the statistical data produced.
In addition, the Commission must adopt implementing powers to enable it to adopt arrangements for transmitting data, including data interchange standards, for the dissemination of results by Eurostat, and also to develop and to publish methodological standards and criteria to ensure that the data produced comply with the regulation’s requirements.
National authorities must:
ensure that:
the data are collected according to the standards set out in Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 223/2009 on the production of European statistical standards;
that the data are produced by the national authorities referred to in Article 5/2009 of the regulation; and
that they do not use the terms ‘transport’ or ‘inland waterway’ in their official language.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation codifies, repeals and replaces Regulation (Euromodal Transport Regulation) 2013/1365.
For more information, see:
‘Inland waterways statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Inland waterways: waterway: any body of water, river or other natural or artificial waterway, which is used to transport goods, passengers or goods in or out of a Member State.

Summary 48:
Rail transport statistics: European Union rules
Rail transportation statistics: EU rules
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/643 on rail transport statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down EU rules on the collection, transmission and dissemination of railway transport statistics in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of EU statistics on the transport of goods and passengers by rail.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 91/2003, which had been substantially amended several times.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all railway undertakings operating in a commercial rail transport market in the EU and to Eurostat, the EU’s statistical office.
EU countries must ensure that:
rail transport statistics are collected and disseminated by Eurostat in accordance with the rules laid down by the European Parliament and the Council;
the data are transmitted to the European Commission and Eurostat by the EU countries under the authority of the bodies and institutions in charge of producing official statistics;
Eurostat is responsible for the transmission of the data to the Commission and for the dissemination of the statistics to the public.
The data are collected by EU countries’ competent authorities.
Data collection and dissemination
Each year, EU countries must send Eurostat:
data on:
the number of passengers and goods transported by rail;
traffic accidents;
passenger numbers;
rail freight movements;
total number of freight movements.
Railway traffic accidents.
Each EU country must transmit the data it receives from the EU country concerned.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that the data are:
 collected in line with the requirements laid down in Annex I to Directive 2012/34/EU on the production of official statistics on all modes of transport (Annex II to Directive 2004/49/EC as regards common safety indicators and common methods of calculating accident costs);
transmitted to EuroStat by the country in which the data were collected;
receive from Eurostat the data on freight movements and freight movements by rail, as well as freight movements between EU countries;
collect data on the number of passenger movements and the total number of rail freight movements in each EU country;
transmit the data collected by the national authorities for the purposes of producing EU statistics.
National authorities must transmit to the EU the data they have received from the European Agency for Railways (Eurostat) on the annual number of train accidents and the annual data on rail passengers. The Commission may adopt measures to reduce the data transmission period for annual passenger data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 2 May 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 May 2019.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person travelling by rail to and from a destination.
* Train: a train that carries goods and/or passengers.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    The regulation repeals and repeals Council Regulation 91/3/EC.
  It repeals the previous regulation (Regulation 91/03/EC) and its subsequent amendments.
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 49:
Third countries whose nationals must be in possession of visas when crossing the external borders and those whose nationals are exempt from that requirement (EU) 2018/1806) (codification) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 — third-country nationals’ visa requirement and the exemption from the visa requirement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the list of non-EU countries that require their nationals to carry a visa when crossing EU external borders.
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC, No 401/2001) which had been substantially amended several times.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to nationals of the following countries:
Iceland,
Norway,
Switzerland,
United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland.
Exemptions
EU countries may exempt certain categories of persons, such as:
recognised refugees and stateless persons;
public school pupils;
visa-pass holders;
non-governmental organisations (NGOs);
local traffic permit holders.
Non-EU country nationals may be exempt from the requirement if:
they reside in an EU country that does not require a visa;
they have a valid residence permit issued by the EU country in which they are resident;
their residence permit is valid for a period of no more than 90 days in any 180-day period.
National authorities may suspend the exemption if they consider that:
the situation has changed in relation to the status or designation of certain countries or entities; or
the conditions of entry into the EU have changed.
Visa exemption
The EU country which determines whether an EU national is exempt from a visa must make a case-by-case assessment of a variety of criteria, including the nationality of the person concerned and the validity of the travel document. The decision is based on the third country of origin of the EU national and on the criteria set out in Annex I to the regulation.
If a country considers that its national law does not allow it to grant an exemption, it must inform the other EU countries and the European Commission of its decision.
The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the application of the regulation and on any amendments to the annexes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Visas: a visa issued by a third country for the purpose of allowing its nationals to enter the EU.
* Exemption: an exemption granted to a person who is no longer a national of the country issuing the visa, but who is a resident of that country and who is entitled to an exemption under international law.

Summary 50:
Eurojust — European Union Agency for Criminal Justice Cooperation (Eurojust)
Eurojust – European Union agency for criminal justice cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1727 on Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the legal framework for Eurojust, the EU’s agency for judicial justice cooperation.
It repeals and repeals Council Decision 2002/187/JHA.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that participate in enhanced cooperation on the establishment of the European Public Prosecutor's Office (EPPO).
It does not apply to countries that do not participate in the EPPO.
The European Public Prosecutors Office is not competent for offences affecting the financial interests of the EU. However, it may exercise its competence in cases where it is not, although the European Judicial Authority (EJA) is competent. In such cases, Eurojust may act at the request of the non-participating EU countries or at the EJA's request, or at a request of Eurojust.
EuroJust is governed by a regulation, adopted in accordance with the ordinary legislative procedure. It also requires the European Parliament and the national parliaments to participate in Eurojust‘s activities.
In order to facilitate cooperation between Eurojust and the EJPO, the regulation sets out the rules for the Eurojust Board, which comprises the European Commission and the European Parliamentary and Council of Europe (EPE) and the Council of the Union. The regulation also lays down the rules on the role of the EPE and the evaluation of the regulation.
EU countries’ participation in the evaluation procedure of EuroJust.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 51:
Combating money laundering by criminal law
Combating the money laundering problem
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1673 on combating money laundering
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to combat money laundering and the related financing of terrorism and organised crime by means of criminal law.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that all offences that are punishable by a term of imprisonment as set out in this directive are considered predicate offences for money laundering. Moreover, to the extent that the application of those penalty thresholds does not already do so, EU countries should include a range of offences within each of the categories of offences listed in this Directive.
Where a category of offences, such as terrorism or environmental offences, includes legal acts of the EU, this directive should refer to those legal acts. Any kind of punishable involvement in the commission of a predicate offence as criminalised in accordance with national law should also be considered as a criminal activity for the purposes of this directive.
In cases where EU law allows EU countries to provide for sanctions other than criminal sanctions, this Directive should not require EU States to classify the offences in those cases as predicate offences. In that case, Member States should decide how to delimit the range of offence within each category.
Taxation and tax-related offences
In addition to tax offences, the directive applies to indirect tax crimes. These include:
the transfer of funds between accounts;
the exchange of information on tax payments;
any other form of tax evasion.
The directive also applies to:
money laundering offences committed by public office holders in line with their national legal traditions;
tax-related tax crimes committed by tax authorities;
non-tax related tax offences.
Member States should ensure that:
all tax offences are covered by this directive, including:
tax evasion;
falsification of tax information;
transfers of funds;
exchange of information between tax authorities.
Exceptions
The Directive does not apply to certain types of tax offences such as tax evasion, tax fraud, tax avoidance, tax evasion and tax evasion in relation to the proceeds of crime.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Fighting money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �   барнанска гаранка  харкова  настанна бълакограшка, годанца манта каккокрак гласки  гнаснкой гшанога,  балкомакнак  клабрагакцка (Directive 2018/849 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 October 2018 on combatting money laundering through criminal law) (OJ L 284, 12.11.2018, pp. 22-24)

Summary 52:
Recovery plan for bluefin tuna in the eastern Atlantic and Mediterranean
Recovery Plan for Bluefin tuna
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1627 on a multiannual recovery plan for Atlantic Tunas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the implementation of the recovery plan set out in the International Convention for the Conservation of Atlantic Tuna (ICCAT) adopted in 2006.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 302/2009.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The recovery plan covers the period from 2007 to 2022. It covers fishing in the Atlantic Ocean and the Mediterranean.
EU countries must ensure that fishing gear and fishing techniques are selective and have a reduced environmental impact, including gear and techniques used in traditional and artisanal fisheries, thereby contributing to a fair standard of living for local economies.
The plan sets out:
the total allowable catch and the fishing capacity;
the fishing limit;
rules concerning sport and recreational fishing activities, farming and fishing capacity measures, as well as the ICCAT Scheme of Joint International Inspection (JIIS);
rules on the transfer and caging of live tuna;
measures concerning the monitoring of fishing gear;
requirements for the monitoring and evaluation of the fishing fleet;
guidelines on the use of fishing equipment and techniques; and
rules for monitoring and evaluating the fishing gear.
European Commission Implementing Regulation (EU countries’ obligations
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 2 years on the implementation and implementation of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULAR REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
Regulations (Regulation 2016/1559/2007 and 2016/1827/EU have applied since 1 January 2007. The regulation repeals Council Regulations (EC).
BACKGROUND
The EU is party to ICCAT’s 2006 recommendation for the conservation of bluefin tuna.
For more information, see:
‘Bluefin tuna recovery plan’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Bluefin Tuna: a species of tuna that is protected under the Convention on the conservation and management of Atlantic Tuna.

Summary 53:
Protecting the EU industry against injurious pricing of vessels
Protecting The EU Industry Against Injurious Pricing of Vessels
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1035 on protection against the sale of ships below their normal value
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the calculation of the ‘normal value’* of a vessel.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 385/96.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any vessel sold below its normal value. It does not apply to:
the sale of a ship to a non-EU country for use in international trade;
the export of a similar vessel to an EU country;
ship-purchase transactions.
The normal value is calculated on the basis of a representative sale of the same vessel in the ordinary course of trade in the exporting country.
EU countries must inform the European Commission of the normal value of the vessel when it is sold to an importer or to a buyer outside the EU. The normal value must not be lower than the value calculated in accordance with the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement (see summary).
The Commission must take all necessary steps to ascertain the validity of the accounting structure of the seller’s accounts and the accounting rules of the buyer.
If a vessel is sold at a price below the normal price, it must be investigated by the Commission. The Commission may also take action against the seller if it considers that the sale is likely to cause injury to the Union industry.
 or that the vessel is not to be at a loss.
In the event of a sale at a lower normal value, the EU country must notify the other EU countries of its intention to take action. The EU countries must also inform the Commission of their intention to do so.
Expiry procedure
A vessel may be subject to an investigation by the EU only when the buyer is a Union buyer, and provided the ship is not a military vessel. It is expedient to define the circumstances in which a domestic sale may be considered to be made at loss and may be disregarded, and in which recourse may be had to sale of such a vessel to a third country, or to another EU country, at a higher price.
Importers and importers must ensure that:
a ship is sold for a price that is not below the market price of the country in which it is registered;
it is not sold for less than the normal market price;
a vessel has been repaired or rebuilt for a higher market price than its normal market value;
there is no difference between the price paid for the repair or reconstruction of the ship and the price at which the vessel was sold.
Shipowners must be informed of the procedure to be followed in the case of any sale of vessels at a below-normal market price. They must also provide the Commission with a list of the vessels for which they are liable to be held liable for any damage caused by the sale at less than their normal price. In addition, the Commission must inform them of the measures to be taken in the event that they consider that a vessel has not been sold at the price set out in the regulation.
Where a vessel appears to have been sold for sale below its market price, the vessel must be inspected and, where appropriate, the owner must be notified of the reasons for its sale. The vessel must also be examined in the light of the information provided in the annex to the regulation and any other relevant information provided by the shipowner or importer.
Annex III to the 1994 anti-dumping agreement contains the basic principles of the Shipbuilding Agreement and the IPI Code. It sets out the procedure for the enforcement of the EU anti-injurious pricing rules.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULAR REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
Regulations (EU, EU) 2017/1036 and (EU), 2017/1139 have applied since 1 July 2017.
Regulated (EU).
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to protect the industry against the threat of anti-competitive conditions in the commercial shipbuilding and repair industry. These include:
measures to prevent and combat the abuse of subsidies and countervailing measures;
measures for the protection of the interests of shipowners and operators.
For more information, see:
‘Injunctive pricing’ (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Importers: any person, company or organisation that has a dominant position in the market for the sale or repair of vessels.
* Shipowner: any individual or group of individuals or bodies that has an exclusive right to sell a vessel for a particular purpose.

Summary 54:
Cableway installations — conformity assessment and authorisation (Regulation (EU) 2016/424 on cableway installations and repealing Directive 2000/9/EC (SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2000/424 of the European Parliament and of the Council on cableways
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the essential requirements and conformity assessment procedures for cableways* that are designed, constructed and operated with a view to transporting persons.
It repeals and repeals the previous cableway directive (Directive 1999/98/EC).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
new cableways, such as funicular railways, aerial ropeways (cable cars, gondolas, chairlifts) and drag lifts;
subsystems* for the transport of persons;
modifications of existing cableways;
installations that are new to the EU or are being relocated from one EU country to another;
new components and safety components made by a third party.
EU countries must apply the regulation’s essential requirements to new cableways and to modifications of existing installations. The regulation does not apply to installations that have been adapted from non-EU countries and that have not been incorporated into the EU territory.
Comformity assessment procedures
The European Commission must carry out a conformity assessment of a cableway installation before authorising it to be placed on the market in the EU. It must submit a report to the European Commission every 5 years. The report must contain the following information:
the type of the cableway and its components;
the design and construction of the installation;
a description of the components and subsystems covered by the regulation.
the conformity assessment procedure.
The Commission must submit the report within 5 years of the submission of the report and submit it to the national competent authority.
If the Commission considers that the cableways comply with the regulation, it must issue a certificate of conformity to the installation. The certificate must bear the CE marking and be accompanied by a copy of the conformity assessment report. The Commission must transmit the report to all EU countries within 6 months of receiving the report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 31 March 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
Cableways are mainly lift systems used in high-altitude tourist resorts, in urban transport facilities or in sports facilities.
For more information, see:
European cableways (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Cableway: a lift system used to transport persons using a cable.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities in the European Union (EU) (Directive 2019/882)
Assessment of the accessibility requirements for products, services and assistive technologies in the EU.
Accessibility requirements for goods and services in the Member States
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU)* 2019/884 on the accessibility of products, service and equipment for people with disabilities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve access to accessible products* and services within the EU by eliminating and preventing barriers to the free movement of certain accessible products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services designed or adapted to the specific needs of persons who have any physical, mental, intellectual or sensory impairments*, age related impairments, or other human body performance related causes, permanent or temporary, which, in interaction with various barriers, result in their reduced access to products or services, leading to a situation that requires those products and service to be adapted to their particular needs.
It does not apply to the following products:
lifts and escalators;
accessibility of lifts, escalators, lifts and walkways;
chairs and seat belts;
personal protective equipment (PPE);
accessories for persons who are blind or visually impaired.
EU Member States must ensure that:
all products, equipment and services that meet the requirements of the directive are made available to persons with disability;
they are made accessible to persons who do not have access to them;
there is no discrimination based on their disability status.
the directive does not cover:
the use of lifts or escalators
the accessibility of escalators or walkways
access to and from public places
the access to and use of walkways.
Exemptions
Member States may exclude certain products from the scope of this directive, such as:
medical devices
medical equipment
accessory devices
accessive technologies
accessorisation of buildings and buildings
accessible signs and information.
In addition, Member States may exempt from the directive certain products that are not covered by the directive, for example:
vehicles and equipment used in the transport sector,
vehicle-related equipment and accessories,
equipment used for the transport of goods and for the use of assistive technology.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 7 June 2019. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 7 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
Accessible products
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility Directive’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Persons with disabilities: any person who has a long-term disability, including those who have sensory or physical impairments.
ACT
Director-General of the Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion of Persons with Disabilities (DGESD) - European Commission.
