Summary 56:
Supply chain due diligence obligations for EU importers of tin, tantalum and tungsten, their ores, and gold originating from conflict-affected and high-risk areas (Regulation (EU) 2017/821)
Suppliers must ensure that their supply chain checks are carried out when importing raw materials originating from areas affected by conflict and high risk areas.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down supply-chain due diligence* obligations for European Union importers* of tin (Tin) and tantalum (Tungsten) ores and non-gold originating from the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC) and its neighbours in Central Africa.
It aims to prevent the financing of armed groups and security forces in resource-rich areas by controlling trade in minerals.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
Tin, tantalums, tungstens, gold and copper ores originating in the DRC and Central Africa, as well as to the United States of America (US) and the European Economic Area (EEA).
Importers must:
ensure that their imports comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation;
check that the minerals they are importing are not being used for weapons of mass destruction (WMDs) and that they are not used for human rights abuses;
apply due diligence procedures when carrying out their checks.
EU importers must also ensure that they:
identify the sources of the minerals;
report to the European Commission and the results of their checks;
the results of the checks to the EU country’s competent authorities;
provide the Commission with a report on their compliance with the regulation.
The Commission must also:
assess the impact of the regulation on human rights and the environment;
make a report to the Commission every 3 years on the implementation of this regulation. The Commission must submit a report every 5 years on its implementation to the Council, the European Parliament, the Council of Europe and the Economic and Social Committee of the European Union (ESC).
EU countries must also inform the Commission of the measures they have taken to ensure that the regulation is complied with.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLACE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 19 May 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 19 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has been actively engaged in an initiative of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD) to advance the responsible sourcing of minerals from conflict areas, which has resulted in the adoption of the OECD Due Diligence Guidance for Responsible Supply Chains of Minerals from Conflict-Affected and High-Risk Areas.
For more information, see:
‘The raw materials initiative — meeting our critical needs for growth and jobs in Europe’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Conflict-affected areas: areas where armed groups or security forces are involved in armed conflict and where their revenues fuel the outbreak or continuation of violent conflict, undermining endeavours towards development, good governance and the rule of law.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    барканска бранка  граксанна глакова   (OJ L 130, 19.5.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 57:
EU customs code
EU Customs code
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 952/2013 — laying down the Union Customs Code
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
  
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 450/2008, which lays down the EU Customs Code (Modernised Customs Code).
It sets out the rules for the exchange of information between EU countries’ customs authorities and between economic operators and customs authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to goods imported into the EU from non-EU countries. It does not apply to goods originating in the EU.
EU countries must:
ensure uniform application of customs controls, including:
common risk criteria and standards, control measures and priority control areas;
identify the ports or airports where customs controls and formalities are to be carried out;
specify the technical arrangements for developing, maintaining and employing electronic systems;
conclude the rules on the conferral and proving of the entitlement for a customs representative to provide services in an EU country other than the one where he or she is established;
submit and accept an application for a decision relating to the application of the customs legislation, and on the taking and the monitoring of such a decision;
annulment, revocation and amendment of favourable decisions;
use of binding information after it ceases to be valid or is revoked;
request EU countries to revoke decisions relating to binding information;
draw up delegated acts to supplement or amend certain non-essential elements of the regulation.
Implementing powers
The European Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 TFEU of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
Delegated acts
The implementing powers conferred on the Commission are as follows:
to specify the format and code of the common data requirements for the purpose of the exchange and storage of information and the storage of such information and to adopt decisions allowing one or several countries to use means other than electronic data-processing techniques;
to define the customs authority which is responsible for the registration of economic operators, of other persons and for the development, maintenance and use of electronic systems.
The Commission must ensure that EU countries' experts and the business community are consulted in a transparent manner, and well in advance.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 10 October 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 October 2014.
BACKGROUND
EU customs law
For more information, see:
‘EU Customs Code’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Common data requirements: a set of common rules for exchanging information between the customs authorities of the EU countries and the economic operators authorised to provide customs services.

Summary 58:
Enforcement of intellectual property rights in the EU (Regulation (EU) No 608/2013)
Enforcing the intellectual property right (IPR) in order to protect consumers, manufacturers and traders.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 608 /2013 — EU customs enforcement of Intellectual Property Rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to strengthen the enforcement of the EU’s IPR by customs authorities.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1383/2003.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to goods which, in accordance with EU customs legislation, are liable to customs supervision or customs control, and to carry out adequate controls on such goods with a view to preventing operations in breach of IPR.
The goods covered by the regulation include:
trade names protected as exclusive property rights under national law;
topographies of semiconductor products and utility models and devices which are primarily designed, produced or adapted for the purpose of enabling or facilitating the circumvention of technological measures;
infringements resulting from so-called illegal parallel trade and overruns;
goods that have been manufactured with the consent of the right-holder but placed on the market without his consent;
manufacturers that have not authorised a certain quantity of goods to be manufactured and have not been duly authorised to manufacture such goods.
Implementation of the regulation
EU countries must ensure that:
customs authorities carry out appropriate controls on goods suspected of infringing the EU IPR;
the right-holders, users or groups of producers are informed of the measures to be taken against them; and
the customs authorities cooperate with each other.
In addition, EU countries must cooperate with the European Commission to ensure that the regulation is implemented effectively and effectively.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
  It has applied since 29 June 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 29 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Implementing Regulation (EU).
KEY TERMS
* Importers: any person, organisation or group of persons acting on behalf of a company or organisation.
* Expiry: the period during which the goods are intended for sale or to be sold.

Summary 60:
European Social Fund Plus (ESF+)
European social fund plus
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1057 establishing the European Social Fund+
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European social fund Plus (ESSF+)*.
It aims to support investments in people and systems in the policy areas of employment, education and social inclusion, thereby supporting economic, territorial and social cohesion in accordance with Article 174 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
KEY POINTS
 
The ESF+ contributes to the implementation of key EU initiatives and activities, in particular the European Pillar of Social Rights (Pillar) and the InvestEU programme.
It is funded by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund (EMFAF), and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD), as well as by the Cohesion Fund (InvestEU) and by the EU’s general investment programmes.
EU countries’ national multiannual investment strategies outline and coordinate priority investment projects to be supported by EU or national funding. These strategies are presented alongside the yearly national reform programmes underpinned by national strategies.
In order to ensure the full alignment of the ESF+, EU countries must take into account the relevant integrated guidelines and relevant country-specific recommendations adopted pursuant to Article 121(2) and (4) of the TFEU (European Employment and Social Fund Guidelines (see summary) adopted by the Council of the previous year. The ESFocusing on employment, training and social protection and inclusion:
EU funding is to be used in the following areas:
Equal opportunities and access to the labour market;
fair working conditions;
education and training;
social protection;
equality and inclusion;
the fight against social exclusion, poverty and discrimination;
environmental and sustainable development;
sustainable development.
ESF+ actions
The EU countries' national strategies are to identify and coordinate investment projects in the areas of:
employment;
training and education;
access to employment;
equal opportunities and equal opportunities;
equitable working conditions.
Where appropriate, EU funding may also be used for:
investments in the social protection area;
investment in the environment;
research and innovation;
employment and social innovation; and
education.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation applies from 30 June 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 June 2023.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘European Social Funds Plus’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 61:
European Solidarity Corps Programme (ESCP) — European Solidarity Volunteering Programme
European solidarity volunteering programme (ESP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/888 — establishing the European solidarity corps programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It establishes the European Solidity Corps programme (ESSCP).
It aims to promote solidarity and the visibility of humanitarian aid among EU citizens by creating opportunities for young people to participate in volunteering, traineeship or job opportunities to young people across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The ESCP was established under the European Union’s (EU's) Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) in order to develop solidarity between EU countries and with non-EU countries affected by man-made or natural disasters. The ESCP is the first phase of the ESCP. It aims to provide young people with more and better opportunities for solidarity activities covering a broad range of areas, and to support national, regional and local actors in their efforts to cope with different challenges and crises.
EU countries are invited to organise volunteering, training and job opportunities in the field of humanitarian aid. The aim is to raise awareness of the need for humanitarian aid in the EU and to encourage the participation of young people in humanitarian aid activities.
Participation in ESCP
The ESCP is open to all EU citizens, regardless of their nationality, age, gender or nationality. It is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Commission, the European Social Fund (ESFonds) and the European Fund for Development Cooperation (ESFCDAC) and is managed by the Commission.
Eligures are open to EU citizens aged 18-24 years. The duration of the programme is not less than 3 years.
Organisations participating in the ESSCP are:
the European Union Agency for the Coordination of Humanitarian Aid (EUSF),
the Commission,
the EU Development Fund (EDF) and
the Fund for Social Innovation and Entrepreneurship (FIDE).
ESSCP is organised under the EUSF programme.
The ESSP is organised in cooperation with other EU programmes, such as the EU's Development Fund, the ESDGFC, the Fund for Innovation and the EIDE, and the Commission's Directorate-General for Employment, Social Affairs and Inclusion (DGEA).
The SSP is organised by the ESCP, the Commission and the FIDE. The Commission is responsible for organising the programme and for the coordination of the activities of the participating organisations. The European Commission is also responsible for the management of the annual work programme, the annual report and the annual financial report.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 20 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Humanitarian aid: humanitarian aid is humanitarian aid that meets the needs of people in the most vulnerable situations, including women and children, and people most at risk.
* Solidarity: a sense of collective responsibility expressed through concrete action and concrete action to help those in need.

Summary 62:
European Globalisation Adjustment Fund for Displaced Workers (EGF)
European Globalization Adjustment Funds (EGG)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/691 on the European Globalisation adjustmentment fund for displaced workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s multiannual financial framework for the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that EU funds are used in the framework of sustainable development and the EU's objectives of preserving, protecting and improving the quality of the environment, taking into account the polluter-pays principle.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
EU funds for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027;
EU countries’ contributions to the EU budget;
the European Pillar of Social Rights (EPSR) and the European Social Investment Fund (ESIF);
EU Member States’ contribution to the EGF.
The EGF is the main source of funding for the ESSR and the ESIF. It is funded by the EU general budget.
It is managed by the European Investment Bank (EIB). The EFSR and EIF are the main sources of EU funds. The EIF is managed and administered by the EIB.
Eligibility for participation in the EIF
Member States must ensure that:
the EIF and the ESI are open to all EU countries;
all EU countries have the right to participate in the allocation of EIF funds;
including:
all Member States, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which are not eligible for EIF funding;
any Member State which does not participate in EIFs.
In addition, Member States must:
ensure that EIF beneficiaries are not excluded from the eligibility criteria;
apply the rules of the European Employment and Social Fund (EESF);
notify the European Commission and the Member States of the EFSF beneficiaries’ rights and obligations;
allow the Commission to draw up a list of beneficiaries;
make available to the European Parliament and the Council of Europe the list of recipients of EEF funds.

Summary 63:
Transparency register: mandatory transparency register for EU policy-making and policy implementation — EU countries’ permanent representations
Transparency platform: EU countries' permanent representatives
SUMMARY OF:
Interinstitutional Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council of the European Union and the European Commission on a mandatory Transparency register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to make the registration of interest representatives* in the EU’s public authorities, bodies, offices and agencies, as well as of any association or network of public authorities that acts on their behalf at EU, national or subnational level, a necessary precondition for carrying out certain types of interest representation activities.
The decision adopts the agreement on behalf of the EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
This agreement applies to EU institutions, bodies and bodies, other than the signatory institutions, and permanent representations that voluntarily wish to apply the operating principles of the agreement to benefit from the assistance of the secretariat of the register and its helpdesk support.
It does not apply to:
public authorities of EU countries;
any association, network or other organisation acting on the behalf of EU public authorities;
the permanent representations of EU institutions or bodies, bodies or agencies;
national public authorities.
Activities carried out exclusively for the benefit of an association or a network of associations or other organisations acting on their own behalf;
activities of any public authority acting on its behalf.
Requirements for registration
EU countries must ensure that:
interest representatives are registered in the register;
as a general rule, interest representatives are required to register in order to carry out their activities;
they must be registered in a public authority, body, office or organisation with a registered interest representative;
their activities are subject to the same conditions as those of the public authorities and bodies covered by the 2014 agreement on the transparency register.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that the register is kept up-to-date and that the information it contains is made available to the public and the public.
Registration
The register is open to all interested persons, organisations and self-employed individuals who wish to take part in the exercise of their right to interest representation.
Applicants for registration must comply with the following conditions:
the register must contain a code of conduct which contains the rules and principles to be observed by interest representatives that sign up to a transparency register (the ‘register’);
the registrants must submit an annual report on their activities to the registrar.
An annual report must be published on the register's website. The register must be maintained as a tool for the coordinated visibility of the internal organisation of the institutions and the internal management of the organisation.
Organisations and bodies that wish to participate in the implementation of this agreement must publish on their website the register conditionality and the complementary transparency measures that they put in place to encourage registration, such as dedicated mailing lists, the recommendation that certain decision-makers meet only registered interest representatives, or the publication of information on meetings between certain decision makers and interest representatives.
Member States must ensure their permanent representations are not covered by this agreement.

Summary 64:
Interinstitutional Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission on budgetary discipline, cooperation in budgetary matters and sound financial management, as well as on new own resources (OJ L 433, 22.12.2020, pp. 28-39)
Interinstitutional Agreement between European Parliament and the Council of the European Union on budgetary responsibility, cooperation on budgetary matters, and sound financing.
SUMMARY OF:
InterInstitutional Agreement (EU) 2020/2093 between the EU Parliament, Council and European Commission — budgetary discipline;
InterInterinstational Agreement (EC) 2021/2053 between the EC and European Parliament on cooperation on budget matters and on sound financing;
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020 /2053 on the introduction of new own resource (OJO L 347, 20.11.2027)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to implement budgetary discipline and to improve the functioning of the annual budgetary procedure and cooperation between the institutions.
The decision approves the agreement on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Budgetary discipline
The institutions must ensure as far as possible during the budgetary procedure that sufficient margins are left available for the various headings of the multiannual financial framework (MFF) to cover all expenditure.
Cooperation
The EU institutions must, for the purposes of sound budgetary management, ensure that sufficient margin is left available under the ceilings of the MFF to cover the repayment of the EURI recovery instrument.
EU countries must ensure that the European Adjustment Fund (EAF) mobilises the necessary resources for the implementation of budgetary commitments and budget proposals.
European Fund for Strategic Investments
The Commission must update the forecasts for payment appropriations for the period 2021-2027 at least once a year. The Commission must also consider the rules relating to the mobilisation of European Fund Funds (EIFs) and to the EU’s general budget.
In addition, the Commission must submit a proposal to the Council every year to develop the budgetary rules for the European Fund of Strategic Investments (EFFS).
The Council must take the necessary measures to ensure that appropriate budgetary rules are applied to the EFFS.
Special instruments
The European Commission must prepare a report every year on the implementation and implementation of the special instruments adopted by the EU institutions. The report must include:
a description of the specific budgetary rules and procedures applicable to the European Special Instrument (ESI);
the budgetary rules applicable to ESIs;
the financial rules applicable for ESIs and for the EU general budget;
a summary of the financial rules governing ESIs.
EURO Recovery Instrument
The EAF is a recovery instrument designed to help the EU recover from economic, social and territorial difficulties resulting from the financial crisis of 2008/09. The EAF has a budget of €2.5 billion. The European Commission is responsible for its implementation.
Own Resources Decision
The Institutions must:
ensure that the Commission adopts the own resources decision as soon as possible;
provide the Commission with all the information it needs to make a decision on the use of EU own resources;
cooperate with the national parliaments in respect of own resources.

Summary 65:
European Union Recovery Instrument to support the recovery in the aftermath of the COVID-19 crisis
European Union Relief Instrument for the Recovery of the EU’s Economic and Social Crisis
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2094 establishing a European Union recovery instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a recovery instrument for the European Union (EU).
It aims to provide financial support for the recovery of the economy and social cohesion in the EU in the wake of the crisis caused by the outbreak of the Central African Influenza V epidemic (COVID) on 11 March 2020.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2023.
It applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland and the United Kingdom (1), Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the European Economic Area (EEA), which are excluded from the regulation.
EU countries are required to provide the European Commission with a report every 3 years on the implementation of the recovery instrument.
The Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on how the recovery measures have been implemented. The report must include a description of the financial support provided under the regulation and of the measures that have been put in place.
Programmes
The recovery instruments aim to:
reform labour markets and social protection;
reinforce social inclusion;
support the transition to a climate-neutral economy;
green and digital initiatives;
invest in research and innovation;
provide financial support to small and medium-sized enterprises;
facilitate economic recovery;
contribute to the creation of new jobs and to the sustainable and resilient recovery of economic activity.
In addition, the regulation sets out the conditions under which EU countries may use the recovery instruments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMISSION ENTER INTO FORCE?
The Regulation entered into force on 14 December 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
The COVID crisis has caused significant disturbances to the EU economy, resulting in a steep decline in gross domestic product (GDP) and a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities. A sharp contraction of growth is foreseen for 2020. Many businesses are experiencing liquidity shortages, and their solvency is at risk. At the same time, consumer demand has dropped.
For more information, see:
EU recovery instruments (European Commission).

Summary 66:
Budgetary surveillance in the euro area
Budgets
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1173/2011 on the effective enforcement of budgetary surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the effectiveness of the EU’s budgetary surveillance system.
It establishes a system of sanctions for countries that fail to comply with the rules of the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP).
KEY POINTS
EU countries whose currency is the euro have a particular interest in and a responsibility to conduct economic policies that promote the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union and to avoid policies that jeopardise that functioning. To this end, the regulation strengthens the surveillance of national economic policies.
The regulation applies to all euro area countries. It does not apply to countries that are not members of the SGP.
Scope
It applies to:
budgetary surveillance missions* carried out by the European Commission;
monitoring missions carried out in EU countries;
on-site missions;
recommendations to the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (Eurogroup);
the European Systemic Risk Board (ESRB).
Sanctions
The European Commission may impose sanctions on EU countries that do not comply with its budgetary surveillance rules. The Commission may also impose sanctions if it considers that they are proportionate and proportionate to the situation of the country concerned.
EU Member States may be subject to sanctions if they have not complied with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
Member States must inform the Commission of the reasons for their non-compliance with the regulation and the results of the surveillance missions.
In addition, the Commission may carry out on-site surveillance missions in the EU countries concerned. It may also carry out surveillance missions on its own initiative.
Exemptions from sanctions
A Member State may be excluded from sanctions if:
the country concerned does not fulfil the requirements of the regulation;
it does not meet the minimum requirements for national budgetary frameworks;
its budgetary framework is not compatible with the EU strategy for growth and jobs;
the Member State concerned has not implemented the necessary macroeconomic measures to prevent and correct macroeconomic imbalances;
a Member State has not adopted the necessary budgetary measures to avoid excessive government deficits;
there is a risk of excessive government deficit.
If a Member State does not comply, it is subject to the sanctions laid down by the Regulation. The sanctions may include:
a suspension of the public procurement of goods and services;
suspension of the right of the Member State to participate in the European Semester for strengthened coordination of economic and budgetary policies;
restriction of the rights and obligations of the national authorities;
temporary or permanent suspension of public procurement;
imposition of sanctions on the Member States concerned for not complying with the budgetary surveillance requirements.
An economic dialogue with the European European Parliament may be established, enabling the Commission to make its analyses public and the President of the Council, the European Council and, where appropriate, the president of the Eurogroup to discuss.

Summary 67:
Population and housing censuses (2007-2013)
population and housing surveys (2007 - 2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 763/2008 on population and housing census
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common statistical framework for the collection and compilation of comparable and comprehensive European Union (EU) statistics on the population and the main family, social, economic and housing characteristics of persons.
It aims to ensure the comparability of the data provided by the EU countries and to draw up comparable overviews at EU level.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
The purpose of the regulation is to:
ensure that EU statistics are comparable and comparable;
provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with sufficiently reliable, detailed and comparable data on the populations and housing of the EU’s regions and the European Economic Area (EEA) to enable it to fulfil the tasks assigned to it by Articles 2 and 3 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union;
establish a common methodology for collecting and compiling EU statistics.
EU countries’ national statistical authorities must:
collect and transmit the data to Eurostat for the purpose of producing and disseminating EU statistics;
specify the methodology, definitions and the programme of the statistical data and the metadata;
identify the sources and methodologies used for the data collection;
collect the data for each year.
Eurostatistical authorities must ensure that the data are collected in accordance with the principles of impartiality, objectivity and scientific independence, as well-integratedness, transparency, reliability, relevance, cost-effectiveness and statistical confidentiality.
National and EU statistical authorities are responsible for the transmission of data subject to statistical confidentiality to the European Statistical Office (ESO).
The European Statistics Code of Practice (ESP) sets out the principles and procedures for the production and dissemination of EU statistics under this regulation. The European Statistical Programme Committee, established by Council Decision 89/382/EEC, Euratom, is responsible for ensuring the independence, integrity and accountability of the national and European statistical authorities. It is empowered to amend the regulation for the purposes of the implementing powers conferred on it by Regulation (Euratom) No 1588/90 (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 13 August 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 August 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Population and housing: the population of a given EU country and its main family characteristics.
* Households: the number of people living in a given area and their main characteristics, such as age, sex, income, education, employment, age of residence, occupation, social status and housing type.

Summary 69:
European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) rules of procedure — Rules of Procedure of the European Union institution, bodies, offices and agencies (GDPR) and the European Data Protection Board (EDPB)
European data protection supervisor (ESP) rules and procedures — Rules and principles of procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data by EU institutions, bodies and offices
Decision of 15 May 2020 adopting the rules of procedures of the EDPS
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
   
The regulation establishes an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of EU citizens, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to processing of their personal data, are respected by the EU institutions and bodies.
The decision adopts the rules governing the procedures for the appointment and functioning of the EDPB’s secretariat.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EDPS is responsible for:
ensuring that:
the protection of individuals’ rights and fundamental freedoms of the EU institution, body, office or agency;
the free movement of their data; and
good governance and good governance in the field of data protection.
It is assisted by a secretariat, which is under the control of an independent supervisory authority appointed by the European Parliament and the Council.
Duties and powers
The EU institution or body responsible for the implementation of the regulation and the decision-making process must:
appoint an EDPS supervisory body;
ensure that the European data Protection Supervisor is independent and independent;
act in accordance with the rules and powers conferred on it by the regulation;
follow the strategic priorities set out in its annual report;
take all necessary measures to ensure that the rules relating to personal data protection are complied with;
cooperate with other EU bodies, bodies or offices that are subject to the regulation or any other relevant EU legal act;
operate in the area of information security and privacy.
Election of the Supervisory body
The Supervisory board appoints a European data protection supervisory board (EDPB) which:
is composed of representatives of EU institutions or bodies;
is chaired by an independent supervisor;
has the power to:
make recommendations to the EDPB;
make decisions on staffing and budgetary matters;
provide advice and guidance to the supervisory supervisory bodies of EU bodies and to the European Commission;
prepare and implement annual reports and annual reports;
publish an annual report on its activities and its strategic priorities.
Guiding principles
Good governance and transparency are the guiding principles adopted by the EDDPB. The EDPS must be a proactive and authoritative body, as well as an effective and transparent body. It must engage with the public and the media in order to explain its actions and its activities, and engage in transparent and effective dialogue with stakeholders.
FROM WHEN DO THESE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulations have applied since 23 October 2018. The decision has applied since 26 May 2020 and had to become law in the EU countries by 26 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* EU institution: any EU institution*, body or office, body or agency subject to Regulation (EC) No 45/2001 and Decision No 1247/2002/EC.
* European data privacy board: the body which ensures that the rights and rights of individuals, including the right to privacy and the right of access to information, to their data, and to their information and information security are respected in the context of processing their data.

Summary 70:
System of own resources of the European Union (SUEU)
System of its own resources
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2020/2053 on the system of EU own resources and repealing Decision 2014/335/EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out the EU’s own resources (i.e. the revenue that EU countries contribute to the EU budget) for the period 2021-2027.
It aims to better align these with EU policy priorities, to better reflect the role of the general budget of the EU (‘the Union budget’) in the functioning of the single market, to support the objectives of EU policies and to reduce Member States’ contributions based on gross national income (GNI).
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out:
a new category of own resource based on national contributions calculated on the quantity of non-recycled plastic packaging waste that is not recycled in each EU country;
a mechanism to adjust the contributions of Member States with a GNI per capita below the EU average.
The new own resource will be calculated on a weighted average of the national contributions of the Member States concerned. It will be based on:
the quantity of plastic packaging that does not recycle in each Member State, multiplied by the population of that Member State;
the percentage of the total volume of plastic waste not recycled.
Member States will be free to take the most suitable measures to achieve those goals, in line with the principle of subsidiarity.
An adjustment mechanism with an annual lump sum reduction will be applied to contributions of EU countries with a GDP per capita in 2017 below the European average. The reduction will correspond to 3,8 kilograms multiplied by 3.8 kilograms. Member States will not be able to reduce their contributions more than 5 % of the GNI of their Member States. The adjustment mechanism will be in place for a period of 5 years. The Commission will put forward proposals for additional own resources in the light of the developments in the field of climate change.
In addition, the European Commission will submit a proposal to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of this decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
Council Decision (EU) 2020 /2053 has applied since 15 December 2020.
Amending Decision (Euratom) 2021/2027 has applied from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU budget is the main source of revenue for the EU. However, it is subject to strict budgetary discipline. To ensure adequate resources for the development of the policies of the Union, the EU must ensure that its policies are compatible with its policy priorities. This is why the European Council of 17 to 21 July 2020 concluded that over the coming years the Union would work towards reforming the system in order to introduce new own resources.
For more information, see:
‘The European Union’ (European Commission).

Summary 71:
Labelling of tyres with respect to fuel efficiency and other parameters (Regulation (EU) 2020/740 - fuel efficiency, safety and noise)
Labelling and safety of tyres
Labeling and safety information on tyres
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It aims to encourage end-users to purchase more fuel-efficient tyres by providing them with harmonised information on the rolling resistance* and noise* parameters of tyres.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
new tyres;
new types of tyres (C1 tyres, C2 tyres and C3 tyres);
new type of tyres designed specifically for the European Union (EU).
Tyres are characterised by a number of interrelated parameters. Improving one parameter, such as rolling resistance, may have an adverse impact on other parameters such as wet grip, while improving wet grip performance may have a negative impact on external rolling noise. To reduce the environmental impact of road transport, it is therefore appropriate to update the rules on tyre labelling to provide consumers with more relevant and comparable information on these parameters.
The European Commission has adopted a proposal to encourage tyre manufacturers to optimise all parameters beyond the current standards.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   ъбъкаранкакабарка хракка
It applies from 25 May 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
калканъраххкацкаска браганакца  
For more information, see:
‘Fuel efficiency’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
Fuel efficiency: the energy losses that are due to tyre rolling resistance.
External rolling noise: the noise emitted by a tyre when it is being driven on the road.
Wet grip: the effect of wetness on road traffic noise.

Regulation(EU) 2018/852 amends the regulation to harmonise the information on wet grip characteristics for tyres and to improve the awareness of road users of wet-riding tyres. It also lays down the minimum wet grip requirements for tyres with a wet grip rating of between 0.5 and 0.8 m/s.

Summary 72:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/1077 establishing the Integrated Border Management Fund (IBMF) as part of the EU’s financial support for customs control equipment. The aim is to improve equivalence in the performance of customs controls across the EU and thereby prevent the diversion of flows of goods towards the weakest points in the customs control system, often referred to as ‘import point shopping’. The IBMF provides financial support to EU countries in order to improve the availability, and the proper functioning, of modern and reliable customs control systems.
ACT
Regulation (EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes the IBMF, which is an instrument of the European Union (EU), as a financial support instrument for the implementation of the Customs Union Customs Code (CUSC) and other customs legislation.
The IBMF is designed to ensure that the 2 140 customs offices situated at the external borders of the Union need to be properly equipped to ensure the efficient and effective operation of the customs union.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
customs offices are equipped with:
equipment capable of performing the duties of customs authorities;
equivalence of the equipment with respect to the duties and taxes levied by the EU on the external aspects of the internal market;
the protection of supply chain security and the protection of the economic interests of EU countries;
adequate coordination and cooperation between customs authorities and other law enforcement authorities.
To achieve these objectives, EU countries must:
ensure that customs offices have the necessary equipment;
provide each other with the necessary information and training;
assist each other in assessing the technical and operational needs of their respective customs offices;
establish and maintain a network of liaison offices between the national customs offices and other relevant bodies;
organise the exchange of information and best practice in the field of customs control;
implement the necessary training for customs officers and customs officials;
exchange best practice on the use of technical equipment with other EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2022.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU is one of the largest trading blocks in the world. The need for adequate customs controls with equivalent results is ever more pressing, not only because of the traditional function of customs which is to collect revenue, but also increasingly because of developments in e-commerce and the need to significantly reinforce the control of goods entering and exiting the EU.
For more information, see:
External border management fund (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs control: the enforcement of customs legislation and the presence and movement within the EU of non-EU goods and goods placed under the end-use procedure.
* Importation point: the point where goods are brought into the EU for the purpose of being processed, inspected or transported.

Summary 73:
Common rules for the EU’s internal market for electricity (Directive (EU) 2019/944) (REFERENCES)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to create a competitive electricity market in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
the wholesale electricity market;
the retail electricity market.
It does not apply to electricity transmission systems.
EU countries must:
ensure that wholesale electricity markets are open to all market participants, including producers of renewable energy, new energy service providers, energy storage and flexible demand*;
foster the integration of their national electricity markets and cooperation among system operators at EU and regional level;
encourage the deployment of distributed renewable energy* and energy storage* solutions;
promote the development of innovative renewable energy generation and distribution solutions, as well as energy efficiency solutions.
Regulatory framework
The European Commission has adopted a regulatory framework for the electricity market, based on the principles set out in the communication ‘A Resilient Energy Union with a Forward-Looking Climate Change Policy’. It aims to ensure that:
all market participants participate in the energy transition;
all consumers benefit from new technologies to reduce their energy bills and participate actively in the market; and
vulnerable consumers are protected.
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (1), which are excluded from the scope of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 14 June 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:

Summary 74:
European High Performance Computing Joint Undertaking (EPCJoint undertaking)
European High-performance computing Joint undertaking (ECJU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1173 on establishing the European High-Performing Computing Joint undertaking
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EPCJU under the framework of Horizon Europe, the European Union’s framework programme for research and innovation (Horizon Europe).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EPCU is a joint undertaking established under the Horizon Europe Framework Programme for Research and Innovation. It is responsible for the acquisition, deployment and operation of a world-class supercomputing, quantum and data infrastructure, at federation and interconnection, and for the development of key skills.
Objectives
The purpose of the regulation is to:
support the creation of European public common data spaces and federated, secure cloud infrastructures for public, industrial and scientific applications;
enhance the use of supercomputers and quantum computing technologies;
expand Europe's supercomputer capacity to develop innovative solutions for medicine, transport and the environment;
promote industrial and digital leadership in industrial and industrial computing technologies, including in the next generation of industrial computing;
support research and development in the field of high performance computing (HPC) and the development in HPC;
contribute to the development and deployment of new infrastructure for the trans-European networks policy in the fields of transport, telecommunications and energy.
The regulation repeals Regulation (EU-wide) 2018/1488 of the European Parliament and Council of Europe on the European Partnerships Regulation.
EPCUJU is governed by Regulation (EC) 2019/869/2013 on the establishment of the ECJU, which sets out the conditions under which EU funding may be provided to Joint Undertakings established under Horizon Europe.
It is composed of the following members:
the European Commission,
the Member States and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC);
the Commission and the Member States’ competent authorities.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission is implementing Horizon Europe – the Framework programme for Research & Innovation – the framework for scientific and technological innovation – through partnerships with private and/or public sector partners, in order to achieve the greatest possible impact of EU funding and to make the most effective contribution to the Union's policy objectives.
For more information, see:
European Partnerships – Horizon Europe (European Commission).

Summary 75:
Climate neutrality — EU rules
Climate neutrality – EU rules (EU rules)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1119 establishing the framework for achieving climate neutrality
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s legal framework for the transition to a climate-neutral economy.
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions to no net emissions of GHG by 2050.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Commission.
EU countries must:
ensure that GHG emissions are reduced to zero by 2050, i.e. no net GHG emission in the EU by 2050;
take the necessary measures to prevent GHG from being released into the atmosphere and to minimise the impact of climate change on human health, the environment and the economy;
implement the EU climate action plan for 2020-2030 (see summary), which sets out a number of measures to tackle climate change, including:
the EU climate and health observatory;
the European Climate Adaptation Platform Climate-ADAPT;
measures to adapt the EU to climate change and to the impacts of extreme weather events;
environmental measures to protect, conserve and enhance the EU's natural capital;
actions to protect the health and well-being of citizens from environment-related risks and impacts.
In addition, EU countries are required to:
take action to reduce GHG greenhouse gas emissions to a minimum by 2020;
by 2020, the EU countries must have reduced GHGigaton emissions by at least 60 % from 1990 levels;
at least 80 % by 2030
by 2050, at least 80% by 80%;
by 2030, by 80% for all sectors of the economy
by 80 % for all industries
by 2035 for all other sectors
by 3035 for industrial activities
by 40% for the transport sector
By 2035, by 40 % for the energy sector
by 60 % for agriculture
by 50% for forestry
by 70% for fisheries
by 75% for agriculture and forestry
By 2050, by 60 % in all other fields
by 90 % for transport
by 100 % for energy
by 85% for other sectors.
By 2030, the regulation also requires EU countries to take the necessary steps to ensure that:
GHG emissions by 2050 are no more than 0.5 % of the total GHG emitted by the EU in 2050
by the end of the 2030s.
The European Commission has adopted a climate action strategy for the EU, which aims to transform the EU into a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy, where there are no net CO2 emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use. The strategy is based on the EU Green Deal, which is part of the EU strategy on climate change. The European Green Deal also aims to protect and conserve the Union's natural assets, including its biodiversity and ecosystem services, and to protect citizens from environmental-related threats and impacts, including the emergence and spread of infectious diseases.
For more information, see:
European Green Deal (European Commission)
European Climate Action Plan (European Agency for Climate Action).

Summary 76:
EU4Health Programme 2021-2027: EU action in the field of health (EU4H programme)
EU 4H programme 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/522 establishing a programme for the EU’s action in health (‘EU4health programme’)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a new and reinforced programme for EU action to combat the spread of severe human infections and diseases across borders in order to combat other serious cross-border threats to health and to safeguard and improve the health and well-being of all people in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The programme covers the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
It is designed to complement and support national health policies, encourage cooperation between Member States and promote the coordination between their programmes, in full respect of the responsibilities of Member States for the definition of their health policies and for the organisation, management and delivery of health services and medical care.
EU action
The EU4Health programme aims to improve preparedness for, and the prevention and control of, serious human infections, diseases and parasites across borders. It also seeks to improve the effectiveness of the EU health system and to reduce the impact of these threats on the health of all citizens in the European Union (EU).
It aims to:
ensure that Member States are prepared for and respond to future threats to public health;
promote cooperation and coordination among Member States;
increase the efficiency and effectiveness of national health systems;
improve the effectiveness in the exchange of best practice;
strengthen the capacity of the health system;
enhance the effectiveness and efficiency of the European health system.
Cooperation and coordination
Member States are invited to cooperate and share best practice in the areas covered by the programme.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the programme and the coordination of the actions under the programme with the European Parliament and the Council. The Commission is assisted by the Committee of the Regions, the European Economic and Social Committee and the European Monitoring Centre for the Health System (EMCS).
EU actions
In order to achieve its objectives, the programme is structured as follows:
coordination of actions;
exchange of experience and best practices;
the sharing of best practices and information;
cooperation between the Member States in the prevention, control and management of serious human infection and disease threats.
In line with the goals of the Programme and the Union's competences in the area of public health, the Programme should emphasise actions in relation to the areas of collaboration and cooperation at national level that have an impact at EU level and where there is a high potential for efficiency gains. These actions should include:
promoting the sharing of experience, best practice and best practice,
improving the efficiency of national systems and networks;
encouraging the use of the best practice of the national authorities;
supporting the development of innovative solutions to prevent and control severe human infection threats;
developing the capacity to respond to health threats in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic;
ensuring that the EU is prepared to cope with future threats and to improve its resilience to future risks.
Implementation
The Programme is implemented by the European Commission and the EU Member States. It is implemented in line with Article 168 TFEU (see summary) of the Treaty on European Union.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 February 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The COVID outbreak was declared a global pandemic on 11 March 2020. It led to severe socio-economic consequences and human suffering, which particularly affect people with chronic conditions.
For more information, see:
European response to COVID (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cross-border threat to health: a threat that occurs when a contagious disease spreads from one Member State to another.
* Transnational threat: an infectious disease that is spread by means of contact with infected persons, goods or services.

Aromatised wine — definition, description, presentation, labelling and protection of geographical indications of aromatised products (Regulation (EU) No 251/2014)
Aroma-scented wine — definitions, presentation and labelling
Aromated wine — descriptions, presentation & labelling.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules applicable to the production and presentation of aromated wine products.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1601/91.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all aromated wines, aromatized wine-based drinks and aromate-product cocktails produced in the EU, whether produced in EU countries or in non-EU countries. It does not apply to products produced for export.
The products covered by the regulation are:
wines,
wine-based products,
appliances,
ciders,
drinks and
cocktails.
Labelling
Products must comply with the labelling rules laid down in the regulation. The labelling must state:
the name of the producer;
the production methods used;
a description of the products produced; and
the geographical indication of the product.
EU countries’ national authorities must ensure that:
all products are produced in accordance with the production methods laid down by the European Commission;
there are no restrictions on the production, description or presentation of the aromatisation of products;
technological innovation is taken into account in respect of products for which such innovation serves to improve quality, without affecting the traditional production methods;
products are produced according to the standards established by the International Wine Organisation (WOJ).
The European Commission must publish a list of the methods used in the production of products subject to the regulation, including:
methods used for aromatising wines,
the methods used for the presentation of wine products and
labelling;
rules on flavouring and addition of alcohol to aromated products, and
rules for the protection of the geographical indication.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLACE APPLY?
Regulation(EU) 2018/1169 amends Regulation (EU).
It applies from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules to ensure that the quality of wine produced in its internal market and on the world market is maintained and that consumers can be informed of the quality and quantity of products they are buying.
For more information, see:
‘Aromatic wine products’ on the EU’s website.

* Aromatisation: the addition of aromatics to wine to enhance its flavour and aromatise it.

Summary 80:
Fishing opportunities for certain fish stocks and groups of fish stocks, applicable in EU waters and, for EU fishing vessels, in certain non-Union waters
Fishing rights for certain species
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/123 fixing for 2020 the fishing opportunities for fish stocks*
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the total allowable catch (TAC) for certain fishing stocks* in EU and non-EU waters for the period from 1 January 2019 to 31 December 2023.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
EU fishing vessels fishing in the waters of the European Union (EU), i.e. the exclusive economic zone (EEZ) and the Baltic Sea;
non-EEZ fishing vessels operating in the EEZ and Baltic Sea.
It does not apply to the fishing rights granted to EU fishing companies.
The TAC is based on available scientific advice, taking into account biological and socio-economic aspects whilst ensuring fair treatment between fishing sectors, as well as in the light of the opinions expressed during the consultation of stakeholders, in particular at the meetings of the advisory councils.
EU countries must ensure that:
the TACs are set in line with the objectives of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) established in Article 2(2) of that regulation;
fishing opportunities are allocated to EU countries in such a way as to ensure relative stability of fishing activities of each EU country for each fish stock or fishery;
the fishing opportunities are based on the advice provided by the International Council for the Exploration of the Sea (ICES).
The EU countries must:
ensure that the TAC for each species is set at the level indicated in the ICES’s scientific advice;
take the necessary measures to avoid the occurrence of ‘choke species’;
not discard any catches during the operation of the landing obligation;
make use of the opportunities set out in the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of delegated regulations to implement the EU landing obligation.
For more information, see:
‘Fishing Opportunities’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Choke species: a fish stock which is overfished and over-exploited and which is unable to recover.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 1380/2013 of 27 January 2013 on the fixing and allocation of fishing opportunities (OJ L 25, 30.1.2013, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS

).

Summary 82:
Recovery plan for Mediterranean swordfish (ICCATCAT recovery plan) (2017-2031)
Recovery Plan for Mediterranean Swordfish (ICTCAT)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1154 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 June 2019 on a multiannual recovery plan for European swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union’s common fisheries policy (CFP) is managed in accordance with the objectives of the ICCAT Convention, the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas.
It covers the period 2017–2031.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
European swordfish fisheries (Xiphias gladius) in the Mediterranean Sea (‘Mediterranean swordfish’), which has been overfished over the last 30 years.
EU countries must ensure that:
all fishing gear* and fishing techniques* used in the fishery are consistent with the conservation principles of ICCAT;
fishing gear sizes and techniques are used according to the minimum standards set out in ICCAT Recommendation 16-05 on the conservation of Atlantic tunas;
the European Commission adopts a recovery plan every 2 years;
all EU countries are required to implement the recovery plan by 31 December 2021.
The European Commission publishes a report on the implementation of the regulation every 3 years on the basis of the results of the monitoring carried out by the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULUMENT APPLYEARLY ENTRY INTO FORCEASE?
   
It entered into force on 12 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to ICCAT.

* ICCAT: the International Convention for the conservation and management of Atlantic Tropical Tunas, adopted by ICCAT in Vilamoura, Portugal, in 2016.

Management, conservation and control measures applicable in the Inter-American Tropical Tuna Convention area
Mining, fishing and fishing management in the inter-american tropical tuna convention area (IATTC)
Management, fishing, hunting and fishing controls in the IATTC area
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/56 laying down management, conservation* and control* measures applicable to the tuna purse seine fishery area of the Inter‐American Tropical Fisheries Convention
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the management and control of the fishing and hunting of tuna in the area covered by the inter‐americana tropical tuna convention (ITC) established by the 1949 Convention between the United States of America and the Republic of Costa Rica.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following areas:
the technical specifications for shark lines, closure periods, fish-aggregating devices (FADs), reporting timelines, design and deployment of FADs, data collection and scientific observer coverage;
scientific observer coverage of tuna seine fishing areas;
the rules for unloading and unloading bigeye tuna and turtle mortality records;
rules for the preparation and preparation of scientific information on bigeye mortality records.
It does not apply to:
fishing vessels;
unloading of tuna;
shark lines;
fisheries;
turtle mortality records and data collection;
fish-aggregation devices;
marine mortality records*;
seine mortality data;
data collection.
EU countries’ obligations
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
The EU countries must ensure that:
all fishing vessels are registered with the European Commission;
all EU countries and the European Economic and Social Committee of the European Parliament and of the Council of Europe have the same access to the Commission’s secretariat; and
the Commission prepares a report every 2 years on compliance with the regulation and the measures taken by EU countries with regard to the convention.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
  
It entered into force on 1 January 2021. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2023.
BACKGROUND
The treaty on the law of the sea (Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU) gives the EU the power to adopt decisions (‘Resolutions’) to ensure the long-term conservation and sustainable use of the fishery resources in the Convention area. These resolutions are binding for the contracting parties.
For more information, see:
Inter‐america Tropical tuna convention (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Tuna: a species of fish caught by purse seines (e.g. in the Atlantic Ocean).
* Shark line: a line used to catch sharks.
* FAD: a device used to gather fish from the bottom of the water and to separate them from the rest of the fish.

Summary 86:
Better Law-Making: Interinstitutional Agreement between the European Parliament, the Council and the European Commission
Better Law - Making: InterInstitutional Agreement among the European Union institutions
SUMMARY OF:
INTERINSTITUTIONAL AGREEMENT BETWEEN THE EUROPEAN PARLIAMENT, THE COUNCIL OF THE EU AND THE European Commission on better law-making
Decision (EU) 2017/852 on the conclusion, on behalf of the EU, of the Interinstitutional Agreement between EU institutions on Better Law-making (OJ L 123, 12.5.2016, pp. 1-8)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREATION AND THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to simplify EU legislation and reduce the regulatory burden for citizens, administrations and businesses, especially small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), and to strengthen the competitiveness and sustainability of the Union economy.
The decision marks the conclusion of the interinstitutional agreement.
KEY POINTS
The three institutions recognise their joint responsibility in delivering high-quality Union legislation and in ensuring that such legislation focuses on areas where it has the greatest added value for European citizens, is as simple and as clear as possible, avoids overregulation and administrative burdens for citizens and administrations, especially SMEs, and is transposition and practical application.
In order to achieve this objective, the three institutions agree to pursue the aim of simplifying EU legislation, while respecting the principle of subsidiarity and proportionality. They also agree to promote transparency in the legislative process and to ensure that EU legislation is comprehensible and comprehensible for the public and the public administrations.
EU legislation must be drafted in a way that is consistent with the EU’s general principles of transparency and fairness. It must be written in a language that is easily understood by the public, the public authorities and the EU institutions. The European Commission must gather all necessary expertise prior to adopting delegated acts. The Commission must also ensure that the analysis of the potential ‘European added value’ of any proposed EU action, as well as an assessment of the ‘cost of non-Europe’ in the absence of action at EU level, is fully taken into account when setting the legislative agenda.
National parliaments are responsible for the drafting of EU legislation. In this context, they recall the equality of both co-legislators as enshrined in the Treaties.
Public and stakeholder consultation, ex-post evaluation of existing legislation and impact assessments of new initiatives will help achieve the objective of better law making.
Implementation of the agreement
The 3 institutions undertake to cooperate in order to promote the objectives of the better-law-making agreement. They agree to:
ensure that the legislation is written in such a way as to be comprehensible to the public;
promote transparency and the rule of law in the EU legislative process;
encourage the use of the recasting technique for legal acts;
allow the public to participate in the decision-making process.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
    накабарска банка  ганарка, городна года  ханца каника.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Interinstitutional Agreement between the Europarliament and the Council of the Eurozone, and of the European Atomic Energy Community, of 13 April 2016 on BetterLaw-Making.
Council Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/856 of 13 May 2018 on the signature, on the behalf of Member States, and provisional application, of a Joint Declaration on the Implementation of the Agreement on Better-Law-making between the Union institutions, the European Central Bank and the Commission.

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiative
Citizens' initiative
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 211/2011 on the citizens' initiative — procedures and conditions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures, rules and conditions for the registration of citizens’ initiatives* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to encourage participation by citizens and to make the EU more accessible.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all citizens who wish to participate in the democratic life of the EU by way of a European citizens´ initiative.
It does not apply to organisations or bodies which contribute to forming European political awareness and to expressing the will of citizens of the Union, provided that they do so with full transparency.
Organisers must:
register their initiative with the European Commission;
register it on the Commission’s website;
organise it in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
The Commission must, upon request, provide citizens with information and informal advice about citizens' initiatives, notably as regards the registration criteria.
EU countries must ensure that citizens from their territory are subject to similar conditions for supporting an initiative regardless of the Member State from which they come.
Registration of initiatives
Organisations must register their initiatives with the Commission. They must also register their initiative on the website of the European Parliament, the European Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC).
The initiative must be submitted to the Commission within 3 months of receiving a request for a proposal for a legal act. The Commission must inform the committee of the reasons for rejecting the proposal.
Before the Commission receives a proposal, the committee must register the initiative on its website.
If the Commission considers that an initiative does not comply with the regulation, it must notify the committee and the committee, in writing, of its intention to withdraw the initiative. If the committee decides that the initiative is still valid, it may withdraw it.
In addition, the Commission must provide the committee with a report on the implementation of the regulation every 3 years. The report must contain information on the number of signatures collected by the initiative and on the organisation of the initiative, as well as on the structure of the committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 11 March 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 11 March 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
Citizen's initiative
The European Commission has the right to invite citizens to submit a proposal to implement the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the EU Parliament under Article 225 TFEU.
For more information, see:
‘Citizens initiative’ on the European Agency for Fundamental Rights’'s website
European Citizens’ Initiative (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Citizens' initiative: an initiative designed to promote the EU's democratic life by means of a citizens' committee. It aims to promote European-wide issues and to foster reflection on those issues.
* Organiser: a natural person (organiser) coming from at least 7 EU countries.

Summary 88:
Statistics on pesticide sales and use
Statistics on pesticides
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1185/2009 — statistics on pesticides (Official Journal L 324 of 10.12.2009)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It requires EU countries to collect and transmit to the European Commission statistics on sales* and use* of pesticides. These statistics are essential for assessing policies of the European Union on sustainable development and for calculating relevant indicators on the risks for health and the environment related to pesticide use.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to pesticides which are plant protection products covered by Regulation (EU) No 1107/2009. It does not apply to biocidal products.
EU countries must ensure that the statistics collected are accurate and up-to-date, and that they comply with the statistical rules laid down in the regulation.
They must transmit the data to the Commission within 3 months of placing on the market and 3 months after the end of use of the pesticide.
The data must be collected in accordance with the following rules:
conformity, objectivity, reliability, confidentiality and confidentiality;
transmission of confidential data;
availability and dissemination of the data.
Reporting
The Commission must submit a report every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation and on the quality of the statistical data it has collected. The report must contain information on:
pesticides placed on the EU market;
the number of pesticides used in the EU;
use of pesticides in EU countries; and
the results of the impact assessment of the Thematic Strategy on the Sustainable Use of Pesticides.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report on the use of pesticides by EU countries in the field of agriculture and forestry. It must also report on pesticide use in the fields of forestry and aquaculture.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 10 December 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Pesticides — EU statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Pesticide: any substance used to protect crops against pests or diseases.
* Biocidal product: a substance that is used to kill plants, animals or plants’ natural resources. It is also used as a pesticide to kill insects, molluscs and other pests. It may also be used as an insect repellent.

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — placing on the market and repealing Council Directives 79/117/EEC and 91/414/EC (Official Journal L 309 of 24.11.2009)
Planting on the EU market: plant protection products
Plants protecting plants against harmful organisms, including weeds, and improving agricultural production are the most important way of protecting plants and plant products against the harmful effects of these organisms. Plants protection products may also have non-beneficial effects on plant production.
ACT
Regulation (EC) No 1107/2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 October 2009 concerning the placing of plant protection substances on the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down harmonised rules for the approval of active substances* and the placing on market of plants protection products.
It repeals Directive 79/118/EC, which had been repealed in 1978.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
plant protection products* and their active substances;
products intended for use in the food and feed sectors;
plants intended for human or animal health and for the environment.
The substances covered by the regulation are:
active substances* (e.g. pesticides, herbicides, fungicides, insecticides, plant protection agents, etc.).
It does not apply to products intended for the production of food or feedstuffs, animal feed, cosmetics or cosmetics.
EU countries must ensure that active substances are only included in products if they present a clear benefit for plant production and they are not expected to have any harmful effects on human, animal or environmental health or any unacceptable effects on the environment, or if they have been officially tested and authorised and if incorrectly used.
Authorisations
The European Commission must submit a proposal to the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) for an authorisation of an active substance to be used in the EU. The proposal must contain the following information:
the name of the active substance;
the substance’s active substance(s);
the number and type of active substance.
If the EFSA considers that the substance meets all of the above conditions, it may authorise the use of the substance. If it considers that a substance does not meet all the conditions for authorisation, the Commission may refuse to authorise it.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
The regulations apply from 24 November 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
Plant Protection Products (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Active substance: a substance whose main purpose is to protect plants against the effects of harmful organisms (weeds, insects, molluscs, etc.) and to improve the competitiveness of EU agriculture.
* Plant protection product: a plant protection product that protects plants against certain harmful organisms and plants against their harmful effects. It may also be used to protect plant production against the adverse effects of certain other substances.

  Еганска банка  граксанна года   бъларка, городна бакца каккка (OJ L 309, 24.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
