Summary 90:
Administrative cooperation through the Internal Market Information System (IMI)
Administrative coordination through IMI
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2012 on administrative cooperation through EU acts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal framework for the use of the internal market information system, IMI, to facilitate administrative cooperation between EU countries and between the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council.
It repeals Decision 2008/49/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU acts governing the free movement of goods, persons, services and capital in the EU. It does not apply to:
the Schengen acquis;
the European Pillar of Social Rights (see summary);
the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU); or
the Single Market Act.
Implementation
Implementing the regulation requires EU countries to exchange information with one another and with the Commission on the application of the EU acts listed in the annex to the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the information exchanged in IMI complies with the security and confidentiality requirements laid down in Article 8 and 52 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the Union.
The information exchanged must be collected, processed and used for purposes in line with those for which it was originally collected and subject to all relevant safeguards.
IMI is a software application accessible via the internet, developed by the Commission in cooperation with the EU countries. It is used primarily for the exchange of information, including personal data, which would otherwise take place via other means, including regular mail, fax or electronic mail, on the basis of a legal obligation imposed on EU countries’ authorities and bodies in EU acts.
In order to overcome language barriers, the Commission must provide IMI users with any existing additional translation functionality that meets their needs, is compatible with the confidentiality and security requirements and can be offered at a reasonable cost.
Exchange of personal data
Personal data may only be exchanged via IMI for the purpose of processing EU acts, subject to the conditions set out in Articles 4 and 5 of the TFEU.
Information exchanged via the IMI must comply with the protection rules laid down by the European Data Protection Regulation (EDPPR).
The European Commission must:
ensure that personal data are only exchanged for the purposes for which they were originally collected;
not for any other purpose other than the purpose for which the personal data were collected and processed;
provide IMI operators with the information they need for the processing of EU acts;
make IMI available in all official EU languages, including those of the Member States and the Commission.
Where an EU act requires the Member State to exchange personal data for processing purposes, it must provide the Commission with the necessary information for this purpose. The Commission must also provide the user with the additional information it needs for IMI to be able to use the information it receives.
Access to IMI data
The Member States must allow the Commission to access IMI personal data only if it is necessary for the proper processing of a Union act. This information must be provided in a form that is easily understood and understandable by the user.
Member States must inform the Commission of any data protection restrictions imposed on IMI or on other means of communication, such as electronic mail or fax.
If the Commission considers that a Member State has not complied with the requirements of the regulation, it may suspend or restrict the access of IMI user data for a period of time.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
    баканска бранка  гаксанна  
It has applied since 14 November 2012 and had to become law in all Member States by 14 November 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
The IMI software application is a centralised communication mechanism designed to facilitate cross-border exchange of administrative information and mutual assistance. It helps competent authorities to identify their counterpart in another Member State, to manage the exchange and exchange information, i.e. personal data.
For more information, see:
External market information systems (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Бакналкаганшка:
Internal Market Information Systems (Imi) (European Agency for Safety and Health at European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Agencies).
last update 14.11.2016



Summary 91:
Fisheries in the Mediterranean and the Black Sea: rules on fishing in the GFCM (General Fisheries Commission for the Mediterranean) Agreement area
Fishers in the European Union (EU) must comply with the rules laid down by the General Fisheries Commission (GFCM) for the sustainable exploitation of fishery resources in the EU’s Mediterranean and Black Sea.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1343/2011 on certain provisions for fishing in EU waters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the fishing of fish and crustaceans in the waters of the Mediterranean, Black Sea, the Gulf of Lions, the Mediterranean Sea and connecting waters.
It aims to ensure that fishing is sustainable and at low risk of collapse.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fisheries restricted areas* (i.e. areas where fishing gear cannot be used beyond a certain level of exploitation;
mixed fisheries** where fishing effort is not sufficient to maintain the overall level of sustainable fishing effort;
fishing gears of important species (e.g. adults) which cannot be exploited beyond a minimum level of effort.
EU Member States are contracting parties to the agreement.
Fishery restricted areas
Member States must:
ensure that:
the fishing effort of the fishing gear is not reduced to a level which is not relevant to the overall management of the fishery;
that the fishing effort does not exceed the level of economic activity;
keep a record of the total number of fishing gears used;
monitor the fishing gears and the number of fish caught.
The Member States must also ensure that the management measures are in line with the scientific advice of the Scientific Advisory Committee (SAC).
The SAC is responsible for:
monitoring the management of fisheries;
assisting Member States in the implementation of the regulation;
providing scientific advice to the European Commission;
ensuring that Member States implement the regulation.
Monitoring and monitoring of fishing gear and fishing effort
The European Commission must ensure that: each Member State:
keeps a register of all fishing gears that are used in the area covered by the regulation,
ensures that fishing gears are used only in the areas covered by Regulation (EC),
reports to the SAC every 3 years on the management and management measures taken by the Member States;
regularly consults the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) and the Scientific Committee of the European Parliament and of the Council on the implementation and application of the Regulation;
conducts scientific and technical studies to assess the situation and the impact of the measures taken;
publishes a report on the results of these studies every 5 years;
is assisted by the European Fisheries and Aquaculture Agency (EFAA) and by the Scientific and Technical Committee (STC);
is responsible for the implementation by Member States and the Commission of the annual Regulations on fishing opportunities and fishing quotas, as well as for the annual Regulation on fishing quotas.
In addition, Member States have to submit a report to the Commission on the measures they have taken to implement the Regulation. The Commission then forwards the report to other Member States, which then forwards it to the EU Parliament and to the Council.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The General Fisheries and Coast Guard Commission (GFCC) is a multilateral body set up by the 1998 Agreement for the establishment of the General Mediterranean Fisheries Commission. It is composed of representatives of the contracting parties, Bulgaria, Greece, Spain, France, Italy, Cyprus, Malta, Romania and Slovenia.
For more information, see:
General fisheries and coast guard commission (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    The General Fisheries Council Regulation (Euratom) of 16 June 1998 on the accession of the EU to the General fisheries Commission for Mediterranean (GMC) Agreement (OJ L 347, 30.12.1998, pp. 1-8)

Summary 92:
EU-wide unitary patent protection (until 31 December 2023)
EU - country-by-country uniform patent protection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1257/2012 implementing enhanced cooperation in the area of the creation of unitary patents
Decision 2011/167/EU authorising enhanced cooperation between Belgium, Bulgaria, the Czech Republic, Denmark, Germany, Estonia, Ireland, Greece, France, Cyprus, Latvia, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Hungary, Malta, the Netherlands, Austria, Poland, Portugal, Romania, Slovenia, Slovakia, Finland, Sweden and the United Kingdom
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The regulation aims to ensure that European patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) have a unitary character.
The decision authorises the EPO to grant European patents with unitary effect in EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
patents granted in the EU have a uniform protection in all EU countries, regardless of their nationality, residence or place of establishment;
the EPO may grant a patent to a patent applicant from outside the EU.
Unitary patent protection is achieved by attributing unitary effects to European patents in the post-granting phase by virtue of this regulation and transferring the patent’s main feature to the EU country in which the patent was granted.
EU country authorities must:
ensure that the patent has the same substantive effect throughout the EU;
notify the European patent office of any changes in the patent protection of a patent granted in another EU country;
take all necessary measures to protect the interests of the patent applicant in the event of any change in patent protection in a non-EU country.
In addition, EU countries must take all appropriate measures to ensure the uniform protection of patents granted in their territory.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 31 December 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2020.
BACKGROUND
European Patent Organisation
European patent system
European patents are granted on the basis of a European patent. The European Patent Organisation is the body responsible for granting European patents.
For more information, see:
‘Unitary Patent Protection’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent granting patent protection, i.e. a patent which has the main feature of being patentable in a given EU country. It may be granted in a number of EU countries and may be transferred to other EU countries in the same period of time.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  И                     The regulation applies to EU countries which have signed a special agreement within the meaning of Article 142 of the EPC, a regional patent treaty (Article 45) of the Patent Cooperation Treaty of 19 June 1970 as last modified on 3 February 2001) and the special agreement between Belgium and Bulgaria (Article 19 of the Convention for the Protection of Industrial Property, signed in Paris on 20 March 1883 and last amended on 28 September 1979).
* European patent: any patent which, at the request of the proprietor, benefits from unitary protection by virtue thereof in the participating countries. Such a patent is hereinafter referred to as a ‘European patent with unitARY effect’.

Summary 93:
European Observatory on Infringements of Intellectual Property Rights (EOSI)
European Observatory for Infringement of Intellectual Product Rights (ESIOPR)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 386/2012 on entrusting the Office for Harmonization in the Internal Market (Trade Marks and Designs) with tasks related to the enforcement of intellectual property rights, including the assembling of public and private-sector representatives as a European Observatory on Injunctions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Observatory (ESO) which is the central resource for gathering, monitoring and reporting information and data related to all infringements of copyright, trademarks and design.
It also sets up a European Anti-Counterfeiting and Anti-Piracy Network.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ESO is responsible for:
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on the scope, scale and principal characteristics of counterfeiting and piracy as well as its impact on the internal market;
preparing a comprehensive annual report on counterfeiting, piracy and the impact of these on the EU economy and on the health and safety of EU consumers.
EU Member States must provide the ESO with available reliable and comparable data on counterfeit goods and services. The ESO must also publish each year a comprehensive report covering:
the scope, scope and main characteristics of counterfeit products and services;
the impact of counterfeit goods on the European economy and of the EU’s external trade;
measures to prevent and combat counterfeiting.
Cooperation between Member States and the European Commission
Member States must cooperate with the Commission to establish a network of experts from the public and the private sectors to exchange ideas and expertise on best practices and make recommendations to policymakers for joint enforcement strategies. Member States should also ensure that their national offices and the Commission exchange information and information on infringements.
Organisation of the Observatory
The Observatory is hosted and managed by the Commission. It is composed of representatives from the Member States, the Commission, the private sector and the EU countries.
The Commission:
is responsible for the organisation of the ESOs;
provides the services of the ESAO;
cooperates with Member States on the establishment and operation of the EOSI Network;
coordinates with the EU institutions, the European Parliament, the Council, the Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe on the fight against counterfeiting;
ensures that Member States cooperate with each other on the implementation of the European anti-counterfeiting plan.
In addition, Member States are required to:
ensure that the EoSI Network is regularly updated;
organise regular meetings of representatives of the national authorities and the public sector to exchange experience and best practices;
promote the exchange of information, best practice and best-practice on combating counterfeiting in the EU and in the world;
encourage cooperation between the Commission and Member States in the field of anti-piracy.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 16 May 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Observatory is one of a number of initiatives designed to combat the increasing number of counterfeits and piracy in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:

Summary 94:
Exercise of the EU’s rights under international trade rules
Exemption from international trade obligations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 654/2014 concerning the exercise of the Union‘s rights for the application and enforcement of international trade agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the legal framework for the EU to exercise its rights under the World Trade Organization (WTO) and other international agreements.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following areas:
public procurement;
trade-enhancing measures*;
rebalancing measures*.
It does not apply to:
trade in goods and services between the EU and non-EU countries;
rules on intellectual property rights;
customs and administrative matters.
EU rules
EU countries must apply the rules laid down in the regulation within the scope of the common commercial policy.
They must ensure that:
the measures are based on the best available administrative and commercial practice;
they are proportionate to the economic impact of the measures; and
they do not discriminate between EU countries.
The measures must be applied in accordance with the rules set out in the annex to the regulation.
National authorities must:
ensure that the measures are not disproportionate to the impact on the EU economy;
not discriminate against EU countries’ economic operators;
take all necessary measures to protect the interests of EU citizens;
apply the measures in line with the principles of public procurement and the WTO Agreement on Government Procurement (GPA);
apply them in a transparent and proportionate manner.
In addition, the regulation sets out the procedure for the suspension of concessions or other obligations under any other covered agreement of the WTO.

Summary 95:
Bananas — EU-Central America agreement
Bananas— EU agreement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 20/2013 implementing the bilateral safeguard clause and the stabilisation mechanism for bananas of the Agreement establishing an Association between the European Union and its Member States, on the one hand, and Central America on the other
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for the implementation of certain provisions of the agreement establishing an association between the EU and certain countries of Central America (‘Central America’).
It aims to prevent serious harm to the EU banana growing sector, which is of great importance to the agricultural producers of many of the outermost regions of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to bananas imported into the EU from Central America. It does not apply to bananas originating in the EU or originating from the EU countries.
Safeguard measures
The annexes to the regulation set out the procedure for the application of the ban on imports of bananas from the Central America, as well as for applying the EU’s ‘stabilisation mechanism’ for bananas.
The European Commission is responsible for following up and reviewing the agreement, carrying out investigations and imposing safeguard measures. It must submit an annual report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the implementation and application of its provisions.
In addition, the Commission must monitor the observance by Central America of the social and environmental standards laid down by the International Labour Organisation (ILO) in relation to the protection of human, social, labour and environmental rights in connection with products from that region.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLACTS APPLY?
Decision 2013/8/13/EU — Implementing the EU–Central America Agreement implementing the EU ban on bananas
   
Council Decision 2013/14/EU of 15 January 2013 on the conclusion, by the European Commission, on behalf of the Union, of the European Agreement on the protection and stabilisation of bananas
WHAT DOES THE AGREEMENT DO?
The agreement establishes a safeguard clause for the import of bananas (Bananas) from certain Central America countries. This allows the Commission to impose measures to protect the EU agricultural sector from serious injury caused by imports from the country.
It also establishes a stabilisation system for bananas imported from the countries concerned.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
Central America and the EU (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
*Bananas: the fruit of the banana tree.
*Stabilisation system: a system that allows the EU to apply measures to prevent imports from non-EU countries of bananas, in order to ensure that they do not cause or threaten to cause serious injury to EU producers of like or directly competitive products.

Summary 96:
European account preservation order procedure
European bank account preservation procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 655/2014 establishing a European Account Preservation Order procedure to facilitate cross-border debt recovery in civil and commercial matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Bank Account Preservation Order procedure (EACPO) for the purpose of preventing the transfer or withdrawal of funds held by a debtor in a bank account maintained in a Member State if there is a risk that, without such a measure, the subsequent enforcement of his claim against the debtor will be impeded or made substantially more difficult.
KEY POINTS
The EACPO procedure applies to:
bank accounts held in different Member States;
claims against insolvency proceedings;
commercial matters;
investment projects;
other financial institutions.
Creditors may apply for an EACO to prevent a debtor from transferring or withdrawing funds held in an account in another Member State.
The regulation does not apply to claims against:
the debtor’s own bank accounts;
the accounts of other persons authorised by him to make payments through that account;
those of third parties, such as credit institutions and investment projects.
A creditor may request a European Banking Authority (EBA) to issue a European Banks Protection Order (EBCO) to prevent the transfer, withdrawal or transfer of funds from an account held in one Member State to another. The EBCO may be issued by the EBA for a number of reasons, including:
to prevent the disappearance of assets;
in the event that the debtor fails to appear before a court;
to protect the interests of the debtor;
for the protection of the creditors’ interests.
EACO may also be issued for the purposes of:
protecting the assets of the creditor;
ensuring that the creditor is not liable for any loss or damage caused by the loss or disappearance of funds;
preventing the loss of funds in the event of a bankruptcy;
providing for the recovery of assets.
In addition, EACOs can be issued in cases where the debtor is a resident in a different Member State than the one where the bank account is located.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 27 June 2014.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘European Bank Account Preservation Orders’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Bank account: any bank account in which funds are held by the debtor.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егансканка барка   храка, годанна гарна  глакка (European Banking Authority).
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 97:
Rules for the surveillance of the external sea borders in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the Member States of the European Union (EASA)
Rules for:
EU border surveillance operations at the sea border of other Member States or on the high seas
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 656/2014 — rules for border surveillance at the European sea borders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for EU-wide border surveillance* at the external borders of the EU.
It aims to ensure the efficient monitoring of the crossing of external borders including through border surveillance, while contributing to ensuring the protection and saving of lives.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
border surveillance operations carried out by EU countries at their external sea border;
operational cooperation with non-EU countries, including the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Europol) and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
cooperation between EU countries and EMSA.
EU countries and the EMSA must comply with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Operational cooperation
Cooperation with neighbouring countries is crucial to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to avoid loss of life at sea. To this end, border surveillance is not limited to the detection of attempts at illegal border crossings but equally extends to steps such as intercepting vessels suspected of trying to gain entry to the EU without submitting to border checks, as well as arrangements to address situations such as search and rescue that may arise during a border surveillance operation at sea and arrangements intended to bring such an operation to a successful conclusion.
The European Maritime, Land and Aerial Surveillance System (EMS) is responsible for the coordination of border surveillance activities at the maritime, land and aerial borders of EU countries. The EMSA is also responsible for assisting EU countries in circumstances requiring increased technical assistance at the EU external borders, taking into account the fact that some situations may involve humanitarian emergencies and rescue.
When cooperation with third countries takes place on the territory or the territorial sea of those countries, the parties and the Agency should comply with norms and standards at least equivalent to those set by EU law.
Information exchange
The EMSA and the EU countries must ensure that:
the information exchange of information is based on situational awareness and situational awareness;
the sharing of information improves the information exchange capability between the European border control authorities;
information exchange improves the situational awareness of the information needed to prevent and discourage persons from circumventing the checks at border crossing points.
Cooperating with non–EU countries
The agency may cooperate with the competent authorities of third countries, in particular as regards risk analysis and training, and should facilitate operational cooperation between Member States and third countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 27 June 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 27 June 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  EU border management, asylum and immigration policy is governed by the principle of solidarity and fair sharing of responsibility between the EU Member States pursuant to Article 80 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU). Wherever necessary, EU acts adopted in the framework of those policies are to contain appropriate measures to give effect to that principle and promote burden-sharing including through the transfer, on a voluntary basis, of beneficiaries of international protection.
For more information, see:
‘Border management’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* EU border control: the operation of border controls in the field of immigration and asylum policy.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  The regulation sets out the rules for the border surveillance and maritime surveillance operations conducted by Member States at the border of the other EU countries (e.g. at sea, land or on land) in cooperation with EMSA, the European Customs Union (ECU) and Europol.

Asylum, migration and integration fund and the instrument for financial support for police cooperation, preventing and combating crime, and crisis management
Asylum and migration policy — general provisions on the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund and the EU’s instrument for funding police cooperation and crime prevention and combating.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 — general rules on the asylum, migration, integration and police cooperation fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the general rules for the European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council of Europe (now the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) on EU funding for the area of freedom, security and justice.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU funding to:
the development of a comprehensive EU immigration policy to enhance the competitiveness and social cohesion of the Union;
the creation of a common European asylum system;
preventing and combating serious and organised crime;
fight against illegal immigration, human trafficking, cybercrime and terrorism;
emergency assistance for the management of the external borders of the EU.
It does not apply to actions that are directly oriented towards development and they should complement, when appropriate, the financial assistance provided through external aid instruments.
EU funding
EU countries must submit a national programme for each year to the Commission for the period 2014-2020. The Commission must examine whether the objectives of the programme are consistent with the EU's external action and foreign policy objectives.
The Commission then submits a report to each EU country every 2 years on the results of the dialogue it has had with the relevant EU country.
Each year, the Commission submits to the EU country a list of eligible actions for EU funding. The European Parliament, the European Council and the Committee of the Regions assesses the progress made by the EU countries and the Commission on the implementation of the regulation.
If the Commission considers that an eligible action does not meet the objectives set out in the regulation, it may decide to withdraw the funding. If the Commission decides to continue to fund the action, it must inform the country concerned.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 99:
Asylum, migration and integration fund
Asylum and migration fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 516/2014 establishing the Asylum, Migration and Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the Asylum and Migration Fund.
It aims to contribute to the development of the common EU policy on asylum and immigration and to the strengthening of the area of freedom, security and justice in the light of the principles of solidarity and responsibility-sharing between the EU countries and cooperation with non-EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the general objectives of the Fund, which are to:
promote a uniform and high-quality asylum policy and apply higher standards of international protection;
improve the effective functioning of the Common European System, which encompasses measures relating to policy, legislation and legislation, while acting in accordance with the EU’s common asylum and migration policy;
strengthen cooperation between EU countries in the field of asylum and integration;
facilitate the effective management of migration flows to the EU in areas where the EU adds maximum value, in particular by sharing responsibility and strengthening cooperation with third countries;
support the efforts of EU countries to fully and properly deal with the influx of asylum seekers and displaced persons.
EU countries must ensure that their national programmes include actions addressing the specific objectives of this regulation, and that the allocation of resources between objectives ensures that the objectives can be met.
Funding
The Fund is divided into 2 main objectives:
1.
The general objective:
.
To enhance the effectiveness of the EU's asylum system;
to improve the efforts to fully, properly and effectively deal with asylum applicants and refugees.
to promote the effective sharing of asylum-related responsibilities between EU Member States.
2.
the specific objective: to enhance cooperation between the Member States in the fields of asylum, immigration and integration.
3.
In order to achieve the general objective, the Fund must:
support Member States’ efforts to improve the capacity-building of the European Asylum System;
ensure the effective and fair treatment of refugees and asylum-seekers;
encourage cooperation between Member States and international organisations;
provide assistance to Member States to promote effective cooperation in this field.
Member States must also ensure that the Fund is implemented in the most effective and user-friendly manner possible.
4.
Awards of funding
The EU countries must provide the Fund with financial support for the following actions:
the creation of a European Asylum and Integration Agency (EIA);
the establishment of a network of national authorities responsible for the implementation of the EIA;
the implementation of national programmes under the Fund;
cooperation with the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Europol).
The EIA is composed of the following:
a national authority responsible for implementing the EU asylum policy, which is responsible for managing the asylum system, and
the European Asylum Support Agency, which assists the Commission in implementing the common asylum policy.
5.
Cooperation between the Commission and Member States
The Commission:
provides the Commission with a report on the application of the regulation to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee;
is assisted by the Committee of the Regions.
6.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Asylum: a person who is a national of an EU country and who is in danger of becoming a threat to public policy or public security.
* Migration: the movement of people between the territory of the Member State concerned and its external borders.

Summary 101:
Conservation of fish stocks in relation to countries allowing non-sustainable fishing (Regulation (EU) No 1026/2012 on certain measures for the purpose of the conservation of fishing stocks in the context of the UN Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS) and the UNFSA)
Conservation and management of certain shared, straddling and highly migratory fish stocks (UNFSA).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the conditions under which a country can be considered to be a non-Sustainable Fishing Country (NSFC) and subject to measures under the UNCLOS Convention.
It also defines the type of measures that may be taken with regard to NSCs.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
non-SFC countries that allow fishing activities that jeopardise the sustainability of the stock of common interest (i.e. fish stocks that are not continuously maintained at or above the levels that can produce maximum sustainable yield) or, if these levels cannot be estimated, when the stocks are continuously maintained within safe biological limits.
The Regulation defines the types of measures which may be adopted against a country allowing NSCS fishing. These include:
restrictions on the importation of fish products caught by vessels conducting fisheries on a common interest stock;
restriction of access to ports for those vessels;
preventing EU fishing vessels or EU fishing equipment from being used for fishing the stock under the control of the NSCF;
prohibition of the use of EU fishing gear;
EU fishing vessels and equipment from using the ports of the country allowing the fishing gear of that country.
EU countries may also impose measures against non-SSFCs that fail to cooperate with other countries and the EU in its management of the common interest.
National authorities may impose sanctions on a country that fails to cooperate in the management of a fishery involving a stock common interest to that country and to the EU. These sanctions may include the suspension of fishing rights, duties and interests of other countries or the EU, and the right to be heard and an opportunity to adopt corrective action.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLACE APPLY?
The regulations apply from 14 November 2012.
BACKGROUND
The UN Convention of the International Union for the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish stocks of 4 August 1995 requires the cooperation of all the countries in whose waters the stock occurs (the coastal States) and those countries whose fleets exploit that stock (the fishing States). This cooperation may be established in the framework of regional fisheries management organisations (RFMOs), or, where RFMOs have no competence for the stock in question, by means of ad hoc arrangements among the countries having an interest in the fishery.
For more information, see:
Fishing in the EU (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-sFC: fishing activity that jeopardises the sustainable management of stocks.
* Sustainable fishing: fishing that is carried out without due regard to existing fishing patterns or the rights and duties of other EU countries and EU countries’ interests.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1005/2008 of 29 September 2008 establishing a Community system to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing (OJ L 316, 14.11.2008, pp. 34-39)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 1015/2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 103:
EU generalised tariff preferences for developing countries (GSPs)
EU generalized tariff preferences: developing countries’ rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 978/2012 — applying a scheme of generalised tariffs and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 732/2008
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It applies a general tariff preference scheme to developing countries.
It repeals and repeals Council Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 737/2008 and (EC), which had applied since 1 January 2009.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all developing countries which share a common developing need and are in a similar stage of economic development. It does not apply to countries which have completed their transition into a more developed economy and have not yet diversified their economy.
General arrangement
The general arrangement applies to the following countries:
those countries which are classified by the World Bank as high-income or upper-income countries, i.e. countries which attain higher levels of per capita income levels without the need for a transition to a more diversified economy;
developing countries which do not meet the development needs of the most vulnerable countries and which are not subject to any unjustified trade burdens;
the least-developed countries;
those developing countries subject to a special arrangement.
Special arrangements
The European Commission may adopt a proposal to extend the general arrangement for a period of 10 years from the date of application of the preferences provided for in this regulation, except for the special arrangement for the least developed countries, which will continue to apply without any expiry date. The proposal must comply with World Trade Organisation (WTO) requirements, in particular with the Decision on Differential and More Favourable Treatment, Reciprocity and Fuller Participation of Developing Countries (the ‘Enabling Clause’), adopted under the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) in 1979, under which WTO Members may accord differential and more favourable treatment.
Diversification of the EU’s external trade market
EU countries must diversify their economy through international trade, which can then be reinvested for the benefit of their own development and, in addition, for the diversification of their economies. The general arrangement provides for preferential access to the EU market for goods originating in the EU.
Vulnerable countries
The scheme applies to those countries which meet the following development needs:
the need to diversify its external trade markets;
to reduce poverty and promote good governance and sustainable development; and
to increase the share of EU exports to these countries in their total trade.
The Commission may, under certain conditions, apply a specific type of preferential tariff arrangement to vulnerable countries. This may be:
for goods originating from the EU;
for products originating from non-EU countries; or
for imports originating from EU countries. The Commission may also apply a general arrangement to certain goods originating outside the EU, such as agricultural products.
Exceptions
Certain countries may be excluded from the scheme if they do not fulfil the following conditions:
they are not a member of the World Trade Organization;
they do not have a high level of economic growth or diversification;
their economic needs are not sufficiently diversified to allow them to benefit from the general tariff preferences.
In addition, certain countries may not be eligible for preferential treatment under the general arrangements provided for under this regulation if they are:
not a Member of the WTO;
non-participating in the GATT;
not meeting the conditions laid down in the annex to the regulation;
have not diversified the EU economy sufficiently;
are subject to the conditions set out in the Annex to the Regulation.
If a country does not fulfil these conditions, it may be subject to an unjustified tariff burden. This tariff burden may be reduced or eliminated if the country meets the requirements laid down by the regulation.
Elimination of the special arrangements
A country which does not meet these conditions may not benefit from a special tariff arrangement under the scheme. However, if it meets the criteria set out by the European Commission, the EU may grant a special preferential arrangement to it under the same conditions as the other countries covered by this regulation. In addition, the Commission may grant special preferences to the countries which fulfil the criteria laid down under the regulation, as well as to the non-member countries which comply with the relevant WTO requirements.
Implementation of the regulation
The EU must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of this regulation and to the Committee of the Regions. The report must include a description of the measures taken by the EU to implement the regulation and the measures that have been taken to ensure the continuation of the scheme for the period 2006 to 2015.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
    Еасана  



Summary 104:
United Nations Firearms Protocol — export authorisation and import and transit measures for firearms
United Nations Weapons Protocol — EU export authorisations and import & transit measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 258/2012 — implementing Article 10 of the United Nations’ Protocol against the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms, their parts and components and ammunition
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to facilitate the tracing of firearms and to combat the illicit trafficking of firearms.
It supplements the UN Convention against Transnational Organised Crime (UN Firearms Protocol).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to firearms intended for civilian use. It does not apply to firearms, parts and essential components or ammunition that are intended specifically for military purposes.
EU countries must:
ensure that the authorisation of a firearms, its parts or essential components is issued by the competent authority of the EU country in which the firearms are imported or exported;
implement the export and import authorisation procedures laid down in the annex to the regulation;
apply for authorisation for multiple shipments, transit measures and temporary exports of firearms for lawful purposes;
take appropriate action to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacture or trafficking of such firearms;
provide for the confiscation of items so manufactured or trafficked.
Exchange of information between EU countries
The European Commission is responsible for the exchange of information on firearms and their parts, their essential components and their ammunition.
The Commission is assisted by the European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation (Europol) and Europol.
. It is assisted in this process by Europol, Eurojust and Eurojust.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
From 30 March 2012.

Summary 105:
European Border Surveillance System (Eurosur)
European border surveillance system (EuroSur)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 establishing the European Border Surveillance system (EUROSUR)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union’s (EU), European Union Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders (Eurosur) to improve situational awareness and reaction capability at the external borders of EU countries (‘external borders’) for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the Eurosur Agency (EuroSUR) and the European External Action Service (EEAS).
EurOSUR:
provides the necessary assistance for the development and operation of EUROSUR and the development of CISE, including the interoperability of systems;
cooperates with other EU bodies, offices and agencies, such as the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) and European Union Satellite Centre (EUSC) to make best use of information, capabilities and systems which are already available at European level.
EU countries must establish national coordination centres to improve the exchange of information and the cooperation for border surveillance between them and with the Agency.
The Agency:
is responsible for:
developing, maintaining and coordinating the European border surveillance framework;
providing the necessary financial and human resources;
operating the EuroOSUR system;
the European maritime safety agency (EuroUSC);
provide the necessary technical assistance to the European satellite centre for the surveillance of the maritime domain of the EU.
Rights and obligations of EuroSUR and EESS:
EU country authorities:
have the right to:
exchange information and cooperate with each other;
assist the Agency with the development, operation and maintenance of the European maritime situational awareness framework (EuroOSUR);
ensure that the information contained in the EuroosUR is as complete and updated as possible, in particular with regard to the situation in non-EU countries;
share information with the European external action service (EESS) and with other relevant EU bodies and offices;
participate in the coordination of the EESC and the EUSC;
make use of the information they receive from the European Earth monitoring programme.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the information they provide to the Agency and the EEAS is:
consistent with national law;
relevant to the specific tasks assigned to it under this regulation;
not contradictory to the national law of the country in which it is carried out;
inform the Agency of any changes that may be necessary to ensure the proper functioning of the system.
National coordination centres:
are responsible for coordinating the exchange and coordination of information between themselves and the Agency;
are:
responsible for: monitoring and coordinating border surveillance;
carrying out border surveillance and border control checks;
coordinating border control and border surveillance checks at border crossing points;
exchanging information and training;
conducting border surveillance operations;
organising border control operations and border controls;
transporting migrants and asylum seekers;
collecting, analysing and disseminating information on the migration situation in the EU;
ensuring the protection of migrants and their families;
sharing information on asylum seekers and the removal of persons in need of international protection;
monitoring and analysing the conditions of entry into the EU for the purposes of asylum applications;
checking the conditions under which asylum seekers can be removed from the EU and for their return to their country of origin.
Exceptions to the above-mentioned rights and obligations
The agency and the EU countries:
must respect the fundamental rights and observe the principles for human dignity, the prohibition and prohibition of torture and the prohibition of inhuman, degrading and degrading treatment by means of deprivation of liberty and deprivation of the right of access to information;
respect the rights of the person concerned to information and to access to consular assistance and consular services;
must ensure that persons concerned have access to all consular and other forms of consular aid;
may not be deprived of their right of asylum, consular protection or consular access to the services of a consular or other consular officer;
have access to their personal data and to the information that they have been provided by the Agency or the EASS;
be informed of the measures taken to prevent, detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration, and the measures that are being taken to deal with illegal immigration.
Amendments to the regulation
The European Commission has adopted a number of amendments to this regulation. The most recent amendments concern the following.

An amendment to Regulation (EC) No 2007/2004



Summary 106:
Eurodac: comparison of fingerprints for the effective application of Regulation (EU) No 604/2013 on the European Union’s asylum system (EurodAC)
EuroDac: compatibility of fingerprint data for the purpose of the application of the Dublin Convention (European Asylum System)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2725/2000 — Eurodac — the European Agency for the operational management of large-scale IT systems in the area of freedom, security and justice (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a central system for the comparison of the fingerprint data of applicants for international protection and of persons apprehended in connection with the unlawful crossing of the external borders of the EU.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 407/2002.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the criteria and mechanisms for determining the EU Member State responsible for examining an application for asylum lodged in one of the Member States by a non-EU national or a stateless person and for the exchange of data between Member States’ law enforcement authorities and Europol for law enforcement purposes. It also lays down the rules for the sharing of information between the European Central System and EuroDac.
The European Asylum System (EuroDAC) consists of a central database of fingerprints and of the electronic means of transmission between the EU countries and the Central System, as well as of the communication infrastructure. It is designed to allow each Member State to check whether a third-country national or stateless individual has applied for asylum in another Member State and whether they are eligible for subsidiary protection.
Requests for EuroDAC data are made by the Member State which is responsible for the examination of an asylum application lodged in its territory. Requests are sent to EurodAC via the central system and to Europol. The request must contain the following information:
the name and address of the applicant;
the date and place of birth of the person concerned;
a description of the offence or offence for which the applicant has been arrested or detained;
information on the identity of the individual concerned.
If a Member State considers that the applicant is a national of an EU country other than that of its own, it must inform the European Commission of the reasons for this decision. The Commission then forwards the request to the European Court of Justice (ECJ) for a decision on whether to grant or reject the request. If the Commission decides to grant the request, the Commission must inform Eurodacs’ national authorities of the reason for the decision.
In the event of a refusal, the European Judicial Network (EJN) is notified to the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 26 June 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* EU Member States: EU countries responsible for assessing asylum applications.
* Member States responsible for evaluating asylum applications: the country in which the application has been lodged.

Summary 107:
Quality schemes for agricultural products and foodstuffs (Regulation (EU) No 1151/2012 — quality schemes for agriculture and foodstuff
Quality schemes (Regulations)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the operation of quality schemes* for agricultural and food products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
agricultural products, food and feed;
foodstuffs as traditional specialities;
organic products;
wine-based drinks and aromatized wine-product cocktails.
It does not apply to certain agricultural products, such as honey, honey, olive oil, honeycomb, wine-based products and spirits.
EU countries must apply the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 510/2006 on the protection of geographical indications and designations of origin for agricultural, fisheries and aquaculture products. The regulation also applies to certain specific agricultural products (e.g. wine, honey) and food-related products, in particular those linked to their geographical origin.
Rules on labelling and presentation of agricultural products
Agricultural product quality schemes are governed by the following rules:
the general rules on the definition, description, presentation and description of products and their labelling;
the labelling of organic products, including the description of the labelling, presentation of the products and labelling requirements for the production, organisation and organisation of the European labelling system;
rules on the presentation of products on the European markets and specific rules on specific to agricultural products; and non-organic products, e.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 14 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The quality and diversity of the EU’s agricultural production is one of its important strengths. This is due to the skills and determination of Union farmers and producers who have kept traditions alive while taking into account the developments of new production methods and material.
However, citizens and consumers increasingly demand quality as well as traditional products. Agricultural product quality policy should therefore provide producers with the right tools to better identify and promote those of their products that have specific characteristics while protecting those producers against unfair practices.
For more information, see:
European Union quality schemes (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Organic products: products that are produced in accordance with the European Organic Farming Directive (see summary).
* Aromatized wines: wines that are aromatised with aromatics and which are intended for use in alcoholic drinks.
* Honey: honey derived from beeswax or honey-derived honey.

Summary 108:
Accelerated phasing-in of double-hull or equivalent design requirements for single-Hull oil tankers (recast)
Adapted version of Regulation (EU) No 530/2012 on the accelerated phasing - in-phase phasing in of double hull design standards for oil tanker vessels.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 417/2002, which had to become law in the EU countries by 18 February 2002.
It aims to speed up the phase-out of the single hull design requirements (known as the ‘double hull’ requirements) for all oil tanker ships flying the flag of an EU country.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
single hull oil tanker* vessels flying an EU flag;
oil tankers flying a flag of a non-EU country and used only for government non-commercial services.
EU countries must ensure that:
all new oil tanker designs comply with the regulation’s requirements;
all existing oil tanker design requirements are complied with;
the regulation does not apply to warships, naval auxiliary or other ships owned or operated by a State and used for governmentnon-commercial purposes.
Exemptions
The EU countries may exempt certain types of oil tanker from the regulation, such as:
heavy oil tanker (HGO);
heavy crude oil tanker;
heavy bunker oil (HOB);
oil tanker of less than 500 tonnes;
multi-purpose oil tanker.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
   
It entered into force on 30 June 2012.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * Single-hulk oil tanker: a vessel built for the purpose of transporting crude oil.
* Double-hood: a ship built for use as a floating oil storage facility.

Summary 109:
European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority (EOPA)
European Insurance & Occupational Pension Authority
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1094/2010 establishing a European Supervisory Authority
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European supervisory authority for the insurance and occupational pensions sector.
It aims to improve the quality and consistency of national supervision of the financial sector in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up a European system of financial supervisors, comprising three new European Supervision Authorities (ESAs).
The ESAs are responsible for:
supervision of the banking sector,
the securities sector and
insurance and occupational pension sectors.
The European System of Financial Supervisors (ESFs) is composed of 3 ESAs:
the European Banking Authority (ESA),
the Financial Stability Board (FSB) and
the Insurance and Reinsurance Authority (ERPA).
In addition, the European Systemic Risk Council (ESRCC) is set up. The ESFs and ESRCC have the power to:
make recommendations to the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on the structure of the ESFs;
make proposals to the Commission on how to strengthen the supervisory arrangements of the European banking sector;
take decisions on how the ESF and ESB should be set up;
provide advice to the Council and the EU countries on how they should prepare for and respond to future financial crises.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
*
It has applied since 15 December 2010 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 15 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The financial crisis in 2007 and 2008 exposed important shortcomings in European financial supervision, both in particular cases and in relation to the financial system as a whole. The crisis exposed shortcomings in the areas of cooperation, coordination, consistent application of EU law and trust between national supervisors.
In November 2008, the Commission mandated a High-Level Group chaired by Jacques de Larosière to make recommendations to strengthen European supervision. The report represented the reforms the experts considered were needed and on which work had to begin immediately. It recommended reforms to the structure and structure of supervision in the Union.
For more information, see:
European Financial Supervision (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
European Supervisory Authorities: the bodies responsible for supervising the EU’s financial sector, i.e. the national authorities responsible for the supervision of banks, securities and insurance companies.
ESFs: the entities responsible for ensuring the solvency and soundness of financial institutions in the Member States.
OSPs: the institutions responsible for providing insurance, occupational pensions and reinsurance.
Credit Rating Agencies: the agencies responsible for assessing the creditworthiness of credit rating agencies.

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement for workers within the EU
Freedom of mobility for workers inside the Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 492/2011 on freedom of movement within the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) No 1612/68, which had been substantially amended several times.
It aims to ensure that all workers in the EU have the same priority as regards employment, remuneration and other conditions of work and employment, as well as the right of such workers to move freely within the Union in order to pursue activities as employed persons subject to any limitations justified on grounds of public policy, public security or public health.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to permanent, seasonal and frontier workers and those who pursue their activities for the purpose of providing services.
Eligibility for employment
EU countries must ensure that workers wishing to move to another EU country can apply for employment, irrespective of their nationality, and that they are treated equally in terms of:
the conditions of employment;
the eligibility for housing;
living and working conditions;
compensation; and
the integration of the worker’s family into the host country.
Employment and employment conditions
Employers must inform workers of the conditions under which they can employ them. They must also inform them of any discrimination they may face in relation to their employment, and of the rights and obligations that apply to them as a result of this discrimination.
The employer must inform the worker of the terms and conditions of his or her employment and of any action that may be taken against him or her in accordance with national law.
Housing and living conditions
Workers must be informed of their housing rights and the conditions of their employment. Employers must provide housing for workers who wish to live in another country. They should also inform the workers of their rights to work and to be paid in the host EU country. The employer must also provide the worker with the means to cover the costs of living and working in the country in which he or she wishes to live.
In addition, employers must provide the workers with the necessary information on the conditions applicable to their housing.
Vacancy clearance
If there is a vacancy in one EU country, it must be cleared by the central employment services of the other EU countries.
If a vacancy is not cleared, the employer must notify the authorities of the country where the vacancy is located and inform the employee of the reasons for the absence of the vacancy. The employee must also be informed about the conditions which apply to his/her return to the EU country where he/she is staying.
EU Member States must ensure the following:
that vacancies are cleared within 3 months of the expiry of the contract of employment in question;
that the employer is informed of any measures taken to prevent or restrict the return of a worker to the country of origin;
any measures taken by the employer to prevent, restrict or restrict a worker from returning to the same EU country as a national worker.
National law
National workers must be given the same treatment as national workers in regard to employment as is enjoyed by national workers. The same treatment must be ensured in fact and in law in respect of all matters relating to the actual pursuit of activities as an employed person and to eligibility for accommodation, and also that obstacles to the mobility of workers be eliminated, in particular as regards the conditions for the integration of their family into their host country, and in the case of the family of an employee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 27 May 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
The principle of non-discrimination between workers is one of the objectives of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Equality of treatment between workers’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
                    The European Union (European Parliament and the Council of 5 April 2011 — Freedom of Movement for Workers — codification) (OJ L 141, 27.5.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 111:
EU rules on substances that deplete the ozone layer (Ozone Protocol)
EU rules for substances depleting the ozone surface (OPC)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1005/2009 on substances deplete ozone layer
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that hydrochlorofluorocarbons (OFCs) are no longer used for the maintenance or servicing of refrigeration air conditioning equipment.
It repeals and repeals Regulation 2037/2000, which had been substantially amended several times.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the production and use of OFCs for:
refrigeration and air-conditioning equipment;
the maintenance and servicing of industrial refrigeration equipment; and
the recycling and re-use of recycled or reclaimed material.
The annex to the regulation lists the substances covered by the Montreal Protocol on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer (known as the ‘OPC Protocol’).
It does not apply to the production, use or disposal of:
hydrofluorobutyls and hydrofluoropropane (HFCs);
fluoromethanes;
chlorinated biphenyls;
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs); and
pharmaceuticals.
Exemptions from the regulation
The European Commission has adopted a number of exemptions from the scope of the regulation. These include the following:
:
recycled hydrofluorbide,
s and polyfluorohexylpyrrolidinium (PAPs) for use in the production and distribution of medicines;
piperazine for the manufacture of medical devices;
plastics and polyethylene for the production of pharmaceuticals and biocides;
phosphates and polycyclic fatty acid esters (PFAEs);
polyethylene glycols and phytohexyls (PHAEsAs of 1 January 2020, PAPs and PHAEs are banned in the EU, except for the use of PAAEs and PFAEs in the manufacture, distribution and sale of medicines.
EU countries must ensure that:
pesticides are only used for medical devices and medical devices that are not intended for human consumption;
they are not used to manufacture, transport or dispose of medicines that have a high global warming potential.
In addition, they must:
ensure that the use and disposal of PPAEs is only authorised by the competent authority of the EU country in which they are located;
ban the use, import or export of PAPEs and other PAAE-related substances that are banned under the Protocol;
notify the European Commission and the European Parliament, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of any PAP exemptions granted by the EU countries.
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 amends the regulation to extend the phase-out period of the ozone-depleting substances (ODS) contained in the Protocol to 2025 from 2025 to 2020.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 31 October 2009.
BACKGROUND
The Montreal Protocol is one of the world’s most important international environmental agreements and has been in place since 1980. It aims to protect the stratospheric ozone layer from the harmful effects of the depletion of ozone. It has been amended by the Vienna Convention for the protection of the ozone layer and the Montreal protocol.
For more information, see:
Ozone Protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* OFCS: a substance that depletes the ozone.
* Phytoepropylamide: an organic substance that is a greenhouse gas. It is a substance with a high warming potential and is widely recognised as a major factor in increasing the temperature of the planet.

Summary 112:
European Environment Agency and the European Environment Information and Observation Network
European Union Agency and EEA
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 401/2009 — European Environment Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Union (EU) Environment Agency (EEA) and sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the EEA.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 1210/90.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EEA is the EU’s environmental information and observation network. Its tasks are to provide the EU and the EU countries with objective, reliable and comparable information at European level enabling them to take the requisite measures to protect the environment, to assess the results of such measures and to ensure that the public is properly informed about the state of the environment.
Objectives
The objective of EEA and of the EUEIA is to:
collect, processing and analysis of environmental data at EU level;
cooperate with the EU institutions and EU countries to ensure full application of EU legislation on the environment;
coordinate and coordinate environmental monitoring and analysis;
assist the EU in framing and implementing EU environmental legislation;
provide the EU with the information necessary to identify the environmental needs of EU countries and to carry out effective environmental measures;
collect and analyse environmental data for the purpose of framing EU environmental policies;
prepare and analyse EU-wide environmental policies and programmes;
support the EU Member States in the field of environmental monitoring, analysis and monitoring;
;
contribute to the preparation and implementation of EU environmental protection legislation.
EEA activities
The Agency is governed by the following rules:
its legal autonomy;
the status and structure correspond to the objective character of the results it is intended to produce;
its cooperation with existing national and international facilities;
it is open to other countries which share the concern of the Community and the Member States for the objectives of the Agency under agreements to be concluded between them and the Community.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 23 April 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Union has adopted a number of environmental legislation on environmental protection, including:
Article 174 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (see summary);
Article 251 of the European Parliament and Council of 30 May 2001 on public access to European Parliament, Council and Commission documents;
Article 1049/2001 on the right of access to EU documents.
For more information, see:
European Environmental Agency (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Environmental data: environmental data collected, processed and analysed by the EU Environment Agency.
* EEA: data collected and processed by EU countries in order to identify, analyse and evaluate the needs of European countries in relation to the environment and to implement EU legislation, in particular through the collection, processing, analysis, monitoring and evaluation of environmental protection measures.

Summary 113:
Voluntary participation by organisations in a European eco-management and audit scheme (EMAS)
Voluntary membership by organisations of an environmental organisation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1221/2009 on the voluntary participation by EU organisations in an environmental management and audit system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to encourage EU organisations to participate in the European Union’s (EU) environmental management* and audit* scheme EMAS.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all organisations, in and outside the EU, whose activities have an environmental impact and improve the overall environmental performance of the organisation.
Organisations must register with EMAS to demonstrate an improvement of their environmental performance.
EU countries must encourage organisations to take account of EMAS in their procurement policies and, where appropriate, refer to EMAS or equivalent environmental management systems as contract performance conditions for works and service.
EMAS must be made available to all EU countries’ competent authorities and the public. EU countries must promote the use of the EMAS register to promote the participation of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the scheme.
The European Commission is to review EMAS and propose appropriate amendments to the regulation in the light of the experience gained during its operation.
In addition, the Commission and the EU countries should consider how registration under EMAS may be taken into account in the development of legislation or used as a tool in the enforcement of legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 22 December 2009.
BACKGROUND
European Union environmental management schemes, including EMAS, have demonstrated their effectiveness in promoting improvements of the environmental quality of organisations. However, there is a need to increase the number of organisations participating in EMAS so as to achieve a better overall impact of environmental improvements.
Decision No 1600/2002/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 22 July 2002 laying down the Sixth Community Environment Action Programme identifies the improvement of collaboration and partnership with enterprises as a strategic approach to meeting environmental objectives.
For more information, see:
‘Environmental Management and Audit Scheme’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Environmental management system: a system designed to optimise the environmental impact of a given activity, in particular by reducing the environmental impacts of the activities involved and by improving the quality of the information available to the public about the environmental and social impact of that activity.

Summary 115:
Civil aviation security: common rules in the field of civil aviation security
Civil aviation safety: common rule on civil security
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 300/2008 — common rules on civil aviation safety
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect persons and goods within the European Union (EU) against acts of unlawful interference with civil aircraft that jeopardise the security of civil air transport.
It sets out the basic principles of what has to be done in order to safeguard civil aviation against unlawful interference without going into the technical and procedural details of how they are to be implemented.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to airports serving civil aviation located in the territory of an EU country, to operators providing services at such airports and to entities providing goods and/or services to or through such airports. It does not apply to security measures that apply on board an aircraft, or during a flight, of EU air carriers.
EU countries retain the competence to decide whether to deploy in-flight security officers on aircraft registered in that EU country and on flights of air carriers licensed by it as well as to ensure that such officers are government personnel who are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board the aircraft.
Member States may also require the application of more stringent security measures than those laid down in the regulation. However, without prejudice to the Convention on offences and certain other acts committed on board aircraft, Member States may grant derogations to Member States in the light of the experience gained following the events of 11 September 2001 in the United States.
Monitoring and monitoring of compliance
Each Member State must designate a single authority responsible for the implementation of the regulation and the monitoring of its implementation. This authority is responsible for:
monitoring compliance with the regulation;
coordinating with the other Member States and the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA);
examining the security measures and procedures of third parties involved in aviation security;
exchanging information with the European Commission.
The European Commission may examine the security standards and procedures applicable to non-EU countries in the context of bilateral agreements and bilateral agreements with third parties. It may examine whether to examine the possibility of granting derogations from the requirements laid down by the regulation to third parties in the event of a risk assessment.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 11 March 2008.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules to protect the safety of the EU’s aviation sector, including the regulation on aviation security. These aim to simplify, harmonise and clarify the existing rules and to improve the levels of security.
For more information, see:
Aviation security (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Aviation security: measures to prevent, detect and respond to unlawful interference (i.e. acts of interference) with civil aviation.
* Unlawful interference: acts committed by an individual or group of individuals or groups of individuals, groups or organisations against the safety or security of the safety and security of a civil aviation system.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Ибанскабранка гарка  бакка (Regulation) No 2003/2002 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 December 2002 establishing common rules and common basic standards on aviation safety (OJ L 97, 9.12.2002, pp. 1-2)

Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1725 — European Union targeted measures against specific types of serious human Rights violations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE REGULATION?
The decision establishes a legal framework for targeted sanctions designed to address human rights and international humanitarian crimes. EU countries may freeze the funds or economic resources of, or make available to, natural or legal persons or entities responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in serious human-rights violations or abuses, as well as those associated with them.
In addition, EU countries may prohibit the use of funds and other economic resources to obtain, obtain, hire, hire or obtain economic services, or obtain any other means of obtaining economic resources, or to obtain any kind of economic resources.

a claim for extension or payment of a bond or financial guarantee;
compensation for compensation in respect of a contract or a transaction;
the recognition or enforcement of a judgment, an arbitration award or an equivalent decision, wherever made or given;
other relevant information at their disposal.

the information they have provided to the European Parliament and the Council.
