Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFOR MED IRINI)
EU naval action in Libya
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 on a European Union military operation in Libya (SOPHIA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to launch a military operation to implement the UN arms embargo on Libya by using aerial, satellite and maritime assets.
It aims to prevent the illicit export of petroleum from Libya, to capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy, and to disrupt the business model of human smuggling and trafficking networks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The decision authorises EUNAFOR MED to:
operate in the area of interest of the EU’s Political and Security Committee (PSC), which exercises political control over the operation, provide it with strategic direction and take the relevant decisions in accordance with the third paragraph of Article 38 of the Treaty on European Union;
contribute to the implementation of the arms embargo established by the United Nations Security Council Resolution (UNSCR) 1970 (2011) and subsequent Resolutions, in particular UNSCRs 2292 (2016) and 2473 (2019).
Objectives
The EUNAFORD MED operation is designed to: contribute to the prevention of the illicit transport of petroleum, including crude oil and refined petroleum products, by:
contributing to the training and equipping the Libyan coast guard and navy;
providing technical assistance to the Libyan maritime and coast guard;
military assistance to Libya;
supplying technical assistance for the Libyan naval and maritime forces;
disseminating information to the EU and the EU countries on the situation in Libya.
The PSC may decide in future to extend the scope of the operation in order to allow for the use of aerial surveillance within Libyan airspace, if it assesses that the necessary legal requirements and political conditions are met.
Political and strategic direction
The political and security committee of the PSC is responsible for the political direction and political control of EUNAFDOR MED. It is chaired by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR) and comprises representatives of EU countries. The High Representative is assisted by the European Commission and the European External Action Service (EEAS).
EU funding
EUNAFORCE FOR MED
The operation is funded by the EU budget and the operational expenditure arising from this decision is borne by the Member States. Denmark does not participate in the operation and does not contribute to its financing.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The Decision has applied since 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
The United Nations (UN) Security Council welcomed the Berlin Conference on Libya and endorsed its conclusions, noting that those conclusions represent an important element of a comprehensive solution to the situation on Libya. It also demanded full compliance by all UN Member States with the Arms embargo and further demanded all States not to intervene in the conflict or take measures that exacerbate the conflict.
On 17 February 2020, the Council reached a political agreement to launch an operation aimed at implementing the EU arms embargo and the subsequent UN Security Council Resolutions on Libya, using aerial and satellite surveillance.
For more information, see:
EU NAVFOR MED (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EC) No 596/2020 of 31 March 2020 on a decision to authorise the EU to participate in a new EU military operation, EUNAV FOR MED IRINA, in the context of the political agreement reached in Berlin on Libya (OJ L 101, 1.4.2020, pp. 4-8)
Successive amendments and corrections to Decision No 598/2020 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 118:
False and authentic documents online (FADO) system
False and Authentic documents online system
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/493 on the FADO system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European image archiving system on false and authentic* documents online, known as the European Image Archiving System on False & Authentic Documents Online.
It repeals and repeals Council Joint Action 98/700/JHA.
KEY POINTS
  
The regulation establishes the EU’s electronic storage, rapid exchange and validation of information on authentic and false documents.
The system contains information on all types of authentic travel, identity, residence and civil status documents, driving licences and vehicle licences issued by EU countries, as well as on the laissez-passer issued by the EU and on false versions of such documents in their possession. It should also be possible for the system to contain information on other related official documents, in particular those used in support of applications for official documents issued by non-EU countries and on the false versions thereof.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
the system contains only authentic documents;
the information stored in the system is only used for the purpose of verifying that authentic documents are authentic;
personal data are only stored in a limited number of categories, such as:
falsification images;
identification data;
authentic documents are stored only for the purposes of verifying the authenticity of authentic documents, and
the identity of the person or entity in question;
are stored only in the form of a person has identified in the FadO system;
data are only processed for the specific purpose of identifying the person in question.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that the system does not contain:
identifying the identity of individuals who have been identified as a person in a falsified document;
information on the identity and nationality of the individual who has been identified in a false document.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 30 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Image Archive System on false & authentic* and false* documents is a system set up by the General Secretariat of the Council within the framework of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
For more information, see:
‘FadO’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Falsification: the production and use of false documents and the use of authentic authentic documents obtained by fraudulent means.

Summary 119:
Proportionality test before adoption of new regulation of professions
Proposed regulation of the profession
Proposal for a new professional regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/958 on a proportionality test to be conducted by EU countries before adopting new professional regulations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to establish rules for proportionality assessments before the introduction of new, or the amendment of existing, professional regulations in order to ensure the proper functioning of the EU’s internal market, while guaranteeing transparency and a high level of consumer protection.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the regulated professions falling within the scope of Directive 2005/36/EC. It does not apply to existing regulated professions or new professions that EU countries are considering to regulate, whether they are:
the content of the content of a given regulated profession;
the remuneration of professional organisations; or
the conditions under which professional organisations can organise training or training systems.
It does not affect the freedom of establishment of a regulated profession or the freedom to conduct a business.
EU countries must assess the proportionality of their legislation on all of the professions that are regulated in their territory and communicate the results of that assessment to the European Commission, launching the ‘mutual evaluation process’.
The Commission must assess whether the legislation is justified by public interest objectives;
be suitable for the attainment of the objective which they pursue;
not go beyond what is necessary to attain that objective.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLACE APPLY?

Summary 120:
Multiannual plan for demersal stocks in the North Sea and the fisheries exploiting those stocks
Multi-annual plan: North Sea fisheries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/973 — North Sea multiannual management plan
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the management of the EU’s North Sea (North Sea) fisheries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the North Sea, including:
fisheries exploiting fish stocks in North Sea;
fishing in adjacent North Sea waters;
the fishing of fish caught in the north sea by vessels operating in the waters of Norway, Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein and Norway.
It repeals Regulations (EC) No 676/2007 and (EC)* No 1342/2008.
EU Member States must apply the rules laid down in the regulation.
The European Commission must obtain the best available scientific advice for the stocks falling within the scope of the plan. This advice must be based on publicly available scientific data and methods and that has either been issued or reviewed by an independent scientific body that is recognised at EU or international level. It must indicate, in particular, ranges of FMSY and biomass reference points, i.e. the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) for each species of fish.
National authorities must:
ensure that the plan meets the objectives and quantifiable targets with clear timeframes, conservation reference points and safeguards;
safeguards and technical measures designed to avoid and reduce unwanted catches;
take the necessary measures to avoid, reduce and prevent overfishing and illegal fishing;
apply the measures laid down by the Commission.
Implementing the regulation
The Commission must submit a proposal to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the regulation to the national parliaments every 2 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 4 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Convention of 10 December 1982 on the Law of the Sea, to which the EU is a contracting party, provides for conservation obligations, including maintaining or restoring populations of harvested species at levels which can produce the maximum yield (‘MSY’).
At the United Nations Summit on Sustainable Development held in New York in 2015, the EU and its Member States committed themselves, by 2020, to effectively regulate harvesting, to end over-fishing, illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing and destructive fishing practices and to implement science-based management plans, in order to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce MSY as determined by their biological characteristics.
For more information, see:
Fishing and aquaculture in the European Union (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* North Sea: an area in which the fishing industry has a dominant role in the marine environment and is governed by the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP).
* Fishing: the practice of fishing with fish caught for commercial purposes.
* Limitations: the conditions under which a fishing vessel is allowed to operate in an area.

Summary 121:

Migration, control and management of fisheries in the south Pacific Ocean: management and conservation measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/975 laying down management, conservation* and control* measures applicable to fisheries in certain areas of the south pacific maritime area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the management and management measures that apply to fishing gear and fishing techniques in certain parts of the South pacific marine area of the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure the long-term conservation and sustainable use of fishery resources through the application of the precautionary approach to fisheries management and an ecosystem-based approach.
KEY POINTS
Scope

fishermen’s and operators’ obligations to the Commission.

all vessels are inspected and inspected by the Commission and the Commission at least once a year;
ships are inspected at least twice a year.
the Commission carries out inspections and inspections at least every 3 years.
Inspections and inspections
The Commission is responsible for:
ensuring that fishing gear is used only in a way that minimises the impact of fishing gear on the environment;
monitoring the use of fishing equipment and techniques;
checking that vessels comply with the regulation.
Monitoring and enforcement
Each year, the Commission must carry out inspections of vessels in the area covered by this regulation. The Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 2 years on the inspections carried out and the results of the inspections.
The European Commission must also report every 5 years to the EU Parliament and the Council on the measures it has taken to implement the regulation and the measures that it has adopted.

Summary 122:
Controls on cash entering or leaving the EU
Countries must ensure that cash movements are subject to adequate controls to prevent and detect money laundering and terrorist financing. This regulation repeals Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005.
ACT
Regulation (EU) 2018/1672 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 October 2018 on controls on cash.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down a system of controls applicable to natural persons who enter or leave the EU carrying amounts of cash or bearer-negotiable instruments equal to or greater than EUR 10 000 or its equivalent in other currencies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all persons, legal persons and certain professions who:
enter the EU with cash or cash-related instruments greater than €10 000;
carry out transactions in the EU;
transport cash* between EU countries;
transfer cash* from one EU country to another.
It does not apply to:
cash couriers*;
cash-related transactions* between the EU and non-EU countries.
EU countries must:
ensure that cash couriers comply with the rules laid down in the regulation;
identify and report all cash movements to national financial intelligence units (FIUs);
identifying and reporting any suspicious transactions to the national authorities;
establish procedures for the exchange of information on cash movements.
National authorities must also:
monitor cash movements;
assess the cash movements of individuals, companies and companies;
monitor the movements of cash;
financial institutions and legal persons;
take appropriate action to detect and prevent money laundering, terrorist financing and money laundering-related offences.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLACE APPLY?
From 12 November 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a series of measures to combat money laundering. These include:
Directive 91/308/EEC on financial institutions and certain professionals;
Regulations (EU 2015/849 on money laundering* and (EU 2016/852 on terrorist financing;
EU 2017/853 on cash laundering*; and
EU 2018/854 on cash diversion.
For more information, see:
Anti-money laundering (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Cash couriers: a person who carries out cash movements for the purpose of facilitating criminal or terrorist activities.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 123:
Energy Union and Climate Action — Governance mechanism
Energy Union & Climate Action – Governance Mechanism
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1999 on the governance of the Energy Union and climate action
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the necessary legislative foundation for reliable, inclusive, cost-efficient, transparent and predictable energy governance in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure the achievement of the EU’s 2030 and long-term objectives and targets of the energy union.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy security;
the internal energy market;
energy efficiency; decarbonisation;
research, innovation and competitiveness.
It does not apply to the EU countries’ national laws on energy.
EU countries must:
ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with EU rules on energy efficiency and energy savings;
apply the regulation to their national legislation on energy and climate policy;
make the regulation applicable to all EU citizens, including households and businesses;
take the necessary measures to ensure that it is implemented correctly and transparently in all EU countries.
National legislation
The Regulation applies to the following legislation:
Directive 2009/119/EC on the energy efficiency of energy systems (see summary);
Council Directives 2009/31/EC, 2009/73/EC and 2009/30/EU on the renewable energy of energy sectors;
Council Regulation (EC) No 715/2009 on the climate action of energy sector;
Commission Regulations 94/22/EC (energy efficiency) and 98/70/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council;
Directives 2010/27/EU (energy performance of buildings) and 2013/30 (climate action).
The European Commission must submit a report every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include:
the number of EU citizens covered by the regulation,
the total number of households covered by it and the number of energy efficiency* and energy performance* measures taken by EU countries;
a description of the measures taken to achieve the objectives of the Regulation;
how the regulation has been implemented and how it is being implemented.
The Commission must publish a report on the regulation and its implementation within 5 years of its entry into force. The Commission must also publish a summary of the results of its work in the field of energy governance, including a description of how the regulation was implemented and its impact on consumers and the environment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 21 December 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 21 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Energy security: the system that ensures that consumers have access to reliable, affordable, safe and secure energy, and that energy efficiency is taken into account.
* Decarbonisation: the process of reducing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from energy production and use.

Summary 124:
Persistent organic pollutants (POPs) — EU rules on their production, use and disposal
Persistent Organic pollutants (SOPs)* — EU policies on their manufacture, use, disposal and storage
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1021 on persistent organic pollutants
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the production, placing on the market and use of POPs*.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all POPs listed in the Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants.
It does not apply to substances listed in Annex I of the Protocol to the 1979 Convention on Long-Range Transboundary Air Pollution (LTRAP) or Annex II of the 1979 Protocol on Long Range TransboundARY Air PollUTION on Persistant Organic Pollutes.
EU countries must prohibit the manufacture and placing on market of any POPs, as well as the use of these POPs.
They must also prohibit the import, export or storage of any of the POPs covered by Annex II.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include information on POPs on the EU’s territory and on EU countries’ POPs-related activities.
Exemptions from the regulation
Certain POPs are excluded from the scope of this regulation. These include:
substances covered by Regulation (EC) No 850/2004 (the Basel Convention on the control of transboundary movements of hazardous wastes and their disposal) and
the Minamata Convention on Mercury, which was approved by the EU on 11 May 2017.
However, certain POPs may be exempted from this regulation if they fulfil the following criteria:
they fulfil the essential function of a substance;
they meet the minimum requirements of the European Directive on Chemicals and Waste (Directive 2008/98/EC) (see summary);
they do not pose a risk to human health and the environment.
In addition, the regulation provides for exemptions for certain substances, such as:
perfluorocarbons (PFCs);
pesticides;
methane;
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs); and
tetrachloroethylene.
Implementation
The EU must ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with the provisions of the Rotterdam Convention on hazardous chemicals and pesticides in international trade, which it approved on 19 December 2002. It must also be implemented in the implementation and further development of the Strategic Approach to International Chemicals Management (SAICM) and the sound management of chemicals and waste beyond 2020 within the United Nations framework.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It has applied since 25 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Stockholm Convention was adopted on 14 October 2004.
For more information, see:
European Chemicals Convention (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Persistent organic pollutant: a substance that can persist in the environment and bioaccumulate through the food chain.
* Exemptions: substances covered by the 1979 LTRAP and 1979 Convention.
MAIN DOCUMENT
    барканска (OJ L 169, 25.6.2019, pp. 45-48)

Summary 126:
EU trade mark — uniform protection for goods and services (EU-wide protection)
Union trade mark – uniform protection in all EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1001 on the European Union trade mark
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a uniform EU-wide system for the protection of trade marks.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 40/94, which created a system of trade mark protection specific to the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to trade marks which are governed by a uniform Union law directly applicable in all the EU countries. It does not apply to:
national trade marks;
trade marks obtained only as an EU trade mark in one or more EU countries;
the rights conferred on proprietors of national trade marks by the laws of the EU country in which the trade mark is registered.
EU trade marks may be registered only as EU trade marks if they are:
obliged to be used as a distinctive trade mark or as a trade mark only in the EU;
conformity with the law of an EU country, i.e. the law applicable in that EU country;
use of a distinctive sign or trade mark which is clearly identifiable as belonging to an EU company;
recognition of the goods or services produced by the EU company.
Registration
The European Commission must submit a proposal to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEASC) for the registration of EU trade signs. The proposal must contain the following the following information:
the trade mark’s origin, meaning and purpose;
a description of the products and services it represents;
an indication of the origin, purpose and purpose of the product or service;
information on the rights conferred to the proprietor of a national trade mark.
If the Commission considers that the applicant has not complied with the requirements of the regulation, the applicant may apply for protection at EU level. However, the Commission must inform the applicant of the reasons for this decision. If the applicant does not comply with this decision, the application may be refused.
The applicant must submit an application to the Commission, the EESC and the EASC. The Commission then forwards the application to each EU country. If a country considers that an application is not compatible with its national law, it may refuse to register the application. The applicant must then submit a new application, which must contain all the information necessary for the Commission to determine whether the application is compatible with the national law of the country where the application has been lodged. If it is not, it must be sent to the competent authority of that country.
In the event of a dispute between the Commission and the applicant concerning the validity of the application or the application for protection, the European Court of Auditors may decide to refer the case to the Court of Justice of the EC. The Court will decide whether the case should be brought before the Court or not. The case will be heard by the European Trade Marks Committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    Еганскабанка бакка  Бакакована  
It has applied since 16 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  Жаксанна гакнагканца 
For more information, see:
European Union trade marks (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: a legal instrument which enables the products or services of an organisation to be distinguished by identical means throughout the EU, regardless of frontiers.
* EU trade sign: a trademark which is registered in the territory of the Union and which is applicable to all the countries in which it is applied. It may be used for the purpose of marketing, advertising, trade promotion, trade information, etc.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  IEC Regulation (EEC) 2017 /1001 of the European European Parliament and of the Council of 14 June 2017 laying down the rules on the registration and use of the ‘European Union’ trade mark (codification) (OJ L 154, 16.6.2017, pp. 1-8)

Summary 127:
Accessible format copies of protected works for the benefit of persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled (Regulation (EU) 2017/1563) (See summary).
Accessible formats of protected goods for the purpose of the beneficiary person
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 on the cross-border exchange between the EU and non-EU countries of accessible format copies for non-commercial purposes of certain works and other subject matter protected by copyright and related rights (Marrakesh Treaty) (OJ L 242, 20.9.2017, pp. 1-2)
Regulation(EU) 2018-852 amending Directive (EU), Euratom and the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 September 2017 on the access to the EU’s internal market of accessible formats* of certain protected works and subject matter for the use of the beneficiaries.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVES AND REGULATIONS?
Directives 2001/29/EC and 2001/64/EC provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright for the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain books and other protected subject matter, and for the exchange of those copies between EU countries.
Regulations 2001/30/EC, 2001/31/EC or 2001/32/EC require EU countries to introduce a mandatory exception to certain rights that are harmonised by EU law.
The regulation aims to implement the obligations under the Marrakesh treaty with respect to the export and import arrangements for accessible formats*.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
books, magazines, newspapers, e-books, audio-visual media and other printed material in accordance with the rules contained in Directive 2001/28/EC;
books published in the territory of an EU country that are not parties to the treaty.
Exceptions
EU countries must ensure that:
the authorisation of the authorisation is obtained by the authorising party;
the rights are not infringed;
there are no restrictions on the distribution of the accessible format copy in the EU or the import into the EU of the same book or other protected material.
Authorisation of authorisation
The authorisation must be obtained by:
a person with a visual impairment which cannot be improved so as to give them visual function substantially equivalent to that of a person who has no such impairment;
persons who have a perceptual or reading disability, including dyslexia or any other learning disability preventing them from reading printed works to substantially the same degree as persons without such disability;
people who are unable, due to a physical disability, to hold or manipulate a book or to focus or move the eyes to the extent that would normally be normally acceptable for reading, insofar as, as a result of such impairments or disabilities, those persons are unable to read printed works as substantially as persons who have no such disability.
National authorities must also ensure that authorisation for the distribution and import of the covered works is obtained from the competent authority of the EU country in which the covered work is published. The authorisation may be refused if:
it does not comply with the conditions laid down in the directive;
it is contrary to the public policy of that EU country; or
it would be contrary to public policy or to the interests of the public.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVE AND THE REGULATION APPLY?
The directives have applied since 1 January 2018. The regulation has applied since 20 September 2017.
BACKGROUND
Accessibility to published works
For more information, see:
‘Access to Published Works for Persons Who Are Blind, Visually Impaired, or Otherwise Print Disabled’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Authorisation: the right to authorise a person to make or distribute a copy of a protected work.

Summary 128:
Cooperation between national authorities responsible for the enforcement of consumer protection laws (Regulation (EU) 2017/2394) (European Commission).
Cooperation among national authorities that are responsible for enforcing cross-border consumer protection law
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation 2017/2294 on cooperation between EU countries’ competent authorities* responsible for enforcement of EU consumer protection legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cooperation between the EU’s public enforcement authorities (known as the ‘single liaison offices’) responsible for ensuring that EU consumers’ rights are respected.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are encouraged to designate one of the competent authorities as the single liaison office. This authority should have sufficient powers and necessary resources to undertake that key role. It should also be able to:
cooperate with each other more quickly and more efficiently and to deter traders from committing infringements covered by this regulation;
ensure that necessary information and evidence can be validly exchanged among competent authorities in order to investigate and to order the cessation or prohibition of infringements that have already ceased, but the harmful effects of which may continue.
Each EU country must also decide whether to confer powers on any trader or any other person or body that is designated as a trader or body in relation to an infringement covered by the regulation.
The regulation applies to all persons or bodies that are covered by it. However, it does not apply to trade associations, trade unions, trade associations or other bodies that do not have a formal role in the enforcement system of the EU.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities have the necessary powers to investigate, investigate and, where necessary, impose sanctions on traders who fail to comply with the regulation; and
the relevant authorities are able to cooperate with other competent authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘A Digital Single Market Strategy for Europe’ identified the need to enhance consumer trust through more rapid, agile and consistent enforcement of European Union consumer protection rules. The communication of the European Commission of 28 October 2015, ‘Upgrading the single market strategy: more opportunities for people and business’, emphasised the need for more effective and efficient enforcement.
For more information, see:
‘EU consumer protection’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices — European Union rules on their manufacture, testing and use
In vitro diagnostic medical devices— European Union policies on their manufacturing, testing, use and regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/746 on in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure a high level of safety and health for patients and users in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the rules for the placing on the market and putting into service of in vitro diagnostics* medical devices* and their accessories.
It repeals Directive 98/79/EC and Commission Decision 2010/227/EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to products in the following categories:
products for research and development (R & D);
products intended for medical use;
medical devices, such as medical devices, general laboratory products and products for research only.
The scope of application of the regulation is clearly delimited from other legislation concerning medical devices.
EU Member States must ensure consistent qualification procedures across all products with the exception of products which fall within the scope of the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA), the European Medicines Agency (EMA) or the European Chemicals Agency (ECA).
The European Commission must consult the European Monitoring Centre for Drugs and Drug Addiction (EMCDDA) and the Committee of the Regions before taking a decision on whether a product falls within its scope of regulation.
the Commission must decide on the basis of a request from the European Economic and Social Committee of Regions.
Imporporporters must notify the European Commission and the European Agency for Medicines and Healthcare Products Safety and Innovation (EMHSI) of any product which does not meet the requirements laid down in the regulation. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for this decision within 3 months of receiving the Commission’s proposal.
Manufacturers must ensure that their products comply with the following requirements:
they have a unique device identification number;
they submit a technical documentation to the Commission, which must be accompanied by a certificate of conformity.
They must submit a risk classification and conformity assessment report to the EFSA and the ECSA.
If the Commission considers that a product does not comply with these requirements, it must decide whether the product is subject to a European Commission decision.
In addition, the Commission must take all necessary measures to ensure the safety and efficacy of the product and to ensure that the safety of the subjects participating in performance studies is protected.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 5 May 2017.
BACKGROUND
In the context of the Global Harmonisation Task Force for In vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices (GHSF), which aims to promote the global convergence of regulations which contributes to high levels of safety protection worldwide, and to facilitate trade, the regulation aims to:
ensure the smooth functioning of the internal market;
improve the transparency and traceability of medical devices;
facilitate trade in medical devices in the EU.
For more information, see:
In Vitro Diagnostics (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* In vitro diagnostic device: a medical device consisting of an in vitro analyser (i.e. a device that can be placed into service) and its accessories (e.g. a tube or a device to be inserted into the body).

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash by road between euro-area countries
Cross - border cross-border cross - transport of cash
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1214/2011 on the professional cross-Border transport of Euro cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to make it easier for professional cash handlers to transport euro banknotes and coins by road across the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cash-in-transit (CIT) companies* that transport euro cash between EU countries;
cash handlers* that provide the best price and/or service to the nearest national central bank (NCB) branch or CIT cash centre, even if it is located in another EU country.
It does not apply to the transport of non-euro-denominated cash* by road.
EU countries must ensure that:
the CIT company has a licence to transport cash in the EU;
the driver of the CIT vehicle is a national CIT-licensed person with a minimum of 24 months’ experience of regularly transporting cash in an EU country without infringing national law; and
the vehicle is registered in the country of origin of the cash-handling company.
Exemptions
CIT companies established in EU countries which do not have a specific approval procedure for CIT companies must demonstrate that they have a minimum experience of 24 years’ regularly transporting euro cash in their country of establishment without infringements of national law before they are granted a CIT licence by that country’s national law.
the holder of a licence is not required also to hold a Community licence for the international carriage of goods by road, as laid down in Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 on common rules for access to international road haul (see summary).
Exceptions
The European Central Bank (ECB) is responsible for the management of the IBNS (intelligent banknote neutralisation system), which is designed to neutralise euro notes and coins. The regulation does not harmonise the rules on the use of IBNS.
In addition, the EU countries may decide to allow for the first time the production of euro coins and banknotes in non-EU countries that have authorised it.
The EU countries must also ensure that CIT security staff and the general public are not exposed to risks associated with the activity of transporting cash.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 29 November 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 29 November 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  The introduction of the euro has considerably increased the need to transport the cash needed to be produced abroad. The very principle of a single currency implies the freedom to move cash between participating EU countries. Furthermore, a large number of EU countries whose currency is the euro (hereinafter ‘participating Member States’) have arranged, or may want to arrange for, euro banknote production abroad.
For more information, see:
Euro cash transport (European Central Bank).
* KEY TERMS
* Cross-border cash transport: the professional transport by road of euro notes or coins to and from a central bank or a cash centre in another participating Member State.
* CIT: a company that transports euro cash to, from, or from a bank or cash centre.

Summary 132:
Nutrition information to consumers: Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011 on the provision of food information (Nutrition labelling) (Official Journal L 304 of 22.11.2011).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules on the nutrition information to be provided to consumers by food manufacturers.
It aims to ensure that consumers are appropriately informed as regards the food they consume.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all foods, whether they are prepacked or not. It does not apply to:
prepared foods;
pre-packaged foods not intended for human consumption;
foods intended for sale to the general public;
cookies, crackers and other foodstuffs intended for use in food preparation;
processed foods intended for food preparation.
EU countries must ensure that:
the nutrition information on prepacked foods is clearly visible on the label and on the packaging;
the content and presentation of nutrition information is accompanied by a nutrition-related claim;
there is no obligation on manufacturers to provide the consumer with additional information on the composition of the prepacked food.
There are specific rules on:
prepacked foods containing more than 2% fat and/or sodium;
prepackaged foods containing less than 3% fat or sodium.
Foods prepared for sale in a non-EU country must be labelled with:
a statement of the contents of the food;
a description of the ingredients used;
an indication of the quantity of protein and carbohydrate content of the product;
information on the product’s nutritional value;
instructions on the preparation and storage methods;
advertisements for the preparation, storage and transport of prepacked products.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the labelling, presentation and labelling of pre-packacked foods comply with Regulation (EC) No 178/2002 on the general principles and requirements of food law, establishing the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) and laying down procedures in matters of food safety;
provide consumers with the information they need in order to make informed choices about the foods they buy;
make consumers aware of the risks to their health and well-being posed by the prepackaged food they buy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    дарана барка гарна  года народна
It has applied since 22 November 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 22 November 2013.
BACKGROUND
EU rules on food labelling are laid down in Directive 2000/13/EC on the approximation of the laws of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 March 2000 on labelling.
For more information, see:
Food labelling (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Prepacked foods: any prepacked product that is not intended to be eaten by the consumer.
* Prepackaged food: a product that has been pre-packed and is intended for the general consumption of the consumer, unless it has been labelled as such in accordance with the applicable EU rules.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  егракага заланка  банска: a prepackage of food intended for consumption by the general population.
 
Regulation (EU):  
Nutrition Information to Consumers: the information that must be provided by manufacturers to consumers about the contents and composition of foods.

Summary 133:
EU rules on the issuance of euro coins (Regulation (EU) No 651/2012), including commemorative euro coins, and the use of euro collector coins.
EU rules governing the issue of euro coin
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 on the adoption by the European Central Bank (ECB) of a regulation on the euro’s legal tender — Regulation (EC) No 608/2012 — on the legal tender of euro banknotes and coins
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE DECISION?
The regulation establishes binding rules for the issuing of coins in the euro area.
The decision adopts the regulation on behalf of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
 
Scope
This regulation applies to euro coins intended for circulation, including:
1-euro coins,
2-euro commemorative coins to celebrate specific subjects,
collective euro coins* and
euro collector coins*.
EU countries may issue euro coins to commemorate specific subjects subject to limits set per year and per issuing EU country for the number of issues of such coins. They may not issue coins with a view to their entry into circulation.
Consultation and impact assessment
The European Commission must conduct an impact assessment on the continued issuance of 1- and 2-cent coins. This assessment must take into account:
the cost of production;
the impact on the public acceptability of the coins; and
the number of coins issued per year.
Commission Recommendation 2010/191/EU on the scope and effects of legal tender and on the circulation of euro notes and coins (See summary).
The Commission may also recommend practices regarding:
consultation prior to the destruction of fit euro circulation coins;
use of euro collectory coins; or
the use of coin collector coins, subject to certain conditions.
Issuing of coins
The Regulation sets out the volume of coins to be approved by the ECB for the purpose of issuing euro coins. It does not apply to:
concertificates issued by EU countries to mark the 1-euro coin;
euro coins issued by non-EU countries for the purposes of marking the 2-euro euro coin.
In addition, the regulation sets out certain volume limits for commemorative coin issuances. The volume limits are set per EU country.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
They have applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND

1.euro coin: coins denominated in euro and cent and complying with the denominations and technical specifications laid down by the Council and the Commission.
2. euro coin to mark specific subjects.

European social fund (ESSF) – mission, scope and objectives
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1304/2013 on the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the ESF’s mission and its objectives.
It also defines the type of activities that may be financed.
KEY POINTS
The ESF aims to promote employment, improve access to the labour market and support voluntary labour mobility. It also supports active and healthy ageing, including through innovative forms of work organisation, promoting health and safety at work and increasing employability.
In addition, it promotes active, comprehensive and sustainable inclusion policies in accordance with Article 162 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and thereby contribute to economic, social and territorial cohesion.
EU countries must ensure that:
the ESF contributes to the implementation of the Europe 2020 strategy for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth (the ‘Europe 2020 strategy’);
the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the Cohesion Fund, the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund and lays down, in particular, the thematic objectives, the principles and the rules concerning programming, monitoring and evaluation, management and control.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the implementation and results of the regulation. The report must include:
a description of the activities financed under the regulation;
a summary of the results achieved;
an analysis of how the regulation has been implemented.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
It has applied since 20 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU is confronted with structural challenges arising from economic globalisation, technological change and an increasingly ageing workforce and growing skills and labour shortages in some sectors and regions. They have been compounded by the recent economic and financial crisis, which has resulted in increased levels of unemployment, hitting in particular young people and other disadvantaged people, such as migrants and minorities.
For more information, see:
‘The European social fund’ on European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS

Summary 135:
European demographic statistics (European system of accounts)
European statistical systems (European System of Accounts)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2013 on European demographic statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and dissemination of European demographic data to the European Commission (Eurostat) and the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland. It covers:
the population of the EU countries and the EU regions;
the distribution of the data collected by Eurostat on the basis of the NUTS classification (Nomenclature of Territorial Units (NUTS 3);
regional data on the population of EU countries.
EU countries’ statistical systems
Eurostat’s national statistical systems are based on the European system of Accounts (ESA) classification, which is used for the estimation of total population in the framework of the EEA. The regulation sets out how EU countries collect and transmit the data to Eurostat for the purposes of Eurostat.
National statistics
The EU countries must:
collect and transmit national statistics on the number of people living in their territory;
collect, transmit and disseminate the data for each EU country;
provide Eurostat with a report every 3 years on the progress made towards achieving the objectives of the Sustainable Development Strategy (SDS) and on the demographic situation in the EU;
.
Provide the Commission with an overview of demographic developments and possible future demographic challenges in EU regions.
Each year, Eurostat draws up a report on progress towards achieving SDS objectives.
The Commission publishes the report to the EU Parliament, the Council, the Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every three years. It also publishes a monitoring report every 2 years on progress in achieving the goals of the sustainable development strategy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
From 1 November 2014.
It has applied since 1 November 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 November 2015.
BACKGROUND
The European System of Accountants (ESAA) is the EU‘s statistical system for the calculation of the total population. It is based on a set of sustainable development indicators. The European system is designed to provide an objective statistical picture of progress, based on sustainable growth indicators.
Annual demographic statistics are fundamental for the study and definition of a wide range of policies, with particular regard to social and economic issues, at national and regional level.
For more information, see:
European System Of Accounts (European Commission).

Textile fibres — textile fibres and labels
SUMMARY OF:
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It harmonises EU rules on the labelling* and marking of textile materials and their fibre composition.
It repeals and repeals Directive 73/44/EEC and Directives 96/73/EC and 2008/121/EC on the quantitative analysis of ternary fibre mixtures.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
textile products containing non-textile parts of animal origin.
Labelling
Labels must indicate:
the fibres’ composition;
the name of the economic operator (e.g. the manufacturer, importer or importer of the product);
the labelling or marking of the products’ fibre composition; and
the product’s name.
Manufacturers must indicate the composition of a product on the label, marking or other indications appearing on the product.
The labelling must be clearly visible and clearly legible. It must also indicate the quantity of fibres in the product and the quality of the fibres.
EU countries must ensure that the product is available to the public and that it is manufactured in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Where a product is not manufactured in conformity with the regulation, EU countries’ national legislation, it must be labelled in a language easily understood by the consumer.
In addition, the product must bear the following information on its label or marking:
:
fibre name
the fibre composition
the quantity of fibre
the number of milling and processing units
the quality and quantity of finished products
the content of the finished products.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 18 October 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 October 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of legal acts on textile labelling, marking and other aspects of the production, processing and distribution of textile goods. These have been amended several times.
For more information, see:
Fibre labelling (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Ternary fibres: fibres consisting of a mixture of two or more fibres of the same material.
* Fibre composition: a mixture consisting of one or more strands of a fibre of a material other than that of a textile material. The term ‘ternary’ refers to the mixture of the two strands of the textile material in which the fibre origin is determined.

Summary 139:
Single Resolution Mechanism and the Single Resolution Fund: resolution of credit institutions and certain investment firms (Regulation (EU) No 806/2014)
Single resolution mechanism and the single resolution fund: Regulation (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation [Official Journal L 225 of 30.7.2014]
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes uniform rules and a uniform procedure for the resolution of European banks and investment firms in the framework of a single resolution mechanism (SEMM).
It sets out the rules and procedures to be followed when a bank fails.
KEY POINTS
The SEMM is the European Union’s banking union. It consists of the European Banking Authority (EBA), the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Stability Mechanism (ESM). The EBA and the ESM are responsible for resolving banks that fail. The ESM and the ESCM are funded by the European Investment Bank (ESB).
The resolution of banks is the responsibility of the EBA, the ECB and the ECB. The resolution of failing banks is carried out by the ESI.
The ESI and ESCM have the following tasks:
supervision of banks’ solvency and liquidity;
the management of their assets and liabilities;
coordination of the resolution process;
monitoring and coordination of resolution procedures;
ensuring that the resolution procedure is transparent;
providing information to the public on the resolution and resolution process.
EU countries’ national authorities.
.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Banking Union has been in the process of establishing a banking union since the 2008 financial crisis. The aim of the banking union is to:
ensure that banks and their customers do not face higher borrowing costs because of their place of establishment and irrespective of their real creditworthiness;
improve the functioning of the internal market for banking services;
facilitate the free movement of capital between EU countries.
For more information, see:
European Banking Union (European Commission).

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions at EU airports within a balanced approach (Balanced Approach)
Noise - related operating restrictions in the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 598/2014 on the establishment of rules and procedures with regard to the introduction of noise-related operational restrictions at Union airports within the balanced approach
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules on how EU countries can introduce operating restrictions to reduce the noise impact from aircraft at their airports.
It repeals Directive 2002/30/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all airports in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries may apply the regulation to airports located in their territory if they consider that:
the noise level of an airport is not sufficiently abated to achieve the specific noise abatement objectives of the regulation;
there is a risk of distortion of competition or hampering the overall efficiency of the EU aviation network through the inefficient use of existing capacity;
the regulation does not impose noise quality objectives, which are set out in the regulation’s annexes.
The Regulation applies to:
airport noise assessments carried out by EU countries;
noise assessment procedures carried out in accordance with the rules of the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO);
the selection of the noisiest aircraft (civil aircraft, military aircraft, police aircraft, customs aircraft, search and rescue aircraft, emergency medical aircraft and search-related aircraft;
emergency medical operations;
firefighting operations.
military aircraft and customs flights;
aircraft engaged in regular search-and-rescue operations; and
military flights engaged in search- and rescue operations engaged in various emergency situations.
In addition, the regulation sets out the rights and obligations of the interested parties during the noise assessment process.
Application of the Regulation
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of the regulations. The report must contain information on the number of aircraft subject to the regulation, the number and duration of the operating restrictions and the conditions under which these restrictions may be applied. The Commission must also provide the public with information on how the regulation is being applied.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?

Summary 141:
Insolvency proceedings
Insolvent proceedings
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/848 on insolvencies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve cross-border insolvency procedures.
It lays down rules on jurisdiction, recognition and applicable law in the area of insolvent proceedings.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
bankruptcy proceedings;
the winding-up of companies or other legal persons, judicial arrangements, compositions and analogous proceedings and actions related to insolvent companies;
recognition and enforcement of judgments issued in such proceedings; and
the law applicable to insolvents’ assets.
Application of the regulation
EU countries must apply the rules laid down in Annex A to the regulation. In respect of the national procedures contained in the annexes, the regulation applies without any further examination by the courts of another EU country as long as the conditions listed in the regulation are met. However, if the conditions are not met, the national procedure may be examined by the national courts of the EU country in which the proceedings are taking place.
The European Commission has adopted a report on the application of Regulation (EC) No 1346/2000. The report concluded that the regulation is functioning well in general but that it would be desirable to improve its application in certain areas. It therefore adopted a proposal for a Regulation to be adopted in order to enhance the effective application of certain of its provisions. The proposal was accepted by the European Parliament and the Council.
Amendments to Regulation (EU countries’ national rules on insolventy proceedings
The Commission adopted a regulation amending the regulation in the light of a proposal from the European Economic and Social Committee of the Regions. The regulation repeals and repeals Regulation 1215/2012 (see summary). The regulation was amended several times and further amendments are to be made in the interest of clarity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
  
It has applied since 20 May 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 May 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
The EU has set the objective of establishing an area of freedom, security and justice in civil matters which is governed by EU law. This regulation seeks to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of EU insolvent court proceedings and to avoid incentives for parties to transfer assets or judicial proceedings from one EU country to another, seeking to obtain a more favourable legal position to the detriment of the general body of creditors (forum shopping).
For more information, see:
‘In insolvent company proceedings’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Insolvent company: a company which has been declared insolvent and whose assets have been seized by the authorities of an EU country and which is subject to judicial proceedings in that country. It may be a natural person or a legal person, a trader or an individual. It is not a legal entity.

Summary 142:
Liability of carriers of passengers by sea in the event of accidents (Annex II of the Athens Convention on liability) (OJ L 131, 28.5.2009, pp. 24-28)
Liability for carriers of passenger ships in the case of accidents
Lack of liability for carriers by sea
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 392/2009 on the liability of carriers* of passengers* by sea as regards damage caused to passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on liability for damage caused by a passenger ship to passengers travelling by sea.
It aims to ensure a proper level of compensation for passengers involved in maritime accidents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
passenger ships registered in the European Union (EU) and registered in an EU Member State;
ships registered in a Member State other than the EU;
any vessel registered under the flag of an EU country.
Insurance arrangements
The insurance arrangements required under the regulation must take into account the financial means of the ship-owners and insurance companies. When setting insurance arrangements under this regulation, account should therefore be taken of the different classes of ship and of the seasonal nature of their operations.
The liability of the carrier of passengers is based on:
the carrier’s gross gross tonnage (GGT) and gross cargo weight;
the number of passengers and the number of persons on board.
the gross cargo tonnautical miles (GCT) of the vessel;
number of passengers on board and the gross cargo tonnes (GTCT);
the total number of tonnes of cargo.
In the carrier must make an advance payment to the insurance company in case of death or personal injury to a passenger.
An advance payment does not constitute recognition of liability. The insurance company must make the advance payment if it considers that a passenger is likely to die or be seriously injured. The advance payment must be made at the latest on departure.
If a passenger dies or is seriously injured on board, the carrier is liable for the death or injury. The carrier must also make a claim for the damage caused. Insurance companies must ensure that the insurance policy is affordable for small and medium-sized shipping companies operating national transport services.
Passenger rights
Passengers have the following rights:
an advance payment for the duration of their stay in the Member State in which they are registered;
a right to be informed of the rights conferred on them prior to their journey or, where that is not possible, at the earliest on departure;
an obligation to pay compensation for any damage caused during their stay.
Rights conferred on passengers
The Convention on the Carriage of Passengers and their Luggage by Sea, 1974, as amended by the Protocol of 2002 (the Athens Convention), applies to international transport only. It does not apply to domestic transport.
EU Member States may decide whether to accede to or ratify the 2004 Protocol of the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) relating to compensation for terrorism-related damage. However, they may decide to incorporate certain aspects of the Protocol into their national law.
Amendments to the regulation
Amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 amends the regulation to take account of the changes introduced by the IMO Guidelines on the implementation of the 2004 Athens Convention relating to the liability for damages caused by passenger ships. The amendments include:
a new definition of ‘passenger ship’ in the annexes to Regulation (EC), as well as the definition of the term ‘carrying passengers’;
new definitions of the terms ‘gross cargo weight’ and ‘total cargo’, as defined in Annexes II and III of the regulation;
more detailed definitions of gross cargo weights (GDT) and total cargo tonnes, as set out in the Annexes IV and V of the Regulation;
changes to the definitions of cargo weights and cargo tonnes;
rules on the types of cargo weighing more than 10 tonnes and the weight of the cargo, as laid down in Annex IV of Regulation (Euratom);
new rules on the type and quantity of cargo to be carried by the carrier and the amount of damage to the passenger ship.
Implementing Regulation (C) 2019/853 amends Regulation (e.g. Annexes V and VI of the Euratom) to the Regulation on the insurance arrangements for passenger ships and to the Insurance Rules for the carriage of passengers.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
    Егараканска банка  харка
  Баккальна наксанна  гакшкарна, года гра



Summary 143:
European rail network for competitive freight
European rail system for freight
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 913/2010 concerning a European rail network of international rail freight
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish international rail corridors* for the purpose of improving the conditions of use of the EU’s railway infrastructure.
It harmonises rules relating to those freight corridors.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
international rail freight corridors* which meet specific needs in one or more clearly identified segments of the freight market, i.e. the trans-European Transport Network (TEN-T) and/or the European Railway Traffic Management System (ERTMS) corridors;
the European rail traffic management system (ERTMS)* corridors; and
the interconnections with the rail infrastructure of European non-EU countries.
EU Member States must encourage the establishment of these international freight corridors, as well as projects aimed at encouraging the development of those corridors, within the framework of the Cohesion Fund, research and development programmes and the European Investment Fund for Railways (EIFRD).
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include information on the following:
the number of freight trains carried out in the corridor;
traffic volume and the number of passengers carried out on the corridor.
Annex II to the regulation sets out the rights and obligations of the infrastructure managers.
The Commission must publish the results of the studies carried out under this regulation.
.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has opened up the rail freight market to competition since 1 January 2007. However, market mechanisms have not been and are not sufficient to organise, regulate and secure rail freight traffic. To optimise the use of railway infrastructure and ensure its reliability it is useful to introduce additional procedures to strengthen cooperation on allocation of international train paths for freight trains between infrastructure managers between Member States.
For more information, see:
International rail freight (European Commission).

Summary 144:
Road transport operators — common rules on admission to the occupation of road transport operator
Road transportation operators — Common rules on entry into the occupation OF ROAD TRANSPORT OPERATOR
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1071/2009 establishing common rules concerning the conditions to be complied with to pursue the occupational occupation OF THE OTOBOTER
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to modernise the existing rules on the admission of road haulage operators* and road passenger transport operators* to the EU’s internal market in road transport.
It repeals Council Directive 96/26/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all undertakings wishing to apply for an occupational licence to operate in the European Union (EU) in the transport of goods and certain cabotage operations. It does not apply to undertakings operating in the outermost regions of the EU.
EU Member States may adapt the conditions with which to comply in order to pursue this occupation in these regions. However, the undertakings established in those regions which comply with these conditions may not be granted a licence only as a result of such adaptation.
Requirements for admission
An occupational licence must be obtained from the competent authority of an EU Member State. It must be valid for a minimum of 5 years.
The applicant must have a minimum level of professional qualification and be able to demonstrate that:
they meet the conditions governing admission;
they satisfy the requirements governing access to the European market for international road transport operations (i.e.
they have the necessary documents to prove that they meet the requirements for admission to that market;
the applicant satisfies the conditions laid down in the regulation on common rules for access to international transport markets.
Member States must verify that the applicant satisfies all the requirements laid down by the regulation, including:
the conditions governing the acceptance of the application for admission,
the qualifications and other evidence of formal qualifications;
that the applicant meets the conditions relating to the conditions for admission of the occupation.
If an applicant does not fulfil all the conditions, the Member State may refuse to grant the occupational licence. The Member State must inform the European Commission of the reasons for this refusal. The Commission must then inform the Member States and the European Parliament and the Council of its decision on the application of the regulation.
In addition, Member States must ensure that Member States’ competent authorities verify that a Member State is applying the regulation correctly and that the regulation is being applied uniformly and effectively. Member States are also required to ensure that the Commission publishes a report on the regulation every 5 years on the implementation of this regulation. The report must include information on the number of undertakings that have been granted occupational licences and on the type of traffic they have carried out.
Amendments to the regulation
The Regulation has been amended several times. The most recent amendment was adopted by Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852, which amends the regulation to take account of the fact that certain Member States have not applied the regulation since its entry into force. The regulation also repeals Directive 96(26) EC.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
    Еганскабранка барка  грака (OJ L 300, 14.11.2009, pp. 51-55)
 
It has applied since 14 November 2009.
BACKGROUND
  Бананнакка is the name of the occupational profession in which road transport operators are admitted to the Union.
For more information, see:
Road transport (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Occupational licence: a licence issued by the competent national authority to a transport operator.
* Cabotage: a transport operation in which a vehicle is driven by a driver to transport goods and/or passengers.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  * Operational licence. A licence may be granted to an undertaking which meets the following conditions:
it is valid for 5 years;
it has to be issued by an authority which is competent in accordance with the rules of the Regulation on the acceptance, recognition and application of occupational licences.

Summary 145:
Coordination of social security systems: rules for implementing Regulation (EC) No 987/2009
Coordinating social security rules: rules governing the coordination of Regulation (EU) No 883/2004
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC — Social security coordination rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It modernises the rules on coordination of EU countries’ social security system and simplifies them for all the players involved.
It lays down the rules and procedures for implementing the regulation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who are insured by the social security institutions of an EU country. It does not apply to persons who are not covered by the regulation and who do not have a family member covered by it.
EU countries must establish and manage a centralised database of the national bodies likely to be involved in implementing this regulation. This database must be operational at least from the date of entry into force of this regulation and be updated at least once a year. The Commission must, in particular, take the necessary steps to integrate into that database the information listed in Annex 4 of the regulation in order to facilitate exchanges between EU countries.
The persons covered by this regulation must receive from the competent institution a timely response to their requests. The response must be provided within the time limits prescribed by the national social security legislation of the EU country in question, where such time-limits exist. The persons concerned should also benefit from all the guarantees provided for in the EU provisions on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing and free movement of personal data.
National authorities must ensure that:
information is transmitted electronically;
the information is transmitted in a language easily understood by the insured person;
information on the conditions under which the institution is liable for processing a claim is transmitted to the competent authorities;
data are transmitted electronically in a form which allows them to be updated in real time;
any information transmitted electronically is transmitted by the institution in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
there is no obligation for the institution to provide information on the reasons for the refusal of a request for information.
Information transmitted electronically must be transmitted in the form set out in the annex to the regulation:
the applicant’s name, surname, address, date of birth, nationality, place of residence and place of employment;
a description of the institution responsible for processing the claim;
an explanation of the conditions for the processing of the claim.
If the applicant does not receive a response within the specified time limits, the applicant must be informed of the reason for the decision. The applicant must also be provided with all the information necessary for the proper processing of their claim, including:
a copy of the application,
the identity of the applicant and the identity of their family members;
details of the institutions involved in the process of processing the application.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the applicant is informed of all the rights and obligations that apply to them in the event of a refusal of an application for information;
notify the applicant of any changes to their rights or obligations;
make available to the applicant any information that may be necessary for their application to be processed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
    Еганскабракова бакка  гарка года  
It has applied since 30 October 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 October 2011.
BACKGROUND
The social security coordination regulation is one of a series of measures adopted by the European Commission to modernise and simplify the rules governing coordination of the systems of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
Social security coordination (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Social security: the system of national social insurance systems.
* Member State: the country in which a person is insured.

Statistics on public health and health and safety at work (ECHI) and occupational health indicators (ECHI)
Statistics on Public health and occupational safety and health (ECSI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1338/2008 on Community statistics on public-health and health-safety at work
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical system for the collection of comparable data and information on the European Community’s (ECHIs) health indicators.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries’ statistics on:
public-health indicators;
health and safety indicators*;
occupational health indicators*.
It does not apply to:
the European schedule of occupational diseases (e.g. accidents at work and occupational illnesses);
the indicators of health status, determinants of health and systems;
the indices of health systems.
It applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Ireland, the United Kingdom (1) and the European Economic Area (EEA).
EU countries are required to collect comparable data on the following indicators:
health status;
activity at work;
environmental quality;
quality of life at work.
The European Commission is responsible for collecting and analysing the data for the calculation of the ECHI.
In addition, EU countries are responsible for:
monitoring the use of the indicators and their comparability;
assessing the use and use;
coordinating the indicators’ use with the European statistical system.
Eurostat, the European Statistical System for Health Statistics (Eurostat) and Eurostat's health statistics;
developing the statistical element of the information system on the basis of the Community statistical programme;
cooperating with international organisations;
exchanging information and best practice on the indicators, their use and comparability with other indicators and with Eurostat.
National statistical systems
Each EU country must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 2 years on the implementation of the regulation.
Each year, the Commission publishes a summary of the results of the national statistical systems and the indicators it has collected. The report must be sent to Eurostat, Eurostat and other international organisations. The Commission publishes the summary in the Official Journal of the European Union every 3 years.
An annual report is sent to the Council on the quality of the data collected by EU countries for the ECHIs.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
    Егаранска барка  накакова грашка
  Ибанказанна кракка, Барнальна  хакнагнакса данцка and Бхнанацабкалка (ECI).
It has applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* European Community Health Indicators: indicators that provide an overview of the health status of people in the EU.
* ECHI: a list of European Community health indicators, which provide a description of the factors that determine health status and the determinants that affect health status. They are indicators that are used to assess the impact and effectiveness of the measures taken under the Community action programme in the field of public health (2003-2008).
* Occupational health indicator: an indicator that measures are taken to improve the health of workers in the context of the EU action programme. It is based on surveys of workers’ health at work, and the results are reported in the form of occupational illnesses and accidents.
