Summary 147:
Statistics on job vacancies
Statistics on jobs vacancies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 453/2008 on quarterly statistics on Community job vacancies.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal basis for the production of EU-wide job vacancy statistics.
It aims to improve the understanding of the employment situation and prospects in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries’ national and European statistical authorities and the European Central Bank (ECB).
EU countries are required to collect and publish quarterly data on the number of job vacancies* in the EU.
The statistics cover all sectors of the economy, including:
employment;
economic activity;
employment and labour market conditions;
the level of detail required by economic activity; and
the number of vacancies broken down, inter alia, by economic activities.
National and EU statistical authorities must ensure that the data are relevant and complete, accurate and comprehensive, timely, coherent, comparable, and readily accessible to users. They must also ensure that school career guidance services and vocational training bodies receive the data.
They must:
ensure that all units with fewer than 10 employees are included in the statistics as soon as possible;
make the data available to the social partners at national and EU level;
provide the European Commission with the results of studies and studies on the quality of the data they have collected;
use the statistical classification system (NACE) to determine the scope of the statistics to compile the statistics;
collect the data for economic activity.
Reporting and dissemination.
Each quarter, EU countries must submit to the Commission a report on the production and dissemination of EU job vacancies statistics. The Commission must publish the report within 3 months of the end of the quarter in which the data were collected. This report must contain the following information:
the year-by-year breakdown of the job vacancies in each EU country;
a description of the methodology used to calculate the data;
an indication of the frequency of the changes in job vacancies during the previous quarter;
data on the duration of the quarterly changes in the number and frequency of vacancies;
information on the sources used for the calculation of the statistical data, including the sources and methods used;
any other information necessary for the preparation of the report.
In addition, the Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include:
a summary of the methods used to compile and publish the data,
the results of the studies carried out under the regulation and the methodological principles adopted by the European Statistics Code of Practice (ESP);
the conclusions of the Commission on the independence, integrity and accountability of the national and Community statistical authorities.
Implementation of the Regulation
The Commission must adopt the regulation within 3 years of its entry into force. It may amend the regulation if it considers that it is necessary to do so in order to:
contribute to the development of the European Employment Strategy (see summary);
improve the knowledge of the EU’s labour market and employment situation, in particular through analysis and studies;
develop statistics and common indicators within the framework of the Employment Strategy;
improve information on the level and structure of labour demand.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 4 June 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 4 June 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Quarterly job vacancies: a statistical indicator based on the data collected by EU countries and the ECB.
* Economic activity: economic activity which is defined according to the NACE classification system for economic activities in the Community.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  The Regulation (EU) 2018/852 of the Council of 23 April 2018 on quarterly statistical statistics on Employment and Social Solidarity — Progress (OJ L 145, 4.6.2018, pp. 234-242)
Successive amendments to the Regulation have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

the propulsion system;

Requirements for hydrogen systems and components

In addition, the Commission may adopt specific rules, tests, procedures, procedures and requirements on:
environmental protection;
safety of the systems and subsystems;
transport safety;
energy efficiency;
vehicle design and construction;
hydrogen storage and usage;
reuse of renewable energy resources.
Regulatory framework
The Regulation is a separate regulation for the purposes of the Community type approval system for motor vehicles. It supplements the framework directive on the approval of systems, components, subsystems and related technical units for the hydrogen propulsion.

Summary 149:
Equal market access conditions for natural gas transmission networks: Regulation (EC) No 715/2009 (see summary)
Equity of access to the natural gas market
Equality of access for consumers and businesses in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/73/EC on the internal market in natural gas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for access to gas transmission and distribution networks in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
gas transmission networks*;
third party access services*; and
high-pressure pipelines linking up local distributors to the gas network which are not primarily used in the context of local distribution.
It does not apply to natural gas distribution networks.
Treatment of third parties
Third parties must comply with:
the conditions laid down in Directive 2003/55/EC;
the principle of non-discrimination and the needs of a well-functioning internal market;
transparency and the need to provide appropriate return on investments and incentives to construct new infrastructure, including special regulatory treatment for new investments;
access to the network and the use of auctions to determine tariff levels;
traffic congestion management, transparency, balancing and the trading of capacity rights.
Exemptions from the regulation include:
third parties that are not involved in the transmission or distribution of natural gas;
natural gas pipelines not used for local distribution;
high pressure pipelines that are used only for the transmission of gas from one EU country to another;
non-EU countries’ national distribution networks;
national distribution networks which are used exclusively for the supply of gas to the other EU countries.
the use of auction-to-pipeline services.
EU countries may exclude from the scope of the regulation certain natural gas pipelines, such as those linked to the EU’s external borders, which may not be used for the distribution of gas.
Access to the market
The European Gas Regulatory Forum (EGRF) adopted the first set of guidelines for good practice in 2002. The second set of Guidelines for Good Practice was adopted at the meeting of the Madrid Forum on 24 and 25 September 2003 and the purpose of this regulation is to lay down, on the basis of those guidelines, basic principles and rules regarding:
network access and third party access service;
congestion management and transparency;
capacity management;
bidding and balancing;
trading of gas capacity rights;
exemption from the obligation to provide access services to third parties.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Regulation (EC).

Summary 150:
Negotiating and concluding agreements with non-EU countries on the law applicable to contractual and non-contractual obligations (Regulation (EC) No 662/2009)
Negotiations and conclusion of agreements between EU countries and third countries on specific civil justice issues ( Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 661/2009 establishing a procedure for the negotiation and conclusion agreements between Member States and non–EU countries concerning the law of the EU on particular matters concerning the obligations of contractual and contractual obligations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure to authorise a Member State to amend an existing agreement or to negotiate and conclude a new agreement with a non-European Union country.
It does not apply if the EU has already concluded an agreement with the country concerned on the same subject matter.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following areas of civil law:
contractual and non‑contractual rights and obligations;
judicial cooperation in civil matters;
the recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil and commercial matters.
Authorisation procedure
A Member State must notify the European Commission of its intention to enter into negotiations or to conclude an agreement on a specific civil law issue.
The Commission must submit a proposal to the European Parliament and the Council for the authorisation of the negotiations or the conclusion of an agreement. The proposal must contain the following information:
the nature and scope of the agreement;
a description of the conditions under which the agreement will be concluded;
details of the procedure for concluding the agreement.
If the Commission considers that the proposal is not compatible with the EU acquis, it must inform the Member State concerned. The notification must be accompanied by a copy of the Commission’s report on the implementation of the regulation.
In the event that the Commission decides that the regulation does not conform with the acquis or with the provisions of Article 307 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU), it may decide to terminate it.
Exemption from notification
The Member State may withdraw from the regulation if it considers that it does not have the necessary legal capacity to conclude the agreement or that the agreement does not meet the conditions for its conclusion. The Commission may also withdraw its authorisation if:
a Member State has not indicated that it intends to exercise its external competence to conclude such an agreement by way of an already existing mandate of negotiation or an envisaged agreement; or
the agreement is not consistent with the objectives of the FTA.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Regulation entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
The Lugano Convention confirms that the European Community (now the EU) has exclusive competence in the field of civil justice.
For more information, see:
European Union (European Commission).

Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and international protection
Statistics on immigration and asylum
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 862/2007 on EU statistics on migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical system for the collection and analysis of statistics on the EU’s migration and asylum systems.
It repeals Council Regulation (EEC) no 311/76 on the compilation of statistics of foreign workers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Commission (Eurostat). It does not cover estimates of the number of persons illegally resident in the EU, although they may be included in population stocks due to surveys.
EU countries are required to provide Eurostat with data on:
the number of migrants and asylum seekers in their territory;
the total number of EU citizens who have entered the EU and who have applied for asylum;
total number of asylum seekers who have left the EU;
number of persons who have been granted asylum and who are still seeking asylum.
Data collection
Eurostat must collect, analyse and disseminate the data it receives from EU countries. It must ensure that:
data are collected in a uniform manner, in a timely manner and in a transparent manner;
they are transmitted to Eurostat within 3 months of receipt;
data on the number and type of migrants, asylum seekers and asylum-seekers are collected and disseminated to the Commission within 2 months of receiving the data;
all EU countries submit the data they collect to the European Statistical System (ESSES).
EU data are collected by Eurostat, Eurostat and Eurostat receives the data from the EU countries’ national authorities.
National authorities must:
ensure that the information they provide is accurate, timely and reliable;
provide the Commission with the results of the surveys they have carried out;
use the statistical methods and procedures set out in the regulation;
make sure that the data collected are up-to-date and that they conform with the EU statistical standards.
The European Commission must ensure the quality of the data that Eurostat collects and disseminates. The Commission must also ensure that the results are available to the public and to the media.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
From 31 July 2007.
BACKGROUND
The Thessaloniki European Council of 19 and 20 June 2003 concluded that more effective mechanisms were needed to collect and analyse data on migration in the European Union. In April 2003, the Commission released a communication setting out an action plan to improve the completeness and degree of harmonisation of EU statistics in the field of migration. This included a number of important changes.
For more information, see:
Migration and asylum statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Migration: the movement of people from one EU country to another.
* Asylum seeker: a person who is seeking asylum in another EU country and who is not a national of that country.

   Â  хрансканна банка грака  глаксана   бхакша  (Regulation) (OJ L 199, 31.7.2007, pp. 23-24)

Summary 152:
Specific requirements regarding statutory audit of public-interest entities (Regulation (EU) No 537/2014)
Specific requirements concerning statutory audit OF PUBLIC-OF-INDUSTRY
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 laying down specific requirements on statutory audits of public interest entities and repealing Commission Decision 2005/909/EC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE AND THE REGULATION?
The directive lays down detailed rules on how statutory auditors* and audit firms* are to carry out statutory audits*.
The regulation repeals the decision of 2005/902/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The legislation applies to the statutory audit* of:
financial statements of credit institutions, insurance undertakings, issuers of securities admitted to trading on a regulated market, payment institutions, undertakings for collective investment in transferable securities (UCITS), electronic money institutions and alternative investment funds;
the annual financial statements of investment firms.
It does not apply to the audited internal financial statements* of savings banks, cooperative savings banks or cooperative investment funds.
Requirements for statutory audit firms
EU countries must ensure that:
the statutory audit is carried out by a statutory auditor* or an audit firm* subject to stringent requirements.
The statutory audit must be carried out in accordance with EU law, namely:
Article 1(1) of Council Directive 86/635/EEC;
Article 4(4) of Directive 2004/109/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council; and
Article 15(2) of Directives 2009/65/EC and 2011/61/EU.
Auditors must:
be independent;
be based in an EU country;
have the necessary qualifications and experience;
not have any conflicts of interest;
operate in a non-EU country.
National authorities must ensure:
that statutory audits are carried out under the same conditions as those of other statutory audits;
that the audit firms are subject to the same requirements as those applicable to statutory audits in other EU countries.
They must also:
ensure that the statutory auditor and the audit firm are independent of each other;
carry out their statutory audit in a way that is consistent with the EU’s rules on internal audit;
apply the same procedures and procedures to all statutory audits.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives 2006/43/EC, 2007/64/EC (see summary) and 2009/110/EC have applied since 1 April 2014.
Regulation(EU) 2018 /852 has applied since 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
Audit policy: Lessons from the Crisis (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Public-interest entity: an entity whose annual and consolidated financial statements are audited by one or more persons.
* Investment firm: an investment firm whose annual or consolidated financial statement is audited.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Commission Directive (EU), Euratom, No 652/2018 of 1 December 2018 laying down detailed requirements on the performance of statutory audits (OJ L 158, 27.5.2018, pp. 77-89)
Successive amendments to Directive (Euratom) No 653/2018 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 153:
Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) Regulation (2013-2014)
Common fisheries Policy (2013 - 2014)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the Common Fisheries Policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s rules for the conservation and sustainable exploitation of fisheries resources.
It aims to ensure that fishing and aquaculture activities contribute to long-term environmental, economic, and social sustainability.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fresh water biological resources;
aquaculture;
fishing vessels;
the processing and marketing of fishery products;
recreational fisheries.
EU countries must:
take conservation and management measures designed to maintain or restore marine resources at levels which can produce the maximum sustainable yield both within sea areas under national jurisdiction and on the high seas;
cooperate with other countries to that end;
apply the precautionary approach widely to conservation, management and exploitation of fish stocks;
ensure that fishing vessels are conducted in a manner that is compatible with the objectives of the CFP;
take the necessary measures to ensure the traceability, security and quality of products marketed in the EU.
National authorities must ensure that:
fisheries are carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation;
recreational fisheries are conducted according to the principles set out in the annex to the regulation.
Fishing vessels must comply with the following rules:
the rules on fishing vessels flying the flag of, and registered in, non-EU countries, by EU fishing vessels, or by nationals of EU countries, without prejudice to the primary responsibility of the flag State;
rules on the use of fishing gear and equipment.
Where fishing vessels operate in areas of different jurisdictional status, they must have due regard to other legitimate uses of the seas, and must cooperate with other legitimate fishing vessels.
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of the common fisheries policy. The Commission must also report to these committees on the application of the regulation and the measures it has taken to implement it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
  The CFP was adopted in 2002 and has been in force since 2004.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Common fisheries policy: a system designed to conserve and manage marine biological resources and the management of fisheries targeting them.

Summary 154:
Common organisation of the markets in fishery and aquaculture products (CMO) (summary) Common organisation of markets in fisheries (summary).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1379/2013 on the common organisation of fish markets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the placing on the market of fish products in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishery products, including those produced in the EU and traded in non-EU countries.
It does not apply to:
fishery products originating in the Baltic Sea;
fishing products originating from the Atlantic Ocean;
aquaculture-related products produced outside the EU;
products produced for the production of foodstuffs for human consumption.
Member States must ensure that the regulation is implemented in line with the objectives of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) and with the international commitments of the EU, in particular with regard to those under the provisions of the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
Rules for the producer organisations
The producer organisations are responsible for the implementation of the regulation. They must:
ensure that their members carry out their activities in a sustainable manner;
collect information on fish;
promote market stability and a closer correlation between supply and demand;
coordinate the activities of producer organisations with the European Commission;
make sure that producers are informed, through marketing and educational campaigns, of the value of eating fish and the wide variety of species available, as well as of the importance of understanding the information contained on labels.
Authorisation of fishery producers
The regulations provide for authorisation for fishery producer organisations. The regulation sets out the rules for the authorisation of producers and the conditions for their participation in the management of fisheries.
Producer organisations must also ensure that:
their activities are sustainable;
their members are represented in the decision-making process of the producer organisation;
they have the necessary financial support to enable them to play a more meaningful role in the day-to-day management of the fisheries;
the producer organisation is responsible for:
the allocation of quotas and management of fishing effort;
exchange of information on fishery production;
monitoring and monitoring of the fishery sector;
participation of small-scale producers in the fishing sector; and
the coordination of activities between producer organisations, the Commission and the Member States.
Coordination of activities
The European Commission must ensure:
that producer organisations cooperate with the Commission in order to implement the regulation;
that their activities are coordinated with the other Member States and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC);
that the Commission adopts decisions on the application of the CMO.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the regulation in the light of developments in EU and world markets. These may include:
changes to the definition of the categories of fish species;
changes in the rules on the sharing of resources between the different producer organisations;
adjustments to the rules governing the production and marketing of fish.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
It entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Fishery markets (European Commission).

  Егранска банка   ганца  Зака (Regulation) (OJ L 354, 28.12.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 155:
Securities settlement in the EU
SECURITIES SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 909/2014 on improving securities settlement and on central securities depositories
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the safety, efficiency and competition of the EU’s securities settlement markets.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all securities settlement systems* in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to the activities of the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA), the European Central Bank (ECB) or the European Banking Authority (EBA).
It does not cover the following:
the role of the central counterparties (CSDs) in the securities settlement process;
the risks that CSDs may face when importing the risks encountered by CSDs from other EU countries; and
the risk of insolvency or disruption in the functioning of the CSDs that operate securities settlements systems.
EU countries must ensure that:
all CSDs are subject to the same prudential requirements applicable to all market participants;
all market participants have the same rights and obligations regarding the operation of their securities settlement system;
there are uniform rules applicable to CSDs and CSDs’ liquidity and solvency requirements;
CSDs have to comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the regulation every 5 years.
the Commission must present a report on the regulation to the first 5 years after its entry into force. The Commission must publish the report every 10 years after that.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLACE ENTER INTO FORCE?
The legislation entered into force on 28 August 2014.
BACKGROUND
The securities settlement market is fragmented across the EU and cross-border settlement remains more costly, due to different national rules regulating settlement and the activities. That fragmentation hinders and creates additional risks and costs.
For more information, see:
EU securities settlement rules (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Central securities depository (CSD): a central bank that holds securities and securitised securities.
Central counterparties: banks that provide securities settlement services.

Summary 157:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — Investment for growth and jobs goal and specific provisions concerning the Investment for Growth and Jobs goal (Regulation (EU) No 1301/2013)
European regional development fund (ERFD) — investment for growth, jobs and innovation goal
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the types of activities that can be supported by the ERDF under the investment priorities of the EU’s investment priorities for smart, sustainable and inclusive growth.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1080/2006.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The ERDF is the main instrument of the European Union (EU), the European Social Fund (ESF), the Cohesion Fund, the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF).
It is designed to help to redress the main regional imbalances in the EU. The ERDF contributes to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
Types of activities eligible for ERDF funding
EU countries must designate one or more categories of regions to be eligible for funding under the European territorial cooperation goal. These categories are:
research and innovation, information and communication technologies (ICT), small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and promoting a low-carbon economy;
carbon economy.
In addition, activities (e.g. energy efficiency investments in district heating networks, smart energy distribution, storage and transmission systems and measures aimed at reducing air pollution) are eligible for support, even if one of the indirect effects of such activities is the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions, or if they are listed in the national plan referred to in Directive 2003/87/EC.
Cohesion Fund funding
The EU countries must ensure that:
the ERDF supports research and innovation (R & ICT) and SMEs (Small and Medium-sized Enterprises) activities;
the investment priorities relating to the shift towards a low carbon economy (i.e. promoting the creation of jobs and growth of the SME sector);
investments in the areas of energy efficiency and smart energy transmission systems.
The European Territorial Cooperation goal (ETCo)
The ETFCo goal is a common objective of the ERFD, the ESF and the EMF. It aims to:
contribute to the EU strategy for smart and sustainable growth, thus ensuring greater concentration of ERDF support on the priority of the Union;
increase the contribution of regions with less than 75 % of GDP per capita to the overall ERDF programming;
provide support to regions with a GDP of 75 % or less per capita in line with the specific needs of EU countries’ national strategies;
support the development of islands and islands with a population of less than 2 000 inhabitants, as well as regions with more than 25 % of the GDP of an EU country in terms of their contribution to the total ERDF programme.
Scope of the regulation
The regulation defines the type of activities which can be funded under the ETFO goal. It does not apply to activities that are not listed in Annex I of the EEFO directive, such as:
energy efficiency investments;
energy distribution networks;
smart energy distribution systems;
measures aimed at improving the efficiency of energy markets;
transport infrastructure;
environmental protection measures;
agriculture and rural development.
Exemptions
In order to avoid excessive financing, activities that do not contribute to achieving the EU's investment priorities are excluded from the scope of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 20 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The ErDF was established under Article 176 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Europe (TFEU).
For more information, see:
European Regional Fund (European Commission).

Summary 158:
European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) — specific provisions for the support from the European Regional Development fund to the European territorial cooperation goal
European Regional development fund (ErdF) — special rules for the European Territorial Cooperation goal
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1299/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 17 December 2013 on specific provisions relating to the EU’s territorial development goal
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out specific rules concerning the type of activities which can be supported by the ERDF, the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the scope, geographical coverage, financial resources, thematic concentration and investment priorities, programming, monitoring and evaluation, technical assistance, eligibility, management, control and designation, participation of non-EU countries, and financial management.
European territorial cooperation
The ERDF supports cross-border, transnational and interregional cooperation in order to contribute to the harmonious development of the EU's territory at different levels.
Cross-border cooperation aims to tackle common challenges identified jointly in the border regions, such as: poor accessibility, especially in relation to information and communication technologies (ICT) connectivity and transport infrastructure, declining local industries, an inappropriate business environment, lack of networks among local and regional administrations, low levels of research and innovation and take-up of ICT, environmental pollution, negative attitudes towards neighbouring country citizens and the untapped growth potential in border areas (development of cross-boundary research facilities and clusters, labour market integration, cooperation among education providers, including universities or between health centres), while enhancing the cooperation process for the purpose of the overall harmonious growth of the Union.
Transnational cooperation should aim to strengthen cooperation by means of actions conducive to integrated territorial development linked to the Union's cohesion policy priorities, and also include maritime cooperation not covered by crossborder cooperation programmes, including urban-raging cooperation, as well as research-intensive cooperation between urban regions and urban regions.
Interregional co-operation should enhance the effectiveness of territorial cooperation programmes by encouraging the exchange of experience and knowledge between regions and universities, research institutes and other institutions, and by promoting cooperation between research centres and universities.
Research and innovation
The European Research and Innovation (R&I) Framework (ERIF) aims to encourage research and innovative research and development (R & I) activities in the regions of the Member States and regions of other Member States. It also aims to improve the knowledge-based approach to R & I, and to promote the integration of R&I in the European labour market.
Cooperation between the regions and the universities, universities and other research institutions
The aim of the ERIF is to encourage the exchange and exchange of best practice in the fields of research, innovation and knowledge-intensive research and the creation of research clusters. The ERDF provides financial support for the implementation of these programmes.
Programmes
The programme authorities, programme authorities and authorities in participating Member States, at local, regional or national level, as appropriate, are responsible for:
programming the programmes;
monitoring and evaluation the programme;
assisting the European Commission in implementing the programme.
Financing
The budget for the programme is allocated according to the criteria set out in the regulation. It is divided into 4 categories:
the general ERDF budget;
the regional regional development fund budget; and
the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) budget.
Participation of the Commission
The Commission is responsible for administering the programme for the period 2014-2020. The Commission must submit a report to the national parliaments every 3 years on how the programme has been implemented and the results of the evaluation. The report must include information on the number of beneficiaries, the number and type of projects funded, the total number of projects financed and the total amount of financial resources allocated.
The report must also provide information on:
participation of EU countries in the programme,
the number of countries and regions participating in the project and the number or type of grants received;
how the programme was implemented, and
results of the evaluations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
    данскабранка  барка
It has applied since 20 December 2013 and had to become law in the EU Member States by 20 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
EU territorial development goals
The goal of European territorial development is to reduce disparities between the levels of development of regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island regions. Summary 159:
Guidelines for trans-European energy infrastructure (TEN-E)
Guidelines on trans- European energy infrastructure(TEN - E)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 347/2013 on guidelines for trans–European energy infrastructures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down guidelines for the development of trans–EU energy infrastructure.
It aims to improve the efficiency of the EU’s energy system and to ensure its security of supply.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
trans-European gas and electricity networks;
trans–EU electricity interconnections;
the transmission and distribution of energy between EU countries.
EU Member States must ensure that:
gas networks are interconnected at least to 10% of their installed production capacity;
electricity networks are connected to at least 10 % of the installed generation capacity.
Member States must also:
ensure that gas networks are linked to electricity networks, including through cooperation with non-EU countries;
provide for alternative supply or transit routes and sources of energy;
develop renewable energy sources in competition with traditional sources.
National authorities must:
identify potential future gaps in energy demand and supply;
promote the refurbishment of existing energy infrastructure and the deployment of new energy infrastructure;
encourage the rational production, transportation, distribution and use of energy resources;
accelerate the adoption of common energy efficiency rules;
prepare a common energy infrastructure policy for the period up to 2020 and beyond.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include a description of how the regulation has been implemented and of the progress made to date. The Commission must also present a report on how it is implementing the regulation and how it will be implemented in the light of the developments in the field of energy efficiency and climate change.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 25 April 2013. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 25 April 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Resource efficient Europe’ is part of the European Council's new strategy ‘Europe 2020’. It emphasises the need to modernise and expand Europe's energy infrastructure, in order to make solidarity between Member States operational.
For more information, see:
Energy infrastructure (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trans–EU: an interconnecting network between electricity and gas networks.
* Electricity: a network that links electricity production and distribution in one Member State to electricity transmission in another Member State.
Trans–EU gas: a system that connects gas production and transmission in a single Member State with electricity distribution in another.

Summary 160:
Online dispute resolution for consumer disputes (ODR) (European Commission). Online dispute resolution. European Commission.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 524/2013 on online dispute resolution
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that online consumers and traders have access to a simple, efficient, fast and low-cost alternative dispute resolution* solution to disputes arising from online transactions. This is particularly important when consumers shop cross-border.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all online transactions in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to the sale of goods or to the supply of services.
EU countries must ensure that:
EU consumers can submit online complaints against traders to an online ADR* platform;
EU traders can submit disputes against consumers to an ADR platform; and
EU citizens can submit complaints against a trader to an ODR platform. EU countries must also ensure that EU citizens have the right to participate in the decision-making process of the European Commission when it comes to the application of the regulation.
Disputes can be resolved by:
admitting that a dispute has not been resolved;
agreeing to settle the dispute in writing;
applying the conditions laid down in the regulation;
accepting that the dispute has been resolved in writing.
Adoption of the EU rules
The European Commission adopted the regulation on 21 May 2013. It repeals Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 and Directive 2009/22/EC (Regulation on consumer ODR) with effect from 1 July 2014.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2013.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  �   барнанска гаранка   ‘ODR Regulation’ (ODR Directive 2013/24/EU).
It had to become law in the EU countries by 1 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a high level of consumer protection. However, there is currently a lack of mechanisms which allow consumers to resolve online disputes through electronic means; this leads to consumer detriment, to uneven playing field for traders and thus to fragmentation of the single market.
For more information, see:
‘Alternative dispute resolution’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Alternative dispute resolution (ADR): an online solution to a dispute arising from the sale or supply of goods, services or goods. It is a form of dispute resolution that allows consumers to submit a complaint against a seller or a trader. It can be used to resolve a dispute between a seller and a trader, or between a trader and a consumer.

Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
OJ L 165, 18.6.2013, pp. 1-8

Entry in force
deadline to transpose in other Member States: 1.7.2015

Summary 162:
Agriculture in favour of the smaller Aegean islands
Agricultural support programme for the smaller islands
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 229/2013 — specific measures for agriculture in particular for the small Aegeans
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out specific measures to promote the agricultural production of the small islands of the eastern Mediterranean region of the European Union (EU).
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1405/2006.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all the islands except Crete and Evia, which are covered by the EU’s agricultural support programme (‘support programme’).
The aim of the programme is to ensure the supply of agricultural products and the preservation and development of local agricultural production. It also aims to reduce the cost of transporting agricultural products to the islands and to improve the competitiveness of the sector.
The programme is implemented by means of a support programme, which is established by Greece at the most appropriate geographical level and submitted by Greece to the European Commission for approval. The Commission adopts procedures and indicators to ensure smooth implementation and adequate monitoring. The programme is monitored by the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and the European Monitoring Centre for Rural Development (EMCD).
Programme
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the support programme. It is assisted by the EESC and the national authorities of the EU countries. The EEC and the ESMCD are appointed by the Commission and are responsible for implementing the programme and for monitoring its implementation.
EU countries may propose amendments to the programme to bring it into line with the reality of the situation on those islands.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
The legislation entered into force on 20 March 2013.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation is part of a package of measures designed to promote agricultural production in the southern Mediterranean region.
For more information, see:
European Agricultural Support Programme for the Eastern Mediterranean Region (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Agricultural support programme: an EU-wide programme designed to support the development of the agricultural sector in the south-east Mediterranean region, in particular in the islands of Crete, Epirus, Thessaloniki, Irakli, Lefkada, Kefalonia, Larnaca, Mykonos, Mycenae, Naxos, Piraeus, Presidio, Loutra, Leros, Sarantos, Skopelos, Samos, Kastoria, Rethymno, Sintra, Soutra and Skopetina.
* Support programme: a European Union-wide scheme designed to provide financial support to Greece for the production, processing and marketing of certain agricultural products.

Summary 163:
Statistics of the carriage of goods by road
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 70/2012 on statistical returns in respect of the carriages of goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down the rules and general principles concerning the collection and transmission of statistical data on the transport of goods in the EU by means of vehicles registered in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
carriage of goods and vehicle journeys;
the degree of utilisation of vehicles carrying out this transport.
EU Member States must compile comprehensive regional statistics with regard to both the carriage* and vehicle* journeys. These statistics cover:
the transport vehicles which are authorised by Member States and transport vehicles belonging to public undertakings;
vehicles belonging to undertakings or undertakings with a maximum weight of less than 10 tonnes;
traffic vehicles not normally used for public transport;
transport vehicles not permitted to exceed the maximum weight limit;
public undertakings whose maximum load limit is not higher than 6 tonnes.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to adopt acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU. The Commission, when preparing and drawing-up delegated acts, must ensure a simultaneous, timely and appropriate transmission of relevant documents to the European Parliament and to the Council.
Data collection
Each Member State must compile statistics for the following the request for information made by the European Commission:
in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1172/98 of 25 May 1998;
on the basis of Annex I of the regulation;
in conformity assessment of the sources of administrative data, the cost-effectiveness of available resources and statistical confidentiality.
Member States may exclude from the scope of this regulation:
vehicle loads exceeding the maximum permissible load limit of 10 tonnes, in particular:
goods weighing less than 1 tonne;
non-commercial goods weighing no more than 2.5 tonnes, except for goods weighing more than 1.5 tonne, which may be transported by railway vehicles;
other vehicles of a lower weight than the maximum permitted load limit, except:
military vehicles weighing no less than 2 tonnes, and
public transport vehicles not used for military purposes;
private undertakings not normally involved in public transport, but which are not normally engaged in such undertakings.
In addition, each Member State may exclude certain vehicles from its scope, such as:
cars belonging to the State of origin of the Member State concerned;
categories of goods;
carries of goods belonging to a Member State;
cars registered in another Member State.
Commission implementing powers
In order to carry out the tasks entrusted to it in the context of the common transport policy, the Commission must have at its disposal comparable, reliable, synchronised, regular and comprehensive statistics on the scale and development of the Carriage of Goods by Road, and on the degree of use of vehicles carried out in the Member States. It must ensure that the statistical information is adequately disseminated.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s statistical system is based on a set of rules and principles laid down in the regulation.
For more information, see:
European statistics (European Commission).

Summary 164:
Service of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil or commercial matters (service of documents) in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 1393/2007)
Service of legal and judicial documents in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 1394/2007 — service of documents in EU civil and commercial matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the service of judicial* and other documents* between EU countries.
It aims to improve and expedite the transmission and service of these documents.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
jurisdiction of the transmitting or receiving agency or one agency to perform both functions, for a period of 5 years;
the service of a document on the party’s authorised representative in the country where the proceedings are taking place regardless of the place of residence of that party.
Requests for service
The request for service must be made in the official language of the EU country in which the document is to be served. It must be accompanied by a standard form, to be completed in that language or in another official language accepted by the country in question.
The receiving agency must inform the addressee in writing using the standard form that he may refuse to accept a document to be transmitted in a language other than that of the receiving agency within one month of receipt by the sending agency or within one week of receiving agency.
In exceptional cases, the sending and receiving agencies may refuse service of the document to the addingressee if the following conditions are met:
the document is not understood by the returning agency within a week of receipt;
it has not been served within a month of receiving the document.
the sending agency has not transmitted the document within a year of receiving it;
there is no obligation to transmit the document in another language than the one it has been transmitted in.
EU countries may designate one or more agencies to carry out both functions. This designation may, however, be renewed every five years.
Transmission and service
EU country authorities must ensure that:
judicial and other legal documents are transmitted directly and by rapid means between local bodies designated by the EU countries;
they are transmitted in the standard forms set out in the annexes to the regulation;
transmission is carried out in accordance with the rules laid down in Article 61 of the Treaty establishing the European Community, and in particular Article 61(c) and Article 67(5), second indent, of the Convention on the Service of Documents in Civil and Commercial Matters (Council of Europe Convention of 1997).
The sending agency and the returning agencies must inform each other in writing in writing of any refusal of service to be made of a judicial or extrajudicial document. The sending agency may also refuse service to a document if it does not understand the language in which it is being transmitted or if it is not transmitted in another EU country. The returning agency may not refuse service if it has not communicated the reason for the refusal in writing to the sending or returning agency. The receiving agency may, in exceptional cases:
not transmit a document in the language it understands;
fail to communicate the reason why it is refusing service;
not communicate the reasons for refusal;
refuse to communicate a reason for refusal.
Implementation of the regulation
The European Commission adopted a report on the application of this regulation in October 2004. The report concludes that the regulation has generally improved and expedited the transmission of documents since its entry into force in 2001. However, it notes that certain conditions as to the legibility and reliability of the documents received are still not fully satisfactory.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 10 December 2007.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU has set itself the objective of maintaining and developing the Union as an area of freedom, security and justice, which the free movement of persons is assured. To establish such an area, the Community is to adopt, among others, the measures relating to judicial cooperation in civil matters needed for the proper functioning of the internal market.
For more information, see:
Judicial and other judicial documents (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Judicial documents: documents issued by the courts of an EU country for the purpose of enforcing the law of that country.
* Extrajudicial documents: legal documents issued in the course of judicial or commercial proceedings.

Entry into force - Date of expiry

Summary 165:
Advanced therapy medicinal products — EU rules on their production, distribution and use (Regulation (EC) No 1394/2007)
Advanced therapy medicines — regulation (EU)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules governing the production, import and use of advanced therapy medicines* in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure the free movement of these products within the EU and the effective operation of the EU’s internal market in the biotechnology sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medicinal products intended to be placed on the market in EU countries and either prepared industrially or manufactured by an industrial process, in accordance with the general scope of the European pharmaceutical legislation laid down in Title II of Directive 2001/83/EC on medicinal products for human use.
It does not apply to products which are prepared on a non-routine basis according to specific quality standards, and used within the same EU country in a hospital under the exclusive professional responsibility of a medical practitioner, in order to comply with an individual medical prescription for a custom-made product for an individual patient.
EU countries must ensure that these products comply with the same quality and safety rules applicable to quality and quality standards applicable to all other medicinal products, and that they do not interfere with the fundamental rights of human beings or with the rights of the animal or plant life.
Products may not be used in or administered to human beings with a view to restoring, correcting or modifying physiological functions by exerting principally a pharmacological, immunological or metabolic action.
.
The European Commission must consult the European Committee of the Regions and the Committee on Quality and Safety of Medicines and Healthcare Providers before adopting any decisions on the application of this regulation.
Scope of the regulation
It applies to:
advanced therapy products intended for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body;
somatic cell therapy products;
gene therapy products and somatic cell therapies;
tissue engineered products*;
combinations of medicinal products and medical devices;
all products containing viable cells or tissues.
In addition, it applies to the following products:
products derived from human embryonic stem cells or somatic cells;
products containing viable stem cells and/or tissues derived from non-viable materials which act primarily by physical means, such as products made exclusively of non-Viable materials (e.g. cells, tissues or organs);
products manufactured using a method involving an industrial manufacturing process.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 10 December 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 10 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Advanced therapy medicinal product: a medicinal product intended to treat or prevent diseases in human beings, or that they may be used to restore, correct or modify physiological functions.
* Genetic therapy: a procedure whereby a gene is inserted into a human cell or tissue to repair, restore or modify a defect in the body. It may also be used for the purpose of preventing, treating or modifying the development of diseases or disorders. It is not intended for use in the general population, but only for the use of patients with a specific disease or disorder.
Tissue engineering: a process in which a person is given a specific type of cell, tissue or organ derived from a specific cell or organ.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  The Regulation (EC, No 1393/2007 OF the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 November 2007 on advanced therapy medicaments and amending Directive 2001(83) and Regulation (EU, No 726/2004 (OJ L 324, 10.12.2007, pp. 121-122)
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 166:
Emissions from light passenger and commercial vehicles (Euro 5 and Euro 6) and access to vehicle repair and maintenance information (OASEN format)
Eissions from light passengers and commercial vehicle (Euro5 and Euro6)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 715/2007 on type approval of motor vehicles with respect to emissions from light-passenger and light-duty commercial vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise EU type-approval rules for light passenger vehicles (e.g. vans) with regard to their emission levels.
It sets out the technical requirements for the type approval* of these vehicles.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
light passenger vehicles with a mass of less than 1,000 kg;
light commercial vehicles with at least 1,500 kg of mass;
passenger vehicles with more than 10 seats;
tourist vehicles with up to 10 seats.
EU Member States must apply the technical specifications laid down in the regulation to the European Commission and the Member States’ competent authorities.
Member States must submit a report to the Commission on the implementation of the regulation.
The Commission publishes a report every 2 years on the application for type approval and the results of the inspections carried out. The Commission publishes the report in the Official Journal of the European Union (OJ L 171 of 29.6.2007).
Access to vehicle technical information
The European Commission must establish a standardised format for the transmission of vehicle technical specifications. This format should be used to retrieve the technical information from manufacturers’ websites. It should also facilitate the exchange of technical information between manufacturers and service providers.
Manufacturers must provide the Commission with technical specifications and technical information on the transmission and exhaust emissions of their vehicles. They must also provide information on:
the vehicle’s emission limit values;
the type of engine and the type of exhaust system; and
the number of exhaust emissions from each engine and exhaust system.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report on the compliance of Member States with the regulation, including the number of vehicles registered in each Member State. The report must be sent to the Council and the European Parliament within 3 years of its adoption.
Requests for technical information must be made in a format that can be easily accessed by the public. Member States may require manufacturers to provide technical information in the format set out in this regulation. They may also require the Commission to send technical information to the public in the form of technical specifications or technical reports.
Request for vehicle repair information
Each Member State must send the Commission a request for a technical description of the vehicle repair or maintenance system. This technical description must be provided in accordance with the requirements of Regulation (EU) 2019/852 on vehicle repair services. It must also be provided to the EU Member States and the Commission.
If a Member State considers that the technical description provided by the manufacturer does not comply with the regulations, it must inform the Member State concerned.
Report
The report must contain a report by the end of the 3-year reporting period on the conformity assessment procedure adopted by Member States to the regulation and on the technical specification adopted by the Commission for the Euro 5 and the Euro 6 emission standards.

Summary 167:
Common authorisation procedure for food additives, food enzymes and food flavourings
Common authorised procedure for foods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1331/2008 establishing a common authorisation process for additives, enzymes and flavouring properties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU-wide procedure for the authorisation of food additives*, enzymes and flavourings.
It aims to ensure that these substances are placed on the market and used in foodstuffs for human consumption.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to food additives* and food enzymes*. It does not apply to flavourings*.
Authorisation procedure
The European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) is responsible for authorising food additives and enzymes.
An application for authorisation is submitted to the Commission by the manufacturer or importer of a food additive, food enzyme or food flavouring. The application must contain the following information:
the name and registered trade name of the manufacturer, importer or supplier of the substance;
the number of authorised substances in the EU.
If the Commission considers that the application does not comply with the regulation’s requirements, it must inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) of the reasons for its decision.
The Commission then decides whether the application is valid. If it is not valid, the EU country concerned must notify the EFSA of its decision within 9 months of receiving the application. If the Commission decides that a substance does not meet the conditions of the regulation, it may withdraw it from the EU list. If this is not possible, the European Commission must present a draft regulation updating the list to the EEC.
EU countries must ensure that the substances listed in the regulation are not placed on their market or used in foods.
Evaluation procedure
An EU country must carry out an assessment of a substance before authorising it. This assessment must be carried out by the competent authority of the country concerned. It must include:
a description of the substances concerned;
an assessment of the risks to human health and the environment;
assessment of the products involved;
information on the products concerned.
A decision to authorise a substance is taken on the basis of the assessment and the opinion of the Commission. The Commission may, where necessary, refer the application to the European Medicines Agency (EMA) for further evaluation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Authorisation procedure: the procedure under which a substance may be authorised to use in food.
* Food additives: substances that are added to food to enhance its flavour or to improve its texture, texture or texture.
• Food enzymes: substances which are used to enhance the flavour or texture of food. They are substances that have been added to foods to improve the texture, taste or texture, or to increase the flavour of the food in question.

Requirements for accreditation and market surveillance relating to the marketing of products in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 765/2008)
Requirements for accredited and regulated bodies in the field of health and safety in the European Union (EU).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules and principles concerning the accreditation* of products and the market surveillance* of these products.
KEY POINTS
Accreditation

medical devices;
medicinal products for human and veterinary use;
motor vehicles and aviation.
Market surveillance
It applies to products:
conformity assessment;
market surveillance;
controls of products from non-EU countries;
the CE marking.
It does not affect the substantive rules of existing legislation setting out the provisions to be observed for the purpose of protecting public interests such as health, safety and protection of consumers and of the environment, but should aim at enhancing their operation.

the number of products covered by the regulation;
a description of the conditions under which accreditation may be granted;
how the regulation has been applied.
In addition, the Commission must publish a list of the bodies that have been accredited by the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 13 August 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 August 2009.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Accreditation: an official statement of the technical competence of bodies whose task is to ensure conformity with applicable EU legislation.
* Market surveillance: the system of market surveillance designed to assess and ensure that a product complies with the applicable requirements.

Summary 169:
EU-Iceland agreement on the surrender procedure (2006-2020)
EU-Ireland agreement on:
the surrender procedure
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Union and the Republic of Iceland and the Kingdom of Norway on the extradition procedure between the EU and Iceland and Norway
Decision 2006/852/EC on the signature, on behalf of the EU, and provisional application of the Agreement between the Union and Iceland on the European Arrest Warrant
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve judicial cooperation in criminal matters between EU countries and Iceland.
The decision approves the signature and the provisional application by the EU of the agreement.
KEY POINTS
The EU and the EU countries are parties to a number of international conventions in the field of extradition, including the European Convention on extradition of 13 December 1957 and the Convention on the suppression of terrorism of 27 January 1977.
In accordance with the provisions of this agreement, the surrender for the purpose of prosecution or execution of sentence shall be based on the principles of fundamental rights and in particular the EU Convention on Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms.
A judicial authority of the country where the requested person has been arrested shall have to take the decision on his or her surrender. This decision must be subject to sufficient controls.
An arrest warrant shall not be issued on grounds of nationality, race, religion, sexual orientation, ethnic origin, political opinion or any other objective reason. Nor shall the warrant be issued for reasons of punishing a person who has been convicted of a crime or who is likely to be punished by the death penalty, torture or other inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment.
Iceland and Norway have uniform extradition laws with a common concept of extradition. However, Iceland has expressed their wish to enter into an agreement enabling them to expedite arrangements for handing over suspects and convicts with the EU. The European Union also considers it desirable to have such an agreement in place.
EU countries are obliged to ensure that the extradition system between, on the one hand, the EU country and Iceland, and on the other hand, Norway, is based on minimum standards, taking account of the terms of the Convention of 27 September 1996 relating to extradition.
When an arrest warrant is issued, it must be executed by the competent authorities of the requesting country. The central authorities may provide practical and administrative assistance in the execution of the warrant, but may not be involved in the decision-making process of the requested country.
No person should be surrendered to a country where there is a serious risk that he or she would be subjected to the death sentence, torture, other inhuman and degrading treatment and punishment. The requested country may refuse to execute the warrant on the grounds of the following reasons:
a person has not been informed of the reasons for refusing to surrender;
the person is a national of another EU country; or
the requested person is of a particular ethnic, racial or religious origin.
If a person is refused to surrender on the basis of the above reasons, the request may be referred to the competent authority of that country. In this case, the requesting EU country must inform the other EU countries of the reason for the refusal. If the requested EU country considers that the refusal is justified, it may, under certain conditions, refuse to issue the warrant. In such cases, the country which has issued the warrant must notify the other parties of its reasons for refusal.
Where the request for surrender is refused, the requested party must be informed of these reasons in writing. The request may also be made in the form of a request for a judicial declaration. The requesting party must also be informed, in writing, of the conditions under which the warrant is to be executed. In the case of the refusal of a person to surrender, the person concerned must be provided with all the necessary information, including: the nature of the offence, the nature and duration of the sentence, the circumstances of the arrest warrant, and the circumstances under which it was issued.
Any person who refuses to surrender must be given the opportunity to appeal against the decision of the judicial authority. The person who is refused the right of appeal, in particular, to be heard in a court of law and to be represented by an independent lawyer. In addition, if the person is found to be a danger to public order or to public security, the right to a lawyer must be taken into account when the decision is taken.
This agreement does not prevent a country from applying its constitutional rules relating to due process, the freedom of association, freedom of the press and freedom of expression in other media and freedom to engage in other forms of media. It does, however, prevent the granting of judicial authorisation to an individual who has not complied with the conditions laid down in the agreement to be granted an authorisation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 170:
Rail passengers’ rights and obligations: passenger information and information
Rail passengers' rights and information and reservation systems
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007 on rail passengers' right and obligations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality and effectiveness of rail passenger services in order to help increase the share of rail transport in relation to other modes of transport.
It strengthens the rights of rail passengers.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all passengers travelling by rail in the European Union (EU). It does not apply to:
passengers who are not covered by the contract for international carriage of passengers and luggage by rail (CIV);
passenger transfers from one operator to another (COTIF);
travel information and ticket vending.
Railway undertakings and ticket vendors must:
provide passengers with information on the accessibility of rail services, access conditions of rolling stock and the facilities on board;
ensure that disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility are able to buy tickets on board a train without extra charges.
Train passengers are entitled to compensation in the event of delay or cancellation of a rail service.
EU Member States must ensure that:
railways are insured for the duration of the service;
the passenger is entitled to the same amount of compensation as other passengers in the case of delay and cancellation of the same rail service; and
the railway undertakings are liable for any damage caused by a major accident or major renovation work.
Passenger information
*
Railways must provide passengers with sensory impairment with information regarding:
the accessibility of railway services,
access conditions of the rolling stock,
the conditions of access to the facilities of the train and
access to the ticket vending machines
the number of seats available on board the train.
Member States must also ensure that passengers are provided with:
information on the conditions of accessibility of the rail services;
information about the conditions for the use of the passenger information system and the reservation systems;
accessibility of the ticketing machines and the information provided by the ticket vendor.
Information and reservation system
Member states must ensure:
that passengers are given the opportunity to buy a ticket in advance and as soon as possible;
that the information is provided in a language easily understood by all passengers;
they are given access to a reservation system and to the information they need to make a reservation;
there is an obligation on the railway company to inform passengers of any delays and to inform them of the reasons for these delays.
The railway company must also provide information to passengers on the availability of tickets and on the number of available seats on the train, as well as on the information it must provide to the passengers on board.
In addition, Member States are required to ensure that railway companies:
make available to passengers information on:
traffic conditions and timetables for the carriage of goods and passengers on trains;
make sure that passengers have access to all available seats;
give passengers the possibility to make an advance reservation for a particular train;
use the information and reservations system provided for in the regulation;
take all necessary measures to facilitate the transfer of passengers from one company to another by the provision of through tickets, whenever possible.
Report on the implementation of the regulation
Report from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the application of the Regulation and its subsequent amendments.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    даранска барка  грабанка, гъланна гарна  хракка and глакага цанъка (гналканика) бодан гована,  гшан  бхнаки гонан) and  калнаханоганг часки  
годнар гунки (бнадна)  Бархкакна (карлки).
BACKGROUND
The European Union’s common transport policy is based on the principle of equal treatment of all passengers. This regulation aims to ensure equal treatment for all passengers, irrespective of their disability, age or any other factor.
For more information, see:
‘Rail passenger rights’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 171:
Statistics on education and lifelong learning (ECLIFE)
Statistics on Education and Longevity
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 452/2008 concerning the production and development of statistics on education, training, lifelong learning
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of education and training statistics in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to EU countries’ national statistical systems for the production of EU-wide statistics on:
education and training;
lifelong learning;
employment.
EU countries must collect data on the following:
the number of people in education;
participants in training; and
participation of EU countries in the EU’s integrated information system (Eurostat).
Data collection
Each year, EU countries must submit to the European Commission an annual report on the quality and quantity of data they have collected in the field of education. The report must cover the period from 1 January to 31 December of the previous year. It must include:
data on the number of pupils and learners in each EU country;
the total number of teachers and teachers’ representatives in the education system;
information on the level of education expenditure;
results of training and learning activities;
measures taken by EU countries to improve their education systems.
The Commission must submit a report to the Council every 2 years on the implementation of the regulation. The Commission must publish the results of the report in the Official Journal of the European Statistical System.
Eurostat publishes the report every 3 years. This report must include information on the data collected, including:
total number of learners and teachers;
number of learners;
percentage of learners in education systems;
quality of education systems in EU countries;
degree of education completeness and completeness;
education expenditure in terms of the number and quality of teachers, teachers and trainers;
total education expenditure per pupil.
Data quality
The report must be based on a coherent set of coherent concepts and comparable data in view of the establishment of an integrated European information system on 15 specific areas of education (e.g. education, learning and employment). It must also cover the following areas:
children and adolescents;
vocational education (education, training and vocational education);
employment and employment (education and employment);
the quality of lifelong learning.
In addition, the report must contain:
a description of the methods used to collect the data;
a summary of the measures taken by the EU countries for the collection of the data.
Annex II to the regulation sets out the methods and procedures for collecting the data for the purposes of producing the report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The European Council held in Brussels on 22 and 23 March 2005 agreed to relaunch the Lisbon strategy. It concluded that Europe must renew the basis of its competitiveness, increase its growth potential and its productivity and strengthen social cohesion, placing the main emphasis on knowledge, innovation and the optimisation of human capital. In that respect, the employability, adaptability and mobility of citizens is vital for Europe.
For more information, see:
‘Education and training’ on the Eurostat website.
* KEY TERMS
* LIFETIME: lifelong learning: the process of developing and promoting a skilled, trained and adaptable workforce. It is the process through which people are trained and reintegrated into the labour market. It involves lifelong learning in order to develop and promote a skilled and trained workforce.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Егаранска на барка  халанка, годанна грака (OJ L 145, 4.6.2008, pp. 227-280)

Summary 172:
Energy statistics
Energy statistics.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1099/2008 on energy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) energy statistics system.
It aims to ensure the availability of accurate, up-to-date data on energy quantities, their forms, sources, generation, supply, transformation and consumption, for the purpose of monitoring the impact and consequences of its policy work on energy.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy production and transmission statistics;
energy consumption data;
the energy performance of buildings, renewable energy and nuclear energy;
transmission of energy data to the European Energy Market Observatory (EEMO);
transport data.
EU countries must report to the EEMO the following data:
total energy production and consumption;
total electricity generated from renewable energy sources in the internal electricity market;
electricity generated from nuclear energy in the electricity market.
Transmission of electricity and electricity generation and transmission data
the transmission of electricity generation, transmission and distribution of electricity in the energy market; and
transportation data in the transport sector.
National authorities must ensure that:
the statistics are comparable and comparable;
they are comparable with other EU statistics, such as those of the European Parliament and the Council of Europe, the European Economic Area (EEA) and other EU countries;
there is a comparable methodology for transmission, distribution and distribution statistics; and distribution.
There is also a common methodology for the transmission of energy generation and distribution data and transmission.
In addition of electricity production and electricity transmission data, the regulation requires EU countries to report the following information:
actual electricity generation capacity;
actual transmission capacity
actual energy generation capacity
total power generation capacity.
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to supplement the regulation by:
establishing a European energy market Observatory;
developing a public domain energy forecast model;
ensuring the availability and comparability of EU energy market statistics and energy end-use efficiency and energy services;
introducing a better interface between these data and other statistical surveys such as the population and housing censuses and transportation data. The regulation sets out the rules for the dissemination of these statistics and for the exchange of information between the Commission and the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
